Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
6 min
Establishing Causation (Ta'lil) Before Qiyas
Explaining the need to establish that a text is mu'allal before applying qiyas with examples from riba and khamr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a text is mu'allal and apply the Hanafi rule that only mu'allal texts can be extended by qiyas.

Core Idea
A text's ruling can only be extended by qiyas if its illah is proven from the text itself — ta'lil is a prerequisite.

In Hanafi usul, not every text allows analogy. The Lawgiver has set some texts as self-contained (ghayr mu'allal) and others as causes waiting to be extended (mu'allal). This lesson teaches you to distinguish between them and to prove the presence of an illah from the text before you attempt qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of texts
From the passage, note that texts are divided into mu'allal (the ruling has a known cause) and ghayr mu'allal (cause unknown). Only mu'allal texts can be used as the basis for qiyas.
The passage states: 'النصوص نوعان: معلول وغير معلول'
2
Understand the need to prove ta'lil
The author argues that you cannot simply assume a text is mu'allal; you must establish evidence (dalil) that this particular text contains an illah. This is because the Lawgiver tested us with both stopping at the text and with deduction.
Contrast with following the Prophet, which requires no proof of cause.
3
Apply to the riba example (gold and silver)
The hadith 'hand to hand' includes an implicit illah: avoiding uncertainty and surplus (riba). Because the illah is evident from the text itself, the ruling is extended to other commodities by consensus.
The text says: 'فقد صح التعدي' (extension is valid).
4
Apply to the khamr example (Hanafi vs Shafi'i)
Al-Shafi'i claims the prohibition of khamr is mu'allal with the illah of intoxication. The Hanafi response: the text prohibits khamr by its essence, not by a general cause. There is no textual proof for the illah of intoxication, so extension by qiyas is invalid.
The footnote cites a hadith: 'حرمت الخمر بعينها'.
5
Derive the usul rule
From the two examples, extract the rule: For a text to be extended by qiyas, the illah must be proven from the text itself (dalil must show the text is mu'allal). Without that, qiyas is not valid in the Hanafi school.
This is a key difference from the Shafi'i school, which accepts more inferential illah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاختصاص في بعض الأمور؛ لأن الاقتداء بالنبي عليه الصلاة والسلام إنما صار واجباً لكونه رسولاً وإماماً صادقاً، وهذا لا شبهة فيه، فلم يسقط العمل بما دخل من الاحتمال في نفس العمل. فأما ها هنا فإن النصوص نوعان: معلول وغير معلول، فيصير الاحتمال واقعاً في نفس الحجة، ولأن الشرع ابتلانا بالوقف مرة وبالاستنباط أخرى، كل ذلك أصل، فلما اعتدلا لم يستقم الاكتفاء بأحد الأصلين. فأما الرسول عليه الصلاة والسلام فإنما بعث للاقتداء به، لا معارض لذلك، فلا يبطل بالاحتمال. ومثال هذا الأصل: قولنا في الذهب والفضة: إن حكم النص في ذلك معلول، فلا يُسمع منا الاستدلال بالأصل، وهو التعليل أصل في النصوص، بل لا بد من إقامة الدلالة على أن هذا النص بعينه معلول، ودلالة ذلك أن هذا النص تضمن حكم التعيين بقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: يداً بيد (1)، وذلك من باب الربا أيضاً. ألا يُرى أن تعيين أحد البدلين شرط جواز كل بيع؛ احترازاً عن الدين بالدين، وتعيين البدل الآخر واجب؛ طلباً للتسوية بينهما؛ احترازاً عن شبهة الفضل الذي هو ربا. وقد قال النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام: إنما الربا في النسيئة (2)، وقد وجدنا هذا الحكم متعدياً عنه حتى قال الشافعي رحمه الله في بيع الطعام بالطعام: إن التقابض شرط. وقلنا جميعاً فيمن اشترى حنطة بعينها بشعير بغير عينه حالاً غير مؤجل: إنه باطل وإن كان موصوفاً؛ لما قلنا. ووجب تعيين رأس مال السلم بالإجماع. وإذا ثبت التعدي في ذلك: ثبت أنه معلول، فلا يتعدى بلا تعليل بالإجماع، فقد صح التعدي، ولم تكن الثمنية مانعة، وإذا ثبت فيه: ثبت مسألتنا هذه؛ لأنه هو بعينه، بل ربا الفضل أثبت منه. وقال الشافعي رحمه الله: إن تحريم الخمر معلول، فلا بد من إقامة الدليل عليه ولا دليل عليه من قبل النص، بل الدليل دل على خلافه، فإن النص أوجب تحريم الخمر لعينها، وليست حرمة سائر الأشربة (1). (1) فصل: قوله بقوله يدا بيد: هو في حديث عبادة عند محمد «الأصل». (2) حديث: إنما الربا في النسيئة: متفق عليه من حديث أسامة بن زيد. (1) قوله: النص أوجب تحريم الخمر لعينها يشير إلى ما رواه العقيلي عن علي رضي الله عنه أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: «حرمت الخمر بعينها، والسكر من كل شراب». ولهذا الحديث عند العقيلي طريقان في إحداهما محمد بن الفرات، قال يحيى: ليس بشيء، وقال البخاري: منكر الحديث، وقال العقيلي: لا يتابع على هذا. وفي الأخرى: عبد الرحمن بن بشر الغطفاني، قال العقيلي: مجهول، وحديثه غير محفوظ، وما رواه الدارقطني عن ابن عباس مثله قال الدارقطني: الصواب موقوف. وأخرج النسائي الموقوف عن ابن عباس أنه قال: حرمت الخمر بعينها، والسكر من كل شراب. وفي رواية: وما أسكر من كل شراب رواه الطبراني أيضاً من طرق رجال بعضها رجال الصحيح، ولفظه: حرمت الخمر بعينها القليل منها والكثير، والسكر.
The specificity in some matters; because following the Prophet (peace be upon him) became obligatory due to him being a truthful messenger and imam, and there is no doubt in this, so the action is not invalidated by the possibility that entered into the action itself. But here, the texts are of two types: mu'allal (with a known cause) and ghayr mu'allal (without a known cause), so the possibility occurs in the proof itself. And because the Lawgiver tested us sometimes with stopping (at the text) and other times with deduction, each of these is an origin. So when they are balanced, it is not proper to suffice with one of the two origins. As for the Messenger (peace be upon him), he was sent to be followed, with no opposing factor, so it is not invalidated by possibility. An example of this origin: our statement regarding gold and silver: the ruling of the text in that is mu'allal, so we do not accept from ourselves deduction from the origin, while the causation is an origin in the texts, but rather it is necessary to establish the evidence that this particular text is mu'allal. The evidence for that is that this text includes the ruling of specification by his statement (peace be upon him): 'hand to hand', and that is from the category of riba as well. Do you not see that the specification of one of the two countervalues is a condition for the validity of every sale, as a precaution against debt for debt, and the specification of the other countervalue is obligatory, seeking equality between them, as a precaution against the suspicion of surplus which is riba. And the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Riba is only in delayed payment', and we have found this ruling extended beyond him until al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) said regarding the sale of food for food: that hand-to-hand transfer is a condition. And we all said regarding one who buys specific wheat with unspecified barley immediately without delay: it is invalid even if described, for what we said. And the specification of the capital of salam is obligatory by consensus. And when the extension is established in that, it is established that it is mu'allal, so it does not extend without causation by consensus. So the extension is valid, and the quality of being a price was not a preventing factor. And when it is established in that, our case is established, because it is the same, rather riba al-fadl is stronger than it. And al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The prohibition of khamr is mu'allal, so it is necessary to establish evidence for it, but there is no evidence for it from the text, rather the evidence indicates the opposite. Because the text made the prohibition of khamr specific to its essence, and not the prohibition of other beverages. (Footnote 1: The text 'the prohibition of khamr by its essence' refers to what al-Uqayli narrated from Ali ... etc.)
Source pages p. 568

Commentary
This passage is a rule in usul al-fiqh about the need to prove that a text is mu'allal (contains an underlying cause) before extending its ruling via qiyas. The author begins by contrasting following the Prophet (obligatory without question) with deriving rulings from texts (where doubt can arise). He states that texts are of two types: mu'allal (ruling has a known illah) and ghayr mu'allal (illah unknown). Only mu'allal texts can be extended by analogy. The example of gold and silver is given: the hadith 'hand to hand' requires immediate exchange, and scholars agree that this ruling is mu'allal because the illah (avoiding riba) is evident from the text itself. Since the illah is proven, the ruling can be extended to other commodities. The author then refutes al-Shafi'i's claim that the prohibition of khamr is mu'allal. He argues that the text itself specifies khamr by name, and there is no explicit textual evidence that the prohibition is based on a general cause like intoxication. The footnote provides hadith support that khamr is prohibited by its essence, not by a general cause. Thus, according to the Hanafi view, without a proven illah from the text, one cannot extend the prohibition of khamr to other intoxicants by qiyas.

Key Terms
معلول (mu'allal): A text whose ruling has a known underlying cause (illah) that allows it to be extended via qiyas.
تعليل (ta'lil): The process of identifying and proving the illah in a text to allow extension via qiyas.
غير معلول (ghayr mu'allal): A text whose ruling does not have a known cause and thus cannot be extended by qiyas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What must be established before extending a text's ruling by qiyas according to the Hanafi usul presented in this passage?
A) That the text is from the Quran
B) That the text is mu'allal (has a known illah)
C) That the text is from the Sunnah
D) That the text is general ('amm)

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, qiyas can only be applied to a text if the text is proven to be mu'allal, i.e., the illah is explicitly or strongly implied in the text.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

The Principle of Ta'diyah: Extension of Rulings via ‘Illah
A usul analysis using a sale case and consensus to establish the principle of ta'diyah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that a ruling proven to be extendable (ta'diyah) must be based on an effective cause ('illah), and identify the steps of such extension using consensus and qiyas.

Core Idea
A ruling that can be extended (ta'diyah) must be mu'allal; extension without 'illah is invalid by consensus.

In this passage from a Hanafi usul text, the author uses a specific sale ruling—the invalidity of buying specific wheat with unspecified barley on the spot—to demonstrate a foundational principle: when a ruling is capable of being extended to other cases, it proves that the ruling is not a pure act of devotion (ta'abbud) but is reasoned on an effective cause. The principle of ta'diyah is established by consensus, and the passage shows how the same cause applies to the present case and even to riba al-fadl.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
The source states: 'With food: indeed, taking possession (qabd) is a condition. And we all said regarding one who buys specific wheat with unspecified barley... it is invalid.' So the base ruling is the invalidity of that specific sale.
This ruling is the starting point for the usul analysis.
2
Note the consensus on ra's al-mal in salam
The text says determination of capital in salam is obligatory by consensus. This related ruling also involves a condition (specification) and supports the idea that such rulings are reasoned.
Consensus is used here to establish the binding nature of the condition.
3
Understand the principle of ta'diyah
The text argues: if ta'diyah (extension) is established in that (i.e., the salam ruling), then the ruling is mu'allal (based on a cause). Therefore, it cannot be extended without ta'lil (identifying the cause) by consensus.
Ta'diyah itself proves the existence of an 'illah.
4
Apply the principle
Since extension is valid, the same cause applies to the case at hand, making it identical. Moreover, riba al-fadl is even more firmly established from this cause, showing the power of the 'illah.
The final step shows that the ruling is extendable to other cases, including riba al-fadl.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالطعام : إن التقابض شرط
وقلنا جميعاً فيمن اشترى حنطةً بعينها بشعير بغير عينه حالا غير مؤجل : إنه باطل وإن كان موصوفاً ؛ لما قلنا.
ووجب تعيين رأس مال السلم، بالإجماع.
وإذا ثبت التعدي في ذلك : ثبت أنه معلول، فلا يتعدى بلا تعليل بالإجماع، فقد صح التعدي، ولم تكن الثمنية مانعة، وإذا ثبت فيه : ثبت مسألتنا هذه ؛ لأنه هو بعينه، بل ربا الفضل أثبت منه.
With food: indeed, taking possession (qabd) is a condition. And we all said regarding one who buys specific wheat with unspecified barley, immediate and not deferred: it is invalid, even if described, because of what we said. And the determination of the capital in salam is obligatory by consensus. And when extension (ta'diyah) is established in that, it is established that it is based on an effective cause (mu'allal). So it cannot be extended without a cause (ta'lil) by consensus. Thus extension is correct, and the quality of being a price (thimaniyyah) is not a preventer. And when it is established in that, our case here is established, because it is the same, rather riba al-fadl is more firmly established from it.
Source pages p. 569

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Hanafi school regarding a specific sale transaction and the usul principle of ta'diyah (extension of a ruling via 'illah). The ruling: If someone buys specific wheat (mu'ayyanah) in exchange for unspecified barley (shu'ayr bi ghayri 'aynih) on a spot basis (hala) without deferment, the sale is invalid even if the barley is described (mawsuf). This is based on a prior principle. The text then states that the determination of the principal (ra's al-mal) in a salam contract is obligatory by consensus (ijma). It then moves to a broader principle: when the extension of a ruling (ta'diyah) is established for a particular case, it proves that the ruling is based on an effective cause (mu'allal). Therefore, the ruling cannot be extended without identifying the cause, by consensus. This establishes that extension is valid, and the fact that the item is a price (thaman) does not prevent extension. The argument then applies this to the case at hand: if extension is proven in the salam case, it is also proven in the case under discussion, because it is identical. In fact, the case of riba al-fadl is even stronger. The commentary follows the author's order: first the ruling, then the proof via consensus and ta'diyah, then the analogy to the current case.

Key Terms
التقابض (al-taqabud): Taking possession; condition in sale of food for validity.
التعدي (al-ta'diyah): Extension of a ruling from its original case to other cases via the same effective cause.
معلول (mu'allal): A ruling that is derived based on an effective cause ('illah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the text conclude about the condition of ta'diyah for a ruling?
A) It is only valid if explicitly stated in the Quran.
B) It proves that the ruling is mu'allal and requires an 'illah.
C) It is invalid without a clear text.
D) It applies only to food sales.

Key Takeaway
The ability to extend a ruling (ta'diyah) is proof that the ruling is based on an effective cause, and extension without an 'illah is invalid by consensus.

The 'Illah for Khamr Prohibition: Hanafi–Shafi'i Dispute
Debating whether the prohibition of khamr is specific to the substance or extendable via 'illah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the 'illah for khamr prohibition according to the Hanafi school and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the witness analogy.

Core Idea
Intoxication (sukr) is the 'illah: the prohibition of khamr extends to all intoxicants, not just grape wine.

This lesson explores a classic usul dispute: does the Quranic text prohibiting khamr (wine) restrict the ruling to that specific substance, or does it indicate a general cause that extends to all intoxicants? We will examine the Hanafi argument that the 'illah is intoxication, using a hadith as evidence, and the Shafi'i objection that the text is specific. By analyzing the legal reasoning, you will understand how to identify an 'illah from a text and apply it via qiyas.

Guided Path
1
State the Shafi'i objection
Identify the Shafi'i claim that the text prohibits khamr for its essence ('ayn), not all intoxicants.
The text says 'khamr bi 'aynihi' – this is the basis of the Shafi'i position.
2
Identify the Hanafi evidence
Locate the hadith cited and determine how it indicates the 'illah of sukr.
The hadith includes 'wa ma balagha al-sukr min kulli sharab' – a direct link to intoxication.
3
Analyze the witness analogy
Explain how the Hanafi school uses the analogy of a witness (shahid) to refute the Shafi'i objection.
The text serves as a witness; the specificity objection is treated as ignorance (not a genuine defect).
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
State the practical difference in rulings between the two schools.
Hanafi: any amount of any intoxicant is haram. Shafi'i: only khamr is haram in any amount; other intoxicants must intoxicate.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Assess which position the author favors and why, based on the evidence presented.
The author argues for the Hanafi view by citing the hadith and using the witness analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : إن تحريم الخمر معلول، فلا بد من إقامة الدليل عليه ولا دليل عليه من قبل النص، بل الدليل دل على خلافه، فإن النص أوجب تحريم الخمر لعينها، وليست حرمة سائر الأشربة ونجاستها من باب التعدي، لكنه ثبت بدليل فيه شبهة احتياطاً. ومثال هذا الشاهدُ لما قبلت شهادته مع صفة الجهل بحدود الشرع: بطل الطعن بالجهل وصح الطعن بالرّق، فكذلك ها هنا متى وجدنا النص شاهداً مع ما ذكر من الطعن بطل الطعن، ومتى وقع الطعن في الشاهد بما هو جرح، وهو الرق: لم يجز الحكم بظاهر الحرية إلا بحجة، فكذلك ها هنا لا يصح العمل به مع الاحتمال إلا بحجة، والله أعلم.
وأخرجه الحارثي في مسند أبي حنيفة عنه عن أبي عون محمد بن عبد الله الثقفي عن عبد الله شداد عن ابن عباس أنه قال: «حُرِّمت الخمر لعينها، قليلها وكثيرها، وما بلغ السُّكْرَ من كل شراب». وفي رواية: «والسكر من كل شراب».
Al-Shafi'i, may God have mercy on him, said: Indeed, the prohibition of khamr is based on a cause (mu'allal). So it is necessary to establish evidence for it, and there is no evidence for it from the text; rather, evidence indicates the contrary, for the text obligated the prohibition of khamr for its very essence, and the prohibition and impurity of other beverages are not from the category of extension (ta'diyah), but it is established by evidence containing a doubt, as a precaution. An example of this is the witness whose testimony is accepted despite his ignorance of the boundaries of the Shari'ah: the objection due to ignorance is invalid, and the objection due to slavery is valid. Similarly, here, whenever we find the text as a witness along with the mentioned objection, the objection is invalid. But when the objection to the witness is a valid defect (jarh), such as slavery, it is not permissible to rule by the apparent freedom except with proof. Similarly, here it is not permissible to act upon it despite the probability except with proof, and God knows best. And al-Harithi narrated it in the Musnad of Abu Hanifah from him, from Abu 'Awn Muhammad ibn 'Abdillah al-Thaqafi, from 'Abdillah Shaddad, from Ibn 'Abbas that he said: 'Wine (khamr) was prohibited for its very essence, its little and its much, and whatever reaches intoxication from every beverage.' In a narration: 'And intoxication (sukr) from every beverage.'
Source pages p. 569

Commentary
This passage is a dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools concerning whether the prohibition of khamr is restricted to the specific substance (khamr 'ayn) or extends to all intoxicants via an effective cause ('illah of sukr). The text begins by attributing to al-Shafi'i the argument that the prohibition is mu'allal (based on a cause) but then states that al-Shafi'i denies any evidence for an 'illah from the text; rather, the text explicitly prohibits khamr itself ('ayniha). Therefore, according to al-Shafi'i, the prohibition of other beverages and their impurity are not derived by extension (ta'diyah) but are established by a separate evidence containing some doubt (shubhah), as a precaution. The text then provides a Hanafi counter-argument using the analogy (tamthil) of a witness (shahid) whose testimony is accepted despite his ignorance of Shari'ah boundaries. This shows that even if the text appears specific ('ayn khamr), it can still function as a witness (shahid) for the 'illah. The objection (ta'n) that the text only mentions khamr is like an objection due to ignorance (jahr bi hudud al-shar'), which is invalid. However, if the objection is a genuine defect (jarh) like slavery (al-riqq), then it cannot be overridden without proof. Similarly, here, the presence of the text as a witness overrides the objection that the 'illah is not textually supported. But if there were a clear contradiction (like raq), it could not be dismissed without evidence. The author then quotes a hadith from the Musnad of Abu Hanifah (via al-Harithi) where Ibn 'Abbas states that khamr was prohibited for its very essence (li 'aynihi), both little and much, and whatever reaches intoxication from any beverage. This hadith supports the Hanafi view that the 'illah of sukr applies generally.

Key Terms
الخمر (al-khamr): Wine from grapes; the specific substance prohibited by name in the Quran.
السكر (al-sukr): Intoxication, the effective cause ('illah) for prohibition of all intoxicants according to Hanafis.
التعدي (al-ta'diyah): Extension of a ruling from its original case (khamr) to other cases via the 'illah.
الشاهد (al-shahid): The text (nass) used as evidence for the 'illah; analogy to a witness in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position presented, what is the 'illah (effective cause) for the prohibition of khamr?
A) The substance itself (khamr 'ayn)
B) Intoxication (sukr)
C) The quantity consumed
D) The type of grape

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school derives the 'illah of sukr from the hadith, extending khamr's prohibition to all intoxicants, while the Shafi'i school restricts it to grape wine.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

Four Conditions of Qiyas in Hanafi Usul
Enumeration and explanation of four conditions for valid qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the four conditions for valid qiyas in Hanafi usul and explain the rationale behind each.

Core Idea
Qiyas requires the original case's ruling not be text-specified, not abnormal, extendable, and remain unchanged.

Qiyas (analogical reasoning) is a key source of Islamic law, but its proper use is guarded by precise conditions. In this passage, al-Bazdawi lists four prerequisites that ensure qiyas does not conflict with textual authority or logical consistency. Understanding these conditions helps you distinguish valid analogical reasoning from flawed applications.

Guided Path
1
Identify Condition 1 and its rationale
Read the first condition: the asl must not have its ruling restricted by another text. Understand why ta'lil cannot invalidate a text.
This condition prevents qiyas from overriding a specific divine command.
2
Identify Condition 2 and its rationale
Read the second condition: the asl's ruling must not itself deviate from qiyas. Understand why such a ruling cannot serve as an asl.
This ensures consistency; abnormal rulings are not extended.
3
Identify Condition 3 and its rationale
Read the third condition: the ruling must extend to an analogue branch with no text. Understand the comparison aspect of qiyas.
Qiyas requires both asl and far' share the effective cause.
4
Identify Condition 4 and its rationale
Read the fourth condition: the asl's ruling must remain after ta'lil. Understand that qiyas does not change the original ruling.
This reaffirms that qiyas cannot contradict the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي أربعة أوجه:
باب شروط القياس
١- أن لا يكون الأصل مخصوصاً بحكمه بنص آخر.
٢- وأن لا يكون حكمه معدولاً به عن القياس.
٣- وأن يتعدى الحكم الشرعي الثابت بالنص بعينه إلى فرع هو نظيره، ولا نص فيه.
٤- وأن يبقى الحكم في الأصل بعد التعليل على ما كان قبله.
أما الأول: فلأنه متى ثبت اختصاصه بالنص: صار التعليل مبطلاً له، وذلك باطل؛ لأنه لا يعارضه.
وأما الثاني: فلأن حاجتنا إلى إثبات الحكم بالقياس، فإذا جاء مخالفاً للقياس: لم يصح إثباته به، كالنص النافي لا يصلح للإثبات.
وأما الثالث: فلأن القياس محاذاة بين شيئين، فلا ينفعل إلا محله، وهو الفرع والأصل معاً، وإنما التعليل لإقامة حكم شرعي، وفي هذه الجملة خلاف.
وأما الرابع: فلما قلنا إن القياس لا يُعارض النص، فلا يتغير به حكمه.
And they are four aspects:
Chapter on conditions of analogy
1- That the original [case] not be specified in its ruling by another text.
2- And that its ruling not be deviated from analogy.
3- And that the legal ruling established by the very text extend to a branch that is its analogue, and there is no text regarding it.
4- And that the ruling in the original [case] remain after the [act of] reasoning by effective cause as it was before.
As for the first: because whenever its specification by the text is established, the reasoning by effective cause becomes invalidating it, and that is invalid, because it does not contradict it.
And as for the second: because our need is to establish the ruling by analogy, so if it comes contrary to analogy, it is not valid to establish it by it, like a negating text is not suitable for affirmation.
And as for the third: because analogy is a comparison between two things, so it only becomes effective in its place, which is the branch and the original together, and reasoning by effective cause is only to establish a legal ruling, and there is disagreement regarding this summary.
And as for the fourth: because we said that analogy does not contradict the text, so the ruling does not change by it.
Source pages p. 571

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul defines the four conditions for the validity of qiyas (analogical reasoning) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author first lists them, then provides the rationale for each. Condition one: the original case (asl) must not have its ruling restricted by another text, otherwise the act of reasoning (ta'lil) would invalidate the text, which is impermissible. Condition two: the ruling of the asl must not itself deviate from qiyas; if it does, one cannot use qiyas to prove another ruling because the asl is abnormal. Condition three: the ruling from the asl must be transmittable to a branch (far') that is its analogue, with no text governing the branch; qiyas is a comparison and only works when both asl and far' share the effective cause ('illa); there is some scholarly debate on this point. Condition four: the ruling in the asl must remain intact after ta'lil; qiyas does not change the original ruling because it cannot contradict the text.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyās): Analogical reasoning: extending a ruling from an original case (asl) to a branch (far') due to shared effective cause.
الأصل (al-aṣl): The original case with a known ruling from text or consensus.
الفرع (al-far‘): The branch case to which the ruling is extended via qiyas.
التعليل (al-ta‘līl): The process of identifying the effective cause ('illa) for the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which condition ensures that qiyas does not conflict with the original text?
A) Condition 1: The original must not be specified by another text.
B) Condition 2: The ruling must not deviate from analogy.
C) Condition 3: The ruling must extend to a branch with no text.
D) Condition 4: The ruling in the original remains unchanged.

Key Takeaway
Valid qiyas requires these four conditions: the original ruling is not text-specified, not abnormal, extendable, and remains unchanged.

Textual Exceptions Not Subject to Qiyas
Illustrates three textual exceptions that cannot be generalized by analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a textual exception (khass) cannot be extended by qiyas, and state the three Hanafi examples provided.

Core Idea
Textual exceptions established as an honor (karamah) cannot be generalized by analogy.

In Hanafi usul, not every ruling found in a text can be used as a basis for analogical reasoning. Some exceptions are explicitly designated as special honors or concessions, and these remain confined to their specific context. This lesson examines three such cases from the source text, illustrating how the indication of specificity overrides the normal operation of qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the general rule
Identify the default legal rule that applies to most cases. For example, testimony requires multiple witnesses; a man may marry up to four women; a sale requires an owned and deliverable item.
2
Locate the textual exception
Find the specific ruling in the sacred texts that contradicts or deviates from the general rule. Examples: acceptance of Khuzaymah's sole testimony, permission for nine wives for the Prophet, and salam (advance sale) in debt.
3
Identify the indicator of specificity
Look for phrases that mark the exception as personal, honorific, or exclusive. In the source, phrases like 'كرامة له' (an honor for him) or 'خالصة لك' (exclusively for you) signal that the ruling is not meant to be generalized.
4
Apply the principle: no qiyas
Conclude that the exception cannot be extended by analogy because its basis is karamah (honor) rather than a common effective cause (illah). Attempting to generalize would invalidate the specific designation.
This principle is agreed upon by both Hanafi and Shafi'i schools in these examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مثال الأول : أن الله تعالى شرط العدد في عامة الشهادات، وثبت بالنص قبول شهادة خزيمة وحده رضي الله عنه، لكنه ثبت كرامةً له، فلم يصح إبطاله بالتعليل. وحل للنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم تسع نسوة إكراماً له، فلم يصح. وكذلك ثبت بالنص أن البيع يقتضي محلاً مملوكاً مقدوراً. وجوز السلم في الدين بالنص، وهو قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم: من أسلم منكم فليسلم في كيل معلوم ووزن معلوم إلى أجل معلوم. وما ثبت بهذا النص إلا مؤجلاً، فلم يستقم إبطال الخصوص بالتعليل. وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : لما صح نكاح النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام بلفظة الهبة على سبيل الخصوص بقوله تعالى: {خالصة لك}
The first example: Allah required a number in general testimonies, and it is established by text acceptance of the testimony of Khuzaymah alone, may Allah be pleased with him, but it was established as an honor for him, so it is not valid to invalidate it by rationalization. And it was permitted for the Prophet, peace be upon him, nine wives as an honor for him, so it is not valid [to generalize]. Similarly, it is established by text that sale requires a owned and possessed subject matter. And salam (advance payment) in debt was permitted by text, which is his saying, peace be upon him: 'Whoever among you pays in advance, let him pay in advance with a known measure, a known weight, and a known term.' And nothing is established by this text except deferred [payment], so it is not correct to invalidate the specific by rationalization. And al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, said: Since the marriage of the Prophet, peace be upon him, by the word 'gift' was established as a special case by His saying: 'Exclusively for you'
Source pages p. 572

Commentary
This passage presents a key usul principle: a ruling that is textually established as a special exception (khass) cannot be extended by qiyas (analogy) because the rationale (ta'lil) that would allow generalization is overridden by the text's indication of specificity. The author gives three examples from Hanafi usul: (1) The general rule for testimony requires multiple witnesses, but the Prophet ﷺ accepted the lone testimony of Khuzaymah ibn Thabit as a special honor, so this exception cannot be analogized to other cases. (2) The Prophet ﷺ was permitted nine wives while ordinary men are limited to four; this is an honored exception and cannot serve as a basis for qiyas. (3) Generally, sale requires an owned and deliverable item, but salam (advance payment) is allowed even though the item is not yet owned; the text permits it only as deferred, so the exception remains specific. The passage then notes al-Shafi'i's agreement that the Prophet's marriage by gift (hiba) is a textual special case, implying that the principle of not extending such exceptions is agreed upon across schools.

Key Terms
تعليل (ta'lil): Rationalization or providing an effective cause (illa) to extend a ruling analogically.
خصوص (khussus): A specific, exceptional ruling that deviates from the general rule due to a textual indication.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why can the acceptance of Khuzaymah's sole testimony not be used as a basis for qiyas to reduce the number of witnesses in other cases?
A) Because the general rule requiring multiple witnesses is stronger.
B) Because it was established as a special honor (karamah) for Khuzaymah, not as a general legal principle.
C) Because the hadith is weak in transmission.
D) Because Qiyas cannot be applied to testimony rules.

Key Takeaway
A textual exception that is explicitly marked as a personal honor or special permission cannot be extended by qiyas; it remains confined to its specific case.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

Rulings Deviated from Qiyas Cannot Be Subject to Ta'lil
Explaining that rulings deviated from qiyas cannot be causally reasoned

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a ruling as ma'dul bihi 'an al-qiyas and explain why it cannot be subjected to ta'lil (causal reasoning).

Core Idea
Rulings deviated from analogy by explicit text are closed to analogical extension.

This lesson from al-Bazdawi's Usul addresses a critical limitation on analogical reasoning: when a ruling is established by explicit text and deviates from the apparent analogy, it cannot be used as a basis for qiyas. Understanding this distinction protects the authority of nass and prevents wrongful extension of exceptional rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the concept
Recognize that certain rulings are established by nass and conflict with what qiyas would require. They are 'deviated' (ma'dul bihi) not 'excepted' (makhsus).
The passage explicitly distinguishes ma'dul bihi from makhsus.
2
Understand why ta'lil is prohibited
Ta'lil is tied to qiyas; applying a cause to a deviated ruling would invert the intended effect, making causal reasoning for the opposite of what it was set for.
3
Analyze the examples
Three examples are given: forgetful eating in fast, forgetful tasmiya on slaughter, and the Bedouin's expiation. Each demonstrates a ruling that is ma'dul bihi and thus not open to ta'lil.
The examples show the practical application of the theoretical rule.
4
Distinguish from istihsan and other principles
Istihsan is based on hidden analogy (qiyas khafi), not deviation. Also, a principle opposed by multiple others is not ma'dul bihi but subject to tarjih.
This clarifies boundaries: not every special ruling is ma'dul bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النسيان بالنص) معدولاً به عن القياس، لا مخصوصاً من النص، فلم يصح والتعليل للقياس وهو معدول به عنه فيصير التعليل حينئذ لضد ما وضع له. مثله ولم يثبت هذا الحكم في مواقعة الناسي بالتعليل، بل بدلالة النص؛ لأنهما سواء في قيام الركن بالكفّ عنهما ، ألا يُرى أن معنى الحديث لغةً: أن الناسي غيرُ جان على الصوم ولا على الطعام، فكان الجماع بدلالة النص، على ما مر. وكذلك تَرْكُ التسمية على الذبيحة ناسياً جُعل عفواً بالنص (۲)، معدولاً به عن القياس، فلم يحتمل التعليل. وكذلك حديث الأعرابي الذي قال له رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : كُل أنتَ وعيالك» (۱): هذا معدول به عن القياس؛ لأن التكفير إنما يكون بما يقع عليه من بدني أو مالي، لا بما يقعُ له، وكان الأعرابي مخصوصاً بالنص، فلم يحتمل التعليل. فأما المستحسنات: فمنها ما ثبت بقياس خفي، لا معدولاً به. وأما الأصل إذا عارضه أصول : فلا يسمى معدولاً به عن القياس؛ لأن عدداً التعليل لا يقتضي من الأصول، ولكنه مما يصلح للترجيح، على مثال ما قلنا في عدد الرواة. وأما الثالث: فأعظم هذه الوجوه فقهاً وأعمها نفعاً، وهذا الشرط واحد تسمية، وجملة تفصيلاً. من ذلك : أن يكون الحكم المعلول شرعياً، لا لغوياً. ولهذا قلنا: إن من علَّل بالرأي لاستعمال ألفاظ الطلاق في باب العتاق كان باطلاً؛ لأن الاستعارة من باب اللغة، لا تُنال إلا بالتأمل في معاني اللغة، فكذلك جواز النكاح بألفاظ التمليك، واستعارة كلمة النسب للتحرير وكذلك التعليل بشرط التمليك في الطعام في كفارة اليمين : باطل
The ruling on forgetfulness is established by the text (nass), deviated from analogy, not as an exception from the text, so it is not valid to apply causal reasoning to it, and causal reasoning is for analogy, and when it is deviated from it, then causal reasoning becomes for the opposite of what it was set for. Similarly, this ruling is not established in the case of the forgetful person by causal reasoning, but by the indication of the text, because both are equal in the standing of the pillar by refraining from them. Do you not see that the meaning of the hadith linguistically is that the forgetful person is not an aggressor against fasting or against food, so the (ruling on) intercourse is by the indication of the text, as has passed. Likewise, leaving the tasmiya (saying Bismillah) on the animal slaughtered forgetfully is made excusable by the text, deviated from analogy, so it does not admit causal reasoning. Likewise, the hadith of the Bedouin to whom the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Eat, you and your family': this is deviated from analogy, because expiation is only by what falls upon the person (as punishment) bodily or financially, not by what falls for him, and the Bedouin was an exception by the text, so it does not admit causal reasoning. As for istihsan (juristic preference): some of it is established by hidden analogy (qiyas khafi), not deviated from analogy. As for a principle (asl) when it is opposed by other principles: it is not called deviated from analogy, because the number of causal reasoning does not necessitate (choosing) from the principles, but it is suitable for tarjih (preference), as in the example we gave about the number of narrators. As for the third: it is the greatest of these aspects in fiqh and the most beneficial in general, and this condition is one in name but detailed in summary. Among that: that the ma'lul (reasoned) ruling be shar'i (legal), not lughawi (linguistic). For this reason we said: whoever applies causal reasoning by opinion to the use of divorce terms in the chapter of manumission is invalid, because metaphor is from the domain of language, which is only attained by contemplating the meanings of language. Similarly, the permissibility of marriage with words of ownership (tamlik), and the metaphorical use of the word lineage (nasab) for freeing, and likewise causal reasoning with the condition of ownership in food in the expiation of an oath is invalid.
Source pages p. 575

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul outlines an important principle: rulings that are established by explicit textual evidence (nass) and are deviated from the general rule of qiyas (analogy) cannot be subjected to ta'lil (causal reasoning). The reason is that ta'lil is tied to qiyas; if a ruling is already deviated from qiyas, applying a cause (illa) to it would produce a result opposite to what the text intended. The author provides three examples: (1) the ruling that a fasting person who forgetfully eats or drinks does not break his fast—this is from hadith, not qiyas, and cannot be reasoned by cause; (2) forgetting the tasmiya when slaughtering is excused by text, not by analogy; (3) the Bedouin who was told by the Prophet to eat the expiation food himself and his family—this is an exception from the normal rule that expiation is given to others, thus deviated from qiyas. The author then distinguishes these from istihsan, which is based on hidden analogy (qiyas khafi) and thus still within the domain of analogical reasoning. Finally, a third condition for ta'lil is introduced: the ma'lul ruling must be a shar'i (legal) ruling, not a lughawi (linguistic) one. Applying ta'lil to linguistic matters (like metaphor in divorce terms for manumission) is invalid because language requires its own methods of investigation.

Key Terms
معدول به عن القياس (ma'dūlan bihi 'an al-qiyās): A ruling established by nass that goes against the apparent analogy (qiyas jali), thus it is deviated from qiyas and cannot be reasoned by analogy.
تعليل (ta'līl): The process of assigning a legal cause (illa) to a ruling and extending it by analogy; this passage prohibits it for rulings ma'dul bihi.
مستحسنات (mustahsanāt): Rulings derived by istihsan (juristic preference) based on hidden analogy (qiyas khafi), not deviated from qiyas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why can a ruling that is 'ma'dul bihi 'an al-qiyas' not be subject to ta'lil?
A) Because it is based on istihsan, not textual evidence.
B) Because applying a cause would contradict the text's intended deviation from analogy.
C) Because such rulings are only applicable to the specific incident and cannot be generalized.
D) Because ta'lil is only allowed for linguistic rulings, not legal ones.

Key Takeaway
Rulings established by nass that deviate from the apparent analogy are closed to analogical reasoning; they must be accepted as textual exceptions.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
8 min
Conditions for Valid Ta'lil: Hukm Must Be Shar'i and Ta'diyah
States two conditions for valid legal causation: shar'i ruling and transitivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between shar'i and linguistic rulings for valid ta'lil, and evaluate the necessity of ta'diyah in Hanafi vs Shafi'i usul.

Core Idea
A valid 'illa must explain a shar'i ruling and be transitive to new cases.

This passage from Al-Bazdawi's 'Kanz al-Wusul' outlines two critical conditions for a valid legal cause ('illa). First, the ruling being explained must be a shar'i ruling, not a linguistic one. Second, the 'illa must be transitive (ta'diyah) to analogous cases. The Hanafi school insists on these conditions, leading to a significant dispute with al-Shafi'i over whether a non-transitive 'illa can be valid. Understanding these conditions is essential for proper qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
Extract from the text the two conditions for a valid 'illa: that the ruling be shar'i (not lughawi) and that the 'illa be transitive (ta'diyah). Note the author's statement: 'First, the ruling must be shar'i... Second, transitivity.'
2
Examine examples of invalid linguistic 'illas
Review the examples given: using divorce words for manumission, marriage by ownership words, and requiring ownership in food for kaffarah. Understand why these are invalid because the terms are linguistic, not shar'i rulings.
The text says 'food' (الإطعام) is a linguistic name; thus, the ruling derived is not shar'i.
3
State the Hanafi position on ta'diyah
The Hanafi position: ta'diyah is necessary; without it, the 'illa has no effect. The text argues that a proof must produce knowledge or action, and a non-transitive 'illa does neither.
4
Identify the Shafi'i counterposition
Al-Shafi'i is cited as holding that ta'diyah is not necessary. He allows 'illas like 'being a price' (al-thamaniyya) even if not transitive, because the 'illa is a proof that obligates action.
5
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafi response: the text is above the 'illa; the 'illa cannot alter the text. Its only function is to extend the ruling to branches, so without transitivity, it serves no purpose.
The author uses the principle: 'النص فوق التعليل' (the text is above causation).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثالث: فأعظم هذه الوجوه فقهاً وأعمها نفعاً، وهذا الشرط واحد تسمية، وجملة تفصيلاً. من ذلك: أن يكون الحكم المعلول شرعياً، لا لغوياً. ولهذا قلنا: إن من علل بالرأي لاستعمال ألفاظ الطلاق في باب العتاق كان باطلاً؛ لأن الاستعارة من باب اللغة، لا تنال إلا بالتأمل في معاني اللغة، فكذلك جواز النكاح بألفاظ التمليك، واستعارة كلمة النسب للتحرير وكذلك التعليل بشرط التمليك في الطعام في كفارة اليمين: باطل عندنا؛ لأن الإطعام اسم لغوي. وكذلك الكسوة، فلا يكون ما يُعقل بالكسوة حكماً شرعياً ليصح تعديته بالتعليل إلى غيره بل يجب العمل بحقيقة الإطعام، وهو أن المرء طاعماً، ثم يصح التمليك بدلالة النص، فأما الكسوة فاسم لما يلبس لا لمنافع اللباس، فبطل التعليل من كل وجه. وكذلك التعليل لإثبات اسم الزنا للواطة، واسم الخمر لسائر الأشربة، واسم السارق للنباش: باطل؛ لما بينا. والثاني من هذه الجملة: التعدية: فإن حكم التعليل: التعدية عندنا، فبطل التعليل بدونه. وقال الشافعي رحمه الله: هو صحيح من غير شرط التعدية، حتى جوز التعليل بالثمنية واحتج بأن هذا لما كان من جنس الحجج: وجب أن يتعلق به الإيجاب، مثل سائر الحجج. ألا يرى أن دلالة كون الوصف علة لا تقتضي تعديته، بل يُعرف ذلك بمعنى في الوصف. ووجه قولنا: إن دليل الشرع لا بد من أن علماً، أو عملاً، يوجب وهذا لا يوجب علماً بلا خلاف ولا يوجب عملاً في المنصوص عليه؛ لأنه ثابت بالنص، والنص فوق التعليل، فلا يصح قطعه عنه به، فلم يبق فلا يصح للتعليل حكم إلا التعدية إلى الفروع. فإن قال: إن حكم النص ثابت بالعلة كان باطلاً؛ لأن التعليل لا يصلح لتغيير حكم النص به، فكيف لإبطاله؟!
As for the third: it is the greatest of these aspects in jurisprudence and the most comprehensive in benefit, and this condition is one in name and detailed in summary. Among that: that the ruling being explained by the cause be a shar'i ruling, not a linguistic one. For this reason we said: whoever provides a reason based on opinion for using words of divorce in the chapter of manumission is invalid because metaphor is from the domain of language, not attained except by reflection on linguistic meanings. Similarly, the permissibility of marriage using words of ownership, and the metaphorical use of the word of lineage for freeing, and similarly the causation by the condition of ownership in food for the expiation of an oath is invalid according to us, because 'feeding' is a linguistic name. Similarly, clothing, so what is understood by clothing is not a shar'i ruling to make its transitivity by causation to others valid; rather one must act by the literal meaning of feeding, which is that a person becomes fed, then ownership is valid by the indication of the text. As for clothing, it is a name for what is worn, not for the benefits of clothing, so the causation is invalid from every aspect. Similarly, causation to establish the name of adultery for sodomy, and the name of wine for other intoxicating drinks, and the name of thief for a grave robber is invalid for what we have mentioned. The second of these [elements]: transitivity, for the effect of causation is transitivity according to us, so causation is invalid without it. Al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, said: it is valid without the condition of transitivity, to the point that he allowed causation by the [nature of] price, and argued that because this is of the type of proofs, obligation must attach to it like other proofs. Do you not see that the indication of a description being a cause does not necessitate its transitivity, but that is known by a meaning in the description. The basis of our statement: that a legal proof must produce either knowledge or action. This [non-transitive 'illa] produces neither knowledge without dispute nor action in the text-based ruling because it is established by the text, and the text is above causation, so it cannot be severed from it by causation. Therefore no effect remains for causation except transitivity to branches. If he says: the ruling of the text is established by the cause, it is invalid because causation cannot change the ruling of the text, so how can it nullify it?
Source pages p. 576

Commentary
This passage discusses two crucial conditions for valid legal causation ('illa). First, the ruling to be explained (al-hukm al-ma'lūl) must be a shar'i ruling, not a linguistic one. Examples include using divorce words for manumission (invalid because metaphorical language is linguistic), marriage by ownership words, using kinship terms for freeing, and requiring ownership in food for kaffarah (invalid because 'food' is a linguistic term). Second, the 'illa must be transitive (ta'diyah) to other cases. The Hanafi position is that without transitivity, causation has no effect, as the original ruling is already proven by text. Al-Shafi'i disagreed, allowing non-transitive 'illa (e.g., 'being a price' as an 'illa). The Hanafi response is that a proof must lead to knowledge or action; a non-transitive 'illa gives neither new knowledge (text already proves it) nor new action (no change), so it is pointless. The only remaining function of 'illa is to extend the ruling to new cases.

Key Terms
حكم شرعي (ḥukm sharʿī): A ruling from the Lawgiver (Shar') that is not merely linguistic.
تعدية (taʿdiyah): Transitivity of the 'illa to analogous cases; the 'illa must extend beyond the original ruling.
تعليل (taʿlīl): Legal causation: explaining a ruling by an 'illa to allow extension to new cases.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school reject using the word 'food' (الإطعام) as an 'illa in kaffarah rulings?
A) Because 'food' is a linguistic term, not a shar'i ruling.
B) Because 'food' is ambiguous.
C) Because the ruling has already been abrogated.
D) Because the 'illa is not transitive.

Key Takeaway
A valid 'illa requires that the ruling be shar'i and that the 'illa be transitive; without transitivity, the 'illa serves no purpose.

The 'illa Must Be a Shar'i Ruling
Rule: 'illa must be shar'i ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a proposed description (wasf) qualifies as a valid 'illa by distinguishing between a shar'i ruling and a linguistic name.

Core Idea
Linguistic names are not shar'i rulings; they cannot serve as legal causes in qiyas.

When performing qiyas, the 'illa (legal cause) must be a shar'i ruling—an attribute that carries legal significance. Mere linguistic names, like 'feeding' or 'clothing', describe acts but do not convey a shar'i ruling. This lesson explains why such terms fail as 'illa and illustrates the principle with several examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
The passage states that the 'illa must be a hukm shar'i (shar'i ruling), not an ism lughawi (linguistic name). Locate the key phrase 'عندنا؛ لأن الإطعام اسم لغوي'.
This is a foundational rule in Hanafi usul.
2
Analyze the example of it'am
'It'am' (feeding) is a linguistic name for an act. It does not contain a shar'i ruling. The passage notes that one must act on the reality of it'am (i.e., the act of feeding) and then tamlik (transfer of ownership) is validated by textual indication, not by ta'lil.
The shar'i ruling here is tamlik, not it'am.
3
Analyze the example of kiswa
'Kiswa' (clothing) is a name for what is worn, not a name for the benefits (covering) that are shar'i. Therefore, ta'lil based on kiswa fails completely.
The benefit of covering is a shar'i ruling, but the linguistic name 'kiswa' does not convey it.
4
Apply to other examples
The passage lists three more invalid ta'lil attempts: using the name of zina for liwat, khamr for other intoxicants, and sariq for grave-robbers. Each fails because the terms are linguistic names, not shar'i rulings.
Shar'i rulings for those acts exist (e.g., prohibition of intoxicants), but they are not carried by the linguistic name itself.
5
State the conclusion
For ta'lil to be valid, the 'illa must be a shar'i ruling extracted from the text or agreed upon, not a mere linguistic label. The principle prevents extending rulings based on superficial verbal similarities.
This ensures analogical reasoning is grounded in legal meaning, not language.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عندنا؛ لأن الإطعام اسم لغوي. وكذلك الكسوة، فلا يكون ما يُعقل بالكسوة حكماً شرعياً ليصح تعديته بالتعليل إلى غيره بل يجب العمل بحقيقة الإطعام، وهو أن المرء طاعماً، ثم يصح التمليك بدلالة النص، فأما الكسوة فاسم لما يلبس لا لمنافع اللباس، فبطل التعليل من كل وجه. وكذلك التعليل لإثبات اسم الزنا للواطة، واسم الخمر لسائر الأشربة، واسم السارق للنباش : باطل ; لما بينا.
With us: because it'am (feeding) is a linguistic name. And similarly kiswa (clothing), so what is understood by kiswa is not a shar'i ruling to make its transference by ta'lil to others valid. Rather, one must act upon the reality of it'am, which is that a person is fed, then tamlik (transfer of ownership) becomes valid by textual indication. As for kiswa, it is a name for what is worn, not for the benefits of clothing, so the ta'lil is invalid from every aspect. And similarly, ta'lil to establish the name of zina for liwat, the name of khamr for other intoxicating drinks, and the name of sariq for the grave robber: it is invalid, due to what we have explained.
Source pages p. 577

Commentary
This passage discusses a fundamental rule in Hanafi usul: the 'illa (legal cause) used in qiyas must be a shar'i ruling (hukm shar'i), not a mere linguistic name (ism lughawi). The author provides examples: 'it'am' (feeding) is a linguistic term describing an act, not a shar'i ruling; therefore, it cannot be used as an 'illa to extend rulings to other cases. Similarly, 'kiswa' (clothing) is a name for something worn, not for the shar'i benefit of covering, so ta'lil based on it is invalid. The author then mentions that using linguistic names to establish rulings like zina for liwat, khamr for other intoxicants, and sariq for grave-robbers is also invalid because these names are linguistic, not shar'i. The underlying principle is that ta'lil requires a shar'i ruling to be transferred; linguistic descriptions do not carry the authority needed for analogy.

Key Terms
الإطعام (al-it'am): A linguistic name for the act of feeding; not a shar'i ruling.
الكسوة (al-kiswa): A linguistic name for clothing; not a shar'i ruling.
العلة (al-'illa): The legal cause in qiyas; must be a shar'i ruling for valid ta'lil.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The 'illa in qiyas must be a shar'i ruling (hukm shar'i); linguistic names (asmā' lughawiyya) are invalid as legal causes.

Ta'lil Requires Transitivity and Preservation of the Ruling
Arguing that ta'lil must be transitive and cannot change the original ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi requirement that the 'illa be transitive (muta'addi) and identify why a non-transitive 'illa is invalid, and also apply the prohibition of changing the original ruling through ta'lil.

Core Idea
Ta'lil without transitivity is invalid: the only purpose of the 'illa is to extend the ruling to new cases.

This passage from Al-Bazdawi's Usul tackles a fundamental dispute between the Hanafis and Shafi'is: must the 'illa in analogical reasoning be applicable to other cases? The Hanafi school insists on transitivity (al-ta'diya), arguing that ta'lil is meaningless if it does not generate a ruling for new instances. Imam al-Shafi'i, however, allows non-transitive 'illa, such as 'being a fruit' (al-thamaniyya), as a valid cause for a ruling. The passage also warns against using ta'lil to alter the original ruling—the 'illa must transmit the exact ruling unchanged. Through the examples of salam and fasting, the author illustrates what constitutes an impermissible change.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim and dispute
State the Hanafi position: ta'lil requires transitivity. State the Shafi'i position: transitivity is not a condition. Note the key term 'thamaniyya' as an example of a non-transitive 'illa.
This sets the stage for the entire argument.
2
Analyze the Hanafi proof against non-transitive ta'lil
Examine the rational argument: a legal proof must produce knowledge or action. A non-transitive 'illa produces neither new knowledge about the original text (already fixed) nor new action (the ruling is already acted upon). Thus, it is redundant and invalid.
Focus on the phrase: 'لا يوجب علماً بلا خلاف ولا يوجب عملاً في المنصوص عليه'.
3
Examine the Shafi'i argument and the Hanafi response
Shafi'i: ta'lil is a proof like any other, so it establishes obligation. Non-transitive 'illa can still specify the text. Hanafi response: specification is already achieved by not doing ta'lil at all; moreover, non-transitive ta'lil does not prevent transitive ta'lil, so its purported benefit is null.
This is the critical exchange in the dispute.
4
Understand the second principle: no change to the original ruling
The passage then asserts that ta'lil must transmit the exact ruling without change. It uses the example of salam: the concession for deferred delivery transfers the condition of possession from immediate existence to time; to use ta'lil to cancel the concession would be a change and thus invalid.
The key is that the 'illa must replicate the ruling, not alter it.
5
Apply the principle to the fasting example
The passage rejects the analogy that the mistaken and coerced break their fast due to lack of intent (like the forgetful). It argues that the forgetful's fast remains valid not because of lack of intent but because of divine grace; thus, the analogy fails as it changes the underlying reasoning.
This shows a flawed qiyas where the 'illa is misidentified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني من هذه الجملة : التعديةُ: فإن حكم التعليل: التعدية عندنا، فبطل التعليل بدونه. وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : هو صحيح من غير شرط التعدية، حتى جوز التعليل بالثَّمَنية واحتج بأن هذا لما كان من جنس الحجج: وجب أن يتعلق به الإيجاب، مثل سائر الحجج. ألا يُرى أن دلالة كون الوصف علةً لا تقتضي تعديته، بل يُعرف ذلك بمعنى في الوصف. ووجه :قولنا: إن دليل الشرع لا بد من أن علماً، أو عملاً، يوجب وهذا لا يوجب علماً بلا خلاف ولا يوجب عملاً في المنصوص عليه؛ لأنه ثابت بالنص، والنص فوق التعليل، فلا يصح قطعه عنه به، فلم يبق للتعليل حكم إلا التعدية إلى الفروع. فإن قال: إن حكم النص ثابت بالعلة كان باطلاً؛ لأن التعليل لا يصلح لتغيير حكم النص به ، فكيف لإبطاله؟! فإن قيل : إن التعليل بما لا يتعدى يفيدُ اختصاص النص به. قيل له : هذا يحصل بترك التعليل على أن التعليل بما لا يتعدى: لا يمنع التعليل بما يتعدى، فتبطل هذه الفائدة. ومن هذه الجملة : أن يكون المتعدي حكم النص بعينه من غير تغيير ; لما ذكرنا أن ثمرة التعليل التعدية لا غير، فأما التغيير : فلا ، فإذا كان التعليل مغيّراً : كان ذلك باطلاً . ومن ذلك : ما قلنا: إن السَّلَم الحال باطل؛ لأن من شرط جواز البيع : أن يكون المبيع موجوداً مملوكاً مقدوراً، والشرع رخص في السلم بصفة الأجل، وتفسيره : نَقل الشرط الأصلي إلى ما يخلفه، وهو الأجل؛ لأن الزمان يصلح للكسب الذي هو من أسباب القدرة، فاستقام خَلَفاً عنه، وإذا كان النص ناقلاً للشرط، وكانت رخصة نقل: لم يستقم التعليل للإسقاط والإبطال ; لأنه تغيير محض. ومن ذلك : قولهم في الخاطئ والمكرَه : إن فعلهما لا يكون فطراً ; لعدم القصد، كفعل الناسي. وهذا تعليل باطل ; لأن بقاء الصوم مع النسيان: ليس لعدم القصد ; لأن فوات الركن يُعدم الأداء، وليس لعدم القصد أثر في الوجود مع قيام حقيقة العدم، ألا يُرى أن مَن لم يَنوِ الصومَ أصلاً; لأنه لم يشعر بشهر رمضان لم يكن صائماً، والقصد لم يوجد ، لكنه لم يُجعَل فطراً بالنص، وهو غير معلول على ما قلنا. وعلى هذا الأصل سقط فعل الناسي; لأن النسيان أمر جُبل عليه الإنسان، فكان سماوياً محضاً، فنُسب إلى صاحب الحق، فلم يصلح
And the second of these categories: transitivity. Indeed, the ruling of ta'lil is transitivity with us, so ta'lil is invalid without it. Al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, said: It is valid without the condition of transitivity, even allowing ta'lil by thamaniyya. He argued that since this is a type of proof, obligation must attach to it, like other proofs. Do you not see that the indication that a description is an 'illa does not necessitate its transitivity; rather, that is known by a meaning in the description. And the point of our statement: Indeed, a legal proof must necessitate knowledge or action. This does not necessitate knowledge without disagreement, nor does it necessitate action in the textually prescribed (mansus 'alayh) because it is established by the text, and the text is above ta'lil, so it cannot be detached from it by ta'lil. Thus, no ruling remains for ta'lil except transitivity to the branches. If it is said that the ruling of the text is established by the 'illa, that is invalid because ta'lil cannot change the ruling of the text by it, so how can it nullify it? If it is said: Ta'lil with something non-transitive benefits the specification of the text. It is said to him: This is achieved by abandoning ta'lil, and ta'lil with something non-transitive does not prevent ta'lil with something transitive, so this benefit is nullified. And from this category: that the transitive [illa] be the exact ruling of the text, without change; because we mentioned that the fruit of ta'lil is only transitivity, so as for change, no. Thus, if the ta'lil changes, it is invalid. And of that is what we said: that forward salam (salam hal) is invalid because among the conditions for the validity of sale is that the sold item be existent, owned, and deliverable. The Law granted a concession in salam with the description of credit (ajal), and its interpretation: the transfer of the original condition to what substitutes it, which is time, because time is suitable for acquisition which is among the causes of deliverability, so it stands as a substitute for it. And when the text transfers the condition, and it is a concession of transfer, ta'lil for annulment and nullification is not appropriate because it is pure change. And of that is their statement about the mistaken and the coerced: that their action does not break the fast due to lack of intent, like the mistaken. This ta'lil is invalid because the continuance of the fast with forgetfulness is not due to lack of intent; because the loss of the pillar nullifies performance, and lack of intent has no effect on existence with the reality of non-existence. Do you not see that one who did not intend the fast at all because he was unaware of Ramadan is not fasting, and intent was absent, yet it was not made a breaker of the fast by the text, and it is not based on 'illa as we said. And upon this principle, the action of the forgetful person is nullified because forgetfulness is something innate to humans, so it is purely heavenly, and is attributed to the Lord of the right, so it does not become valid.
Source pages p. 577

Commentary
This passage presents a key Hanafi usul principle: the 'illa in qiyas must be transitive (muta'addi) — meaning it can be applied to similar cases beyond the original text. The author contrasts with Imam al-Shafi'i, who allows non-transitive 'illa (e.g., al-thamaniyya, or the fact that a thing is a fruit). The Hanafi argument is that the only purpose of ta'lil is to extend the ruling to new cases; if it is not transitive, it adds no new knowledge or action regarding the original text (which is already established by the text itself). Therefore, the 'illa must produce a ruling in a branch. The passage also warns against changing the original ruling through ta'lil; the 'illa must transmit the exact ruling without modification. Examples illustrate this: (1) Salam hal (forward sale with immediate delivery) is invalid because the concession for salam transfers the condition of immediate possession to deferred possession; to use ta'lil to negate the concession entirely would be a change. (2) The case of the mistaken (al-khati') and coerced (al-mukrah) in fasting: the argument that they break the fast due to lack of intent (like the forgetful person) is invalid because the continuance of fasting during forgetfulness is due to a divine grace (the act is attributed to Allah), not the absence of intent; applying such ta'lil would change the ruling. Thus, any ta'lil that does not transmit the exact ruling or that changes it is invalid.

Key Terms
التعدية (al-ta'diya): Transitivity: the property of the 'illa that allows it to extend the ruling from the original case to a new case.
الثَّمنية (al-thamaniyya): Being a fruit; used by Shafi'is as an example of a non-transitive 'illa.
التغيير (al-taghayyur): Changing the original ruling via ta'lil; considered invalid because ta'lil must transmit the exact ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why is a non-transitive 'illa invalid?
A) Because it is not based on any textual source.
B) Because it does not necessitate new knowledge or action in the original text, which is already established by the text itself.
C) Because it contradicts the consensus of scholars.
D) Because it leads to changing the ruling of the text.

Key Takeaway
For Hanafis, a valid 'illa must be transitive (extend to new cases) and must not change the original ruling; otherwise, the ta'lil is invalid.

The Principle of Ta'diya (Extension) in Qiyas: Valid Extension vs. Improper Change
Explaining ta'diya and responding to Shafi'i objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish valid ta'diya from invalid taghyir by identifying whether the branch case shares the exact illa with the original, and apply this principle to examples from usury, currency specification, expiation, tayammum, and affinity prohibition.

Core Idea
Extension in qiyas is valid only when the branch is a true analogue (nazir); otherwise it is taghyir—a change that alters the original ruling.

In Hanafi usul, qiyas requires that the branch case (far') be a perfect analogue (nazir) to the original case (asl) in terms of the effective cause (illa). If the far' is not a true analogue, extending the ruling is not valid ta'diya but rather taghyir—a change that distorts the original ruling. This lesson explores several disputes with Imam al-Shafi'i where he argues for broader extension, while the Hanafis insist on strict equivalence, using examples from ibadat and muamalat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi principle
The core principle: ta'diya is valid only when the branch is a true analogue (nazir); otherwise it is taghyir. Read the opening statement: 'We have said to a branch that is its analogue, but when it opposes it, then no.'
This sets the foundational rule for the entire discussion.
2
Analyze the first example (usury)
The text's ruling on riba is bounded (mutanahi); Shafi'i extends it to unbounded items. Identify why this is taghyir: the illa in the original is not present in the branch.
Hanafis maintain that the prohibition of riba is limited to specific items with measure.
3
Examine the currency specification dispute
Hanafis argue that specifying currency in a contract changes the nature of a debt into a specific good, altering the ruling. The original ruling for prices (athman) treats them as debts, not specific items.
Specifying currency turns a debt into a specific object—a change in legal nature.
4
Understand the expiation and zihar examples
Shafi'i's condition of faith in expiation restricts an absolute text; his validation of zihar from a dhimmi extends a bounded prohibition to unbounded. Both are taghyir because the illa is not equivalent.
These examples show how restricting or expanding a ruling without proper illa constitutes change.
5
Analyze the tayammum and affinity examples
Tayammum is soiling (talwith), wudu is purification—not analogues. For affinity, the origin is the child, not intercourse; extension to haram intercourse is valid because the illa (creation from two waters) is present in both.
The Hanafi rebuttal on affinity demonstrates correct use of illa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لضمان حقه، فالتعدية إلى الخطأ وهو تقصير من الخاطئ، أو إلى المكرَه وهو من جهة غير صاحب الحق من وجه يكون تغييراً، لا تعدية. ومن ذلك : أن حكم النص في الربا تحريم مُتَنَاه، وقد أثبته الخصم فيما لا معيار له غير متناه.
ومن ذلك : قولهم في تعيين النقود في المعاوضات: إنه تصرف حصل من أهله، مضافاً إلى مَحَلَّه، مفيداً في نفسه، فيصح، كتعيين السلع. وقلنا : هذا تغيير لحكم الأصل ؛ لأن حكم الشرع في الأعيان: أن حكم البيع يتعلق به وجوب ملكها لا وجودها، وحكم البيع في جانب الأثمان وجودها ووجوبها معاً ، بدلالة ثبوتها في الذمة ديونا بلا ضرورة، وبدلالة جواز الاستبدال بها وهي ديون، ولم تُجعل في حكم الأعيان فيما وراء الرخصة، وبدلالة أنه لم يُجبر هذا النقص بقبض ما يُقابله، فإذا صح التعيين : انقلب الحكم شرطاً، وهذا تغيير محض.
وقال الشافعي رحمه الله في كفارة اليمين والظهار: إنه تحرير في تكفير، فكان الإيمان من شرطه وهذا تغيير بقيد الإطلاق، مثل إطلاق المقيد، هذا وما أشبهه تغيير للحكم في الفرع. وقد صح ظهار الذمي عند الشافعي رحمه الله، فصار تغييراً للحرمة المتناهية بالكفارة في الأصل إلى إطلاقها في الفرع عن الغاية.
* ومن ذلك : ما قلنا إلى فرع هو نظيره فأما إذا خالفه: فلا، وذلك مثل ما قلنا في تعدية الحكم من الناسي في الفطر إلى الخاطىء والمكره : إن ذلك ثبت منَّةٌ ، والعذر في الخاطىء والمكره : دون العذر في الناسي، فصار تعديةً إلى ما ليس بنظيره.
ویر وعدي 19 حكم التيمم إلى الوضوء في شرط النية وليس بنظيره؛ لأن التيمم تلويث، وهذا تطهير وغَسل.
وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : أنتم عَديتم حرمة المصاهرة من الحلال إلى الحرام، وليس بنظيره في إثبات الكرامة.
فقلنا : ما عَدَّينا من الحلال إلى الحرام؛ لأن الوطء ليس بأصل في التحريم، حلالاً كان أو حراماً، وإنما الأصل هو الولد المستحق لكرامات البشر، فلما خُلق من الماءين تعدى إليهما الحرمات، كأنهما صارا شخصاً واحداً، فصار آباؤه وأبناؤه كآبائها ، وأبنائها، وأمهاتها وبناتها : مثل أمهاته وبناته.
ثم تعدى ذلك إلى سببه، وهو الوطء، فصار عاملاً بمعنى الأصل، فلم يجز تخصيصه لمعنى في نفسه، وهو الحل، ولا إبطال الحكم لمعنى في نفسه، وهو الحرمة.
وصار هذا مثل قولنا في الغصب إنه من أسباب الملك تبعاً لوجوب ضمان الغصب لا أصلاً ، فثبت بشروط الأصل، فكان هذا الأصل مُجمعاً عليه في الحرمات التي بنيت على الاحتياط.
فأما النسب: فما بني على مثله من الاحتياط، فوجب قطعه عند الاشتباه، ولا يلزم على هذا أن هذه الحرمة لا تتعدى إلى الإخوة والأخوات ونحوهم ؛ لأن التعليل لا يعمل في تغيير الأصول، التحريم، وهذا مما تكثر أمثلته، ولا تُحصى. وهو امتداد
For the guarantee of his right, so the extension to error which is a shortcoming from the mistaken, or to the coerced which is from a direction other than the right-holder from one aspect, becomes a change, not an extension. And from that: that the ruling of the text on usury is a bounded prohibition, and the opponent has established it in what has no measure unbounded. And from that: their saying regarding specifying currency in exchanges: it is a transaction occurring from its people, attributed to its place, beneficial in itself, so it is valid, like specifying goods. And we said: this is a change to the ruling of the original; because the ruling of the Law regarding physical properties is that the ruling of sale attaches to them as obligation of ownership not existence, and the ruling of sale regarding prices is existence and obligation together, by indication of their being established as debts in the dhimmah without necessity, and by indication of the permissibility of substituting them while they are debts, and they are not made in the ruling of physical properties beyond concession, and by indication that this deficiency is not compensated by receiving its counterpart. So if specification is valid, the ruling is turned into a condition, and this is a pure change. And al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, said regarding the expiation of oath and zihar: it is an emancipation in expiation, so faith was from its condition, and this is a change by the restriction of absoluteness, like the absoluteness of the restricted. This and similar are changes to the ruling in the branch. And the zihar of a dhimmi has been valid according to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, so it becomes a change of the bounded prohibition by expiation in the original to its absoluteness in the branch without end. * And from that: we have said to a branch that is its analogue, but when it opposes it, then no. And that is like what we said regarding extending the ruling from the forgetful in breaking fast to the mistaken and coerced: that it is established as a favor, and the excuse in the mistaken and coerced is less than the excuse in the forgetful, so it becomes an extension to what is not its analogue. And the ruling of tayammum is extended to wudu in the condition of intention, and it is not its analogue; because tayammum is soiling, and this is purification and washing. And al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, said: You have extended the prohibition of affinity from the permissible to the forbidden, and it is not its analogue in establishing honor. So we said: We did not extend from permissible to forbidden; because intercourse is not an origin in prohibition, whether permitted or forbidden. Rather the origin is the child deserving human honors. When he was created from the two waters, prohibitions extended to them, as if they became one person. So his fathers and his sons become like her fathers and her sons, and her mothers and her daughters like his mothers and his daughters. Then this extended to its cause, which is intercourse, so it became operative in the sense of the origin. So it is not permissible to specify it for a meaning in itself, which is permissible, nor to invalidate the ruling for a meaning in itself, which is prohibition. And this became like our saying regarding usurpation: it is among the causes of ownership, as a consequence of the obligation of the guarantee of usurpation, not as an origin. So it is established with the conditions of the origin. So this origin is agreed upon in the prohibitions that are based on precaution. As for lineage, it is not built on similar precaution, so it must be cut off in cases of ambiguity. And it does not follow that this prohibition does not extend to brothers, sisters, and the like, because reasoning does not operate in changing the origins [of prohibition]. And this is a topic with many examples, uncountable. It is an extension.
Source pages p. 579

Commentary
This passage discusses the usul principle of ta'diya (extension) in qiyas. The author begins by clarifying that extending a ruling to a case that is not a true analogue (nazir) constitutes taghyir (change) rather than legitimate extension. Several examples illustrate this. First, in usury (riba): the textual prohibition is bounded (mutanahi), but the opponent (Shafi'i) extends it to items without standard measure (unbounded). This is invalid. Second, specifying currency in contracts: the opponent argues it is a valid transaction like specifying goods. The Hanafi response is that this changes the ruling of the original because the ruling for physical objects (a'yan) differs from that for prices (athman); prices are debts and substitution is allowed; specifying them turns a debt into a specific object, altering the legal nature. Third, al-Shafi'i's example of expiation for oath and zihar: he treats emancipation as conditional on faith, but the Hanafis say this restricts an absolute text—a change. Also, Shafi'i validates zihar from a dhimmi, extending a bounded prohibition (only with expiation) to an unbounded one. Then the passage addresses extension from a forgetful person to a mistaken or coerced person: the excuse in the latter is weaker, so analogy is false. Similarly, extending the condition of intention from tayammum to wudu is invalid because tayammum is soiling (talwith) while wudu is purification. A major objection from al-Shafi'i concerns prohibition of affinity (tahrim al-musahara). He says the Hanafis extended prohibition from permissible intercourse (halal) to forbidden (haram), which is not analogous. The Hanafi rebuttal: the origin is not intercourse but the child; prohibitions extend from the child to the two waters (sperm and egg) and then to the act of intercourse. This is like usurpation (ghasb) being a cause of ownership indirectly. The principle: prohibitions are built on precaution (ihtiyat), so extension is broad. Lineage, however, is not built on such precaution, so ambiguity leads to cutting it off. The passage ends noting that reasoning ('illa) does not operate to change established origins.

Key Terms
التعدية (al-ta'diya): Extension of a ruling from an original case to a branch case through qiyas.
النظير (al-nazir): The analogue - a branch case that shares the same effective cause (illa) as the original.
التغيير (al-taghyir): Change - when a ruling is applied to a non-analogous case, altering the original legal principle.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why is extending the prohibition of affinity (musahara) from permissible intercourse to forbidden intercourse valid?
A) Because all forms of intercourse are the same in causing prohibition.
B) Because the origin of the prohibition is the child, not intercourse, and the child is created from both the halal and haram act.
C) Because al-Shafi'i agreed with it.
D) Because the ruling is based on precaution, so it applies broadly.

Key Takeaway
Ta'diya in qiyas requires exact equivalence of the illa; extension to a non-analogue is taghyir (invalid change), as demonstrated by the Hanafi responses to Shafi'i examples on usury, currency, expiation, tayammum, and affinity.
