Redefining the Original: Hanafi Rebuttal of Shafi'i Objection to Qiyas on Mahram Prohibition
Hanafi rebuttal of Shafi'i objection to qiyas in mahram prohibition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Shafi'i objection to qiyas on mahram prohibition and apply the Hanafi rebuttal by correctly redefining the original cause (asl) from intercourse to the child.

Core Idea
The qiyas is not from halal to haram but from the child (the true original) to intercourse (its means).

In this lesson, we examine a classical usul dispute between Hanafis and Shafi'is over the validity of qiyas when extending mahram prohibition from permissible intercourse to prohibited intercourse. The Shafi'i objection is that such an extension is invalid because the analog (haram intercourse) is not similar to the original (halal). The Hanafi response clarifies that the true original is the child, not the act of intercourse, and thus the extension is valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Shafi'i objection
State the objection: 'You extended the prohibition of marriage by affinity from the permissible to the prohibited, and it is not an analog in establishing honor.' Understand the underlying claim: qiyas cannot extend a ruling from a permissible (halal) to a prohibited (haram) because the analog lacks similarity in honor.
2
Recognize the Hanafi redefinition of the original
Pinpoint the Hanafi response: 'We did not extend from permissible to prohibited because intercourse is not the original cause of prohibition... the original is the child.' Grasp how the Hanafis shift the asl from the act of intercourse to the child produced, which is entitled to honor.
3
Trace the extension from child to intercourse
Follow the logic: Prohibition first attaches to the child (as if the spouses become one person, affecting each other's relatives). Then it extends to the cause (intercourse) because intercourse is the means to the child. Thus, the extension is from the child to its cause, not from halal to haram.
4
Apply the analogy of usurpation (ghasb)
Consider the Hanafi example: In usurpation, liability for guarantee causes ownership as a subsidiary, not as an original cause. Similarly, intercourse causes prohibition as a subsidiary to the child. This shows that subsidiary causes follow the original.
5
Distinguish between lineage and affinity
Note the text's distinction: Lineage (nasab) is not built on precaution, so it is severed in doubt. Affinity (musahara) is built on precaution, so it is extended. Understand how this supports the Hanafi position that extension is appropriate for mahram.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصار تعديةً إلى ما ليس بنظيره. حكم التيمم إلى الوضوء في شرط النية وليس بنظيره؛ لأن التيمم تلويث، وهذا تطهير وغَسل. وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : أنتم عَديتم حرمة المصاهرة من الحلال إلى الحرام، وليس بنظيره في إثبات الكرامة. فقلنا : ما عَدَّينا من الحلال إلى الحرام؛ لأن الوطء ليس بأصل في التحريم، حلالاً كان أو حراماً، وإنما الأصل هو الولد المستحق لكرامات البشر، فلما خُلق من الماءين تعدى إليهما الحرمات، كأنهما صارا شخصاً واحداً، فصار آباؤه وأبناؤه كآبائها ، وأبنائها، وأمهاتها وبناتها : مثل أمهاته وبناته. ثم تعدى ذلك إلى سببه، وهو الوطء، فصار عاملاً بمعنى الأصل، فلم يجز تخصيصه لمعنى في نفسه، وهو الحل، ولا إبطال الحكم لمعنى في نفسه، وهو الحرمة. وصار هذا مثل قولنا في الغصب إنه من أسباب الملك تبعاً لوجوب : ضمان الغصب لا أصلاً ، فثبت بشروط الأصل، فكان هذا الأصل مُجمعاً عليه في الحرمات التي بنيت على الاحتياط. فأما النسب: فما بني على مثله من الاحتياط، فوجب قطعه عند الاشتباه، ولا يلزم على هذا أن هذه الحرمة لا تتعدى إلى الإخوة والأخوات ونحوهم ؛ لأن التعليل لا يعمل في تغيير الأصول، التحريم، وهذا مما تكثر أمثلته، ولا تُحصى.
Thus it became an extension to something not its analog. The ruling of tayammum to wudu regarding the condition of intention is not an analog because tayammum is soiling while this is purification and washing. Al-Shafi'i said: You extended the prohibition of marriage by affinity from the permissible to the prohibited, and it is not an analog in establishing honor. We said: We did not extend from permissible to prohibited because intercourse is not the original cause of prohibition, whether permissible or prohibited; rather the origin is the child who deserves human honors. When it is created from the two fluids, prohibition extends to them as if they became one person, so his fathers and sons become like her fathers, her sons, her mothers and her daughters like his mothers and daughters. Then that extended to its cause, which is intercourse, so it became operative with the meaning of the origin, so it is not permissible to specify it for a meaning in itself (permissibility) nor to nullify the ruling for a meaning in itself (prohibition). And this became like our statement on usurpation that it is a cause of ownership as a consequence of the obligation of guarantee, not originally, so it is established by the conditions of the origin, and this origin is agreed upon in prohibitions built on precaution. As for lineage, it is not built on such precaution, so it must be severed in case of doubt. It does not follow from this that this prohibition does not extend to brothers, sisters and the like because causation does not operate to change the origins of prohibition. And this has many examples that cannot be enumerated.
Source pages p. 580

Commentary
This passage presents a Hanafi defense of qiyas (analogical reasoning) against a Shafi'i objection. The issue is the extension of prohibition of marriage due to affinity (musahara) from permissible (halal) intercourse to prohibited (haram) intercourse. Al-Shafi'i argued that the Hanafis extended a ruling from a permissible original to a prohibited analog, which is invalid because the analog is not similar. The Hanafi response clarifies that the original cause is not the intercourse itself but the child (walad) created from the fluids of both spouses. The child, being entitled to honor, causes the prohibition to extend to the spouses' relatives as if they are one person. Then the prohibition extends to the cause (intercourse) because it is the means to that original. Thus, the extension is not from halal to haram but from the child (the true origin) to intercourse (its means). The Hanafis liken this to their rule on usurpation (ghasb) where liability for guarantee causes ownership; it is not an original cause but a subsidiary one. This shows that qiyas is valid when the analogy is properly identified. The passage also distinguishes between lineage (nasab) and affinity: lineage is not built on precaution and is severed in doubt, but affinity is built on precaution and thus extended.

Key Terms
تعدية (ta'diyah): Extension of a ruling from an original (asl) to a new case (far') via qiyas.
نظير (nazir): A proper analog; a case that shares the effective cause ('illah) with the original.
مصاهرة (musahara): Marriage affinity; the relationship created between a spouse and the relatives of the other spouse.
أصل (asl): The original case or cause that bears a ruling and serves as the basis for analogy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi argument, what is the actual original cause (asl) for extending mahram prohibition?
A) The intercourse itself
B) The child created from the two fluids
C) The marriage contract
D) The honor of the husband

Key Takeaway
The validity of qiyas depends on correctly identifying the original cause (asl); here, the child, not the intercourse, is the true basis for mahram prohibition.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

Conditions for Valid Qiyas: Textual Conformity and Preservation
Stating two conditions for valid qiyas with critique of Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi conditions that qiyas must neither contradict nor duplicate a nass and must preserve the original ruling, and identify violations in Shafi'i's examples.

Core Idea
Qiyas cannot contradict a text; if it agrees, it is idle; it must preserve the original ruling unchanged.

This lesson examines two critical conditions for valid analogical reasoning (qiyas) according to the Hanafi school. The third condition ensures that qiyas does not oppose a clear text (nass) nor merely repeat it, while the fourth condition requires that the original ruling of the text remain unchanged. Through examples from al-Shafi'i's rulings, the author demonstrates how violating these conditions leads to invalid reasoning. We'll explore these conditions and their practical application in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
The passage presents two conditions: (3) qiyas must not contradict a nass, nor be redundant; (4) qiyas must preserve the original ruling of the nass unchanged.
These follow two earlier conditions (not in this passage) about the istinbat of the 'illah.
2
Understand the reasoning behind each condition
Contradicting a nass voids the text's ruling; agreeing with it makes the qiyas superfluous. Changing the original ruling by qiyas is arbitrary and invalid.
The author calls agreement 'idle talk' (لغو من الكلام).
3
Examine the first set of examples
Shafi'i's analogies (zakat to sadaqah, tamlik in expiations, faith conditions) are criticized as either contradicting or altering the text's ruling.
These examples illustrate the third and fourth conditions.
4
Focus on the example of tamlik in expiation food
The Hanafi argue that 'it'am' (feeding) literally means the act of eating, not ownership transfer, so requiring tamlik changes the text's ruling.
This is a key example of the fourth condition violation.
5
See the broader pattern
Shafi'i's qiyas often adds restrictions (e.g., faith in recipients, tamlik, invalidation of testimony) that are not in the text, violating the fourth condition.
The author says 'and similar examples are many.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن ذلك قولنا ولا نصَّ فيه ؛ لأن التعدية إليه بمخالفة النص: مناقضة حكم النص بالتعليل، وهو باطل، والتعديةُ بموا افقة النص : لغو من الكلام ؛ لأن النص يُغني عن التعليل. ومثال ذلك: قول الشافعي رحمه الله في كفارة القتل العمد، واليمين الغموس، وشرط الإيمان في مصرف الصدقات؛ اعتباراً بالزكاة. ومثل شرط التمليك في طعام الكفارات، وشرط الإيمان في رقبة كفارة اليمين والظهار، وهذا كله تعدية إلى ما فيه نص بتغييره بالتقييد. وأما الشرط الرابع وهو أن يبقى حكم النص على ما كان قبل التعليل؛ فلأن تغيير حكم النص في نفسه بالرأي باطل، كما أبطلناه في الفروع. وذلك مثل قول الشافعي رحمه الله في طعام الكفارة بشرط التمليك : وإنه تغيير لحكم النص بعينه ؛ لأن الإطعام اسم لفعل يسمى لازمه طعماً ، وهو الأكل على ما قلنا. ومثل قوله في حد القذف : إنه لا يُبطل الشهادة، وهذا تغيير؛ لأن النص يوجب أن يكون حكم القذف إبطال الشهادة حداً، وقد أبطله، فجَعَلَ بعض الحد حداً؛ لأن الوقت من الأبد بعضه. وأثبت الردَّ بنفس القذف دون مدة العجز، وهو تغيير. وزاد النفي على الجلد وهو تغيير . وجعل الفسق مبطلاً للشهادة والولاية وهو تغيير؛ لأن حكم الفسق بالنص التثبتُ والتوقف، دون الإبطال، ومثله كثير.
And from that is our statement: and there is no text in it. Because the extension to it by contradicting the text is a contradiction of the ruling of the text by causation, which is void, and the extension by agreeing with the text is idle talk because the text suffices for causation. An example of that is the statement of al-Shafi'i regarding the expiation for intentional killing, the false oath, and the condition of faith in the distribution of charity, by analogy with zakat. And like the condition of possession (tamlik) in the food of expiations, and the condition of faith in the slave of expiation of oath and zihar. All this is an extension to something that has a text, changing it by restriction. As for the fourth condition: that the ruling of the text should remain as it was before causation, because changing the ruling of the text itself by opinion is void, as we declared void in the branches. That is like al-Shafi'i's statement regarding the food of expiation with the condition of possession: it is a change of the ruling of the text itself, because 'feeding' (it'am) is the name of an act whose necessary concomitant is eating, as we said. And like his statement regarding the hadd penalty for slander: it does not invalidate testimony. This is a change because the text requires that the ruling of slander be invalidation of testimony as a hadd, and he invalidated it, making part of the hadd the hadd, because time is part of eternity. And he established rejection by the slander itself without the period of incapacity, which is a change. And he added exile to flogging, which is a change. And he made sinfulness (fisq) invalidator of testimony and guardianship, which is a change, because the ruling for sinfulness according to the text is verification and suspension, not invalidation. And similar examples are many.
Source pages p. 581

Commentary
This passage presents two conditions for valid qiyas from the Hanafi perspective. The third condition: the qiyas must not contradict a text (nass) nor merely repeat it. If it contradicts, it voids the text; if it merely agrees, it is superfluous because the text is sufficient. The fourth condition: the qiyas must not change the original ruling itself; it can only extend it to new cases. The author provides several examples where al-Shafi'i allegedly violated these conditions: (1) Requiring faith in recipients of charity by analogy with zakat, but the text of the Quran (Tawbah 60) does not specify faith. (2) Requiring possession of food in expiations by analogy, but the text uses 'it'am' which means feeding, not ownership. (3) Ruling that slander does not invalidate testimony, while the text (Qadhf) requires invalidation as part of the hadd. (4) Adding exile to flogging for zina, though the text only mentions flogging. (5) Making sinfulness an absolute invalidator of testimony, while the text says to investigate (tathabbut) and suspend judgment. These examples illustrate that qiyas must not alter the text's own ruling.

Key Terms
نص (nass): A clear textual ruling from Quran or Sunnah that does not require interpretation.
تعليل (ta'lil): The process of identifying the effective cause ('illah) for analogical reasoning.
تمليك (tamlik): The condition of transferring ownership, as opposed to mere permission to use.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of violating the fourth condition (that the text's ruling must remain as before)?
A) Requiring faith in charity recipients
B) Requiring possession of food in expiation
C) Making sinfulness an absolute invalidator of testimony
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
Qiyas must neither contradict nor duplicate a text; it must preserve the original ruling unchanged.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate

Takbir as Means: Qiyas and Limits in Hanafi Usul
Clarifying that takbir is wajib as a means of glorification, not in itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi usul principle that takbir is wajib as a means (āla) not in itself, apply qiyas to extend the ruling to other forms of praise, and identify when qiyas is invalid due to the revealed nature of the ruling (as with hadath).

Core Idea
Takbir is a means to glorification; by qiyas, any praise suffices, but hadath removal is revealed, not analogized.

This passage deepens your understanding of how the Hanafi school uses qiyas to extend rulings while respecting the revealed (tawqifi) nature of certain obligations. The author argues that takbir is not intrinsically wajib; rather, the wajib is glorification, and takbir is one instrument. By analogy, any form of praise (tasbih, tahmid, etc.) serves the same purpose. However, a crucial exception is made for the removal of hadath (ritual impurity), which is confined to water by divine decree, not by rational analogy. This lesson will walk you through the derivation chain and the boundaries of qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Claim
State that the text claims takbir is not wajib in itself (li-‘aynihi) but as an instrument (āla) for the obligation of glorifying Allah. Also note the parallel claim about water: water is not wajib in itself for purification; rather, removal of impurity is wajib, and water is just one instrument.
The key term is āla—an instrument that is not the ultimate end.
2
Trace the Istinbat Chain for Takbir
Identify the underlying textual ruling: the command to glorify Allah (ta‘ẓīm) is general, and takbir is specified as a suitable praise. The author then applies takhṣīṣ (specification) to limit the command to the act of glorification, not the specific utterance. Then perform qiyās: since takbir is absolute praise (thana’ muṭlaq), any other praise shares the same ‘illa (effective cause) of being praise suitable for glorification, so the ruling extends.
The ‘illa is the suitability for glorification, not the specific words.
3
Analyze the Water Analogy and its Exception
Understand the qiyās from water to other purifying substances: the obligation is removal of impurity, water is an instrument, so other instruments (e.g., vinegar, milk?) can also serve. However, the author then stops the analogy for hadath (ritual impurity). Why? Because the effect of water on hadath is a revealed Shar‘ī matter (ḥukm shar‘ī), not rationally understood, and other liquids are generally impure and cause hardship.
Qiyās fails when the original ruling is based on non-rational grounds (tawqīf).
4
Apply the Distinction to Your Understanding
Summarize: The Hanafi school permits qiyās when the ‘illa is rationally perceptible and the original ruling is not exclusive by divine decree. For takbir and other praises, the ‘illa (suitability for glorification) is clear, so extension is valid. For hadath, the ruling is tawqīfī (revealed), so qiyās is blocked.
This distinction is fundamental in Hanafi uṣūl between mu‘qal al-ma‘nā (rationally understood) and ta‘abbudī (worship-based, not reasoned).
5
Reflect on Cross-Madhhab Difference
Implicitly, the Shāfi‘ī school might view takbir as a specific wajib utterance, not extendable to other praises. Contrast the Hanafi position: they treat the command as general and extend via qiyās because the instrument is not the end.
This illustrates how different uṣūl assumptions lead to different furū‘.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما التكبير فما وجب لعينه، بل الواجب تعظيم الله تعالى بكل جزء من البَدَن، واللسان منه ؛ لأنها من ظاهر البدن من وجه، فوجب فعلها، والثناء آلة فعلها، فصار حكم النص أن يجعل التكبير آلة فعلها ؛ لكونه ثناءً مطلقاً، فعديناه إلى سائر الأثنية مع بقاء حكم النص، وهو كون التكبير ثناء صالحاً للتعظيم. وإنما ادعينا هذا، دون أن يكون التكبيرُ بعينه واجباً؛ لأنا وجدنا سائر الأركان أفعالاً توجد من البدن؛ ليصير البدن فاعلاً، فكذلك اللسان. وكذلك استعمال الماء ليس بواجب بعينه ؛ لأن مَن ألقى الثوب النجس: سقط عنه استعمال الماء، لكن الواجب إزالة العين، والماء آلة، فإذا عدينا حكمه إلى سائر ما يصلح آلةً فقد بقي حكم النص بعينه، وهو كون الماء آلة صالحةً للتطهير، وهو حكم شرعي، وهو أن الماء لا ينجس حال الاستعمال، هذا حكم شرعي في المزيل والطهارة في محل العمل فعديناه إلى نظيره. ولا يلزم أن الحدث لا يزول بسائر المائعات؛ لأن عمل الماء لا يثبت في محل الحدث إلا بإثبات المزال، وذلك أمر شرعي ثبت في محل الغَسل، غير معقول عند استعمال الماء الذي يوجد مباحاً، لا يُبالي بخبثه، ولم يستقم إثباته في أوان استعمال سائر المائعات بالرأي، وهو مما لا يُعقل، مع أن سائر المائعات يلحقنا الحرج بخبثها؛ لأنها أموال لا مباحة غالباً.
As for takbir, it is not obligated in itself; rather, the obligation is to glorify Allah with every part of the body, and the tongue is one of them, because it is from the apparent body in one sense. So its action is obligatory, and praise is the instrument (āla) of that action. Thus the ruling of the text makes takbir an instrument of that action, because it is absolute praise. So we extend it to all other forms of praise while preserving the ruling of the text, which is that takbir is praise suitable for glorification. We claim this rather than takbir itself being obligatory because we find that all other pillars are actions performed by the body to make the body an agent; likewise the tongue. Similarly, the use of water is not inherently obligatory, because if someone throws away a najis garment, the use of water is dropped; rather, the obligation is removal of the substance, and water is an instrument. So if we extend its ruling to everything else that can serve as an instrument, the ruling of the text remains as it is: that water is an instrument suitable for purification, and this is a Shariah ruling—that water does not become impure during use. This is a Shariah ruling concerning the remover and purification at the place of action; so we extend it to its analogue. It does not follow that ritual impurity (hadath) is not removed by other liquids, because the effect of water in the place of hadath is only established by establishing the removed substance, and that is a Shariah matter established in the place of washing, not rationally understood when using water that is available as mubah, not cared about for its impurity. Its establishment during the use of other liquids by opinion is not viable, and it is not rationally understood, especially since other liquids cause us hardship due to their impurity because they are generally not mubah property.
Source pages p. 586

Commentary
This passage is an istinbat (derivation) and application of qiyas. The author argues that takbir in prayer is not wajib in itself (as a specific utterance), but rather the wajib is absolute glorification of Allah using any part of the body, including the tongue. Takbir is a means (āla) of that glorification. Since the textual ruling (from hadith or Quranic command) makes takbir a suitable instrument, we extend by analogy (qiyas) to all other forms of praise (like tasbih, tahmid, etc.) because they share the same 'illa of being praise suitable for glorification. The author then draws a parallel with water in purification: using water is not wajib in itself; the wajib is removing impurity, and water is one instrument. So we extend the ruling that water is a valid instrument to any substance that can serve as a purifier. However, there is an exception: ritual impurity (hadath) is only removable by water, not other liquids, because the effect of water on hadath is a revealed (Shariah) matter not subject to rational analogy, and other liquids are generally impure and not mubah (freely available), leading to hardship.

Key Terms
التكبير (al-takbir): Saying 'Allahu Akbar' during prayer.
آلة (āla): An instrument or means to achieve the obligatory act.
الحدث (al-hadath): Ritual impurity that necessitates wudu or ghusl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that other liquids cannot remove hadath despite the analogy to water?
A) Because other liquids are not pure.
B) Because the effect of water in removing hadath is a revealed Shariah matter not subject to rational analogy.
C) Because water is the only liquid that can purify.
D) Because the text explicitly forbids other liquids.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, takbir is wajib as a means (āla) not in itself, and qiyās is valid when the cause is rational but invalid when the ruling is ta‘abbudī, as with hadath removal.

The Pillar of Qiyas: Definition and Types
Defining the pillar of qiyas with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the rukn al-qiyas, identify its three types (inherent, transient, ruling), and distinguish the Hanafi view on the 'illah for riba from the Shafi'i view.

Core Idea
The rukn of qiyas is the common attribute that serves as the sign for the ruling, and it can be inherent, transient, or even a ruling itself.

This passage from Kanz al-Wusul defines the essential pillar (rukn) of qiyas. Understanding this definition is crucial for constructing valid analogies. The author explains that the rukn is the attribute that becomes the sign ('alam) for the original ruling, and it can take various forms—inherent descriptions, transient attributes, proper names, or even a ruling. We will examine examples from hadith and a key dispute with the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the definition
Commit to memory: 'Rukn al-qiyas is that which is made a sign of the ruling of the text, and the branch is made analogous due to its presence.'
This is the axiomatic starting point.
2
Identify the three categories
From the examples, note that the rukn can be an inherent description (wasf lazim), a transient description/name (wasf 'arid/ism), or a ruling (hukm).
Each type is illustrated by a specific example in the text.
3
Analyze each example
For each example (thimaniyyah for zakat, ta'm for riba by Shafi'i, istihadah hadith, debt hadith), identify what type of attribute it represents and how it functions as the 'illah.
Pay attention to the phrase 'جائز أن يكون' to understand permissibility.
4
Note the madhhab difference
Compare the Hanafi use of measurement (kayl) for riba with the Shafi'i use of taste (ta'm). Understand that this difference stems from the flexibility allowed in the Hanafi definition of the rukn.
This ikhtilaf has practical consequences in fiqh.
5
Apply the concept
Given a new attribute, determine whether it qualifies as a rukn based on the categories taught. Practice with scenarios like using 'weight' or 'colour' as causes.
Consider whether the attribute is inherent, transient, or a ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الركن [في القياس] ركن القياس : ما جُعل عَلَماً على حكم النص مما اشتمل عليه النص وجعل الفرع نظيراً له في حكمه بوجوده فيه. وهو جائز أن يكون وصفاً لازماً. مثل الثمنية جعلناها علةً للزكاة في الحلي، والطَّعم جعله الشافعي رحمه الله علةً للربا، ووصفاً عارضاً واسماً. كقول النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام في المستحاضة : إنه دمُ عِرْقٍ انفجر، وهو اسمٌ عَلَمٌ، و انفجر: صفة عارضة، غير لازمة. وعللنا بالكيل، وهو غير لازم، ويكون جلياً وخفياً، ويجوز أن يكون حكماً، كقول النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام في التي سألته عن الحج: «أرايتِ لو كان على أبيك دَيْن»، وهذا حكم.
Chapter of the Pillar [of Qiyas]. The pillar of qiyas: that which is made a sign of the ruling of the text from what the text contains, and the branch is made analogous to it in its ruling due to its presence in it. And it is permissible that it be an inherent description. Like 'price' we made it the cause for zakat on jewelry, and 'taste' Imam al-Shafi'i made it a cause for riba. And a transient description and a name. Like the saying of the Prophet ﷺ regarding the woman with istihadah: 'It is the blood of a ruptured vein.' So it is a proper noun, and 'ruptured' is a transient description, not inherent. And we used measurement as a cause, which is not inherent. And it can be clear and hidden. And it is permissible that it be a ruling, like the saying of the Prophet ﷺ to the one who asked about Hajj: 'Do you think if your father had a debt...' This is a ruling.
Source pages p. 588

Commentary
This passage defines the essential pillar (rukn) of qiyas (analogical reasoning) in Hanafi usul. The pillar is the common attribute that becomes a sign of the original ruling, and by finding it in the new case, the ruling is extended. The author explains that this attribute can be an inherent description (wasf lazim), such as 'price' (thimaniyyah) which we use as the 'illah for zakat on jewelry, or 'taste' (ta'm) which Imam al-Shafi'i uses for riba. It can also be a transient description (wasf 'arid) and a name (ism), as in the hadith of the woman with chronic bleeding: the phrase 'blood of a vein' is a name for a specific thing, and 'ruptured' is a transient description. Additionally, the cause can be a ruling itself, as the Prophet likened the obligation of Hajj to a debt owed by a father. The passage also notes that we (Hanafis) use measurement (kayl) as a cause for riba, which is not inherent. The attribute can be clear (jaliy) or hidden (khafiy).

Key Terms
ركن القياس (rukn al-qiyas): The pillar of qiyas: the common attribute that is made a sign of the ruling from the text, and the branch is made analogous to it in ruling due to its presence.
علة ('illah): The effective cause that justifies extending the ruling. The text uses it interchangeably with the attribute.
وصف لازم (wasf lazim): An inherent attribute that is inseparable from the entity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following can serve as the pillar (rukn) of qiyas?
A) Only inherent descriptions
B) Only transient descriptions
C) Inherent descriptions, transient descriptions, and rulings
D) Only rulings

Key Takeaway
The rukn of qiyas is flexible: it can be an inherent description, a transient description, a name, or even a ruling itself, and Hanafis use this flexibility to derive rulings.

The Pillar of Qiyas: Types of 'Illah in Hanafi Usul
Defining the pillar of analogy and describing types of cause

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the rukn of qiyas and list the permissible types of 'illah according to Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
The 'illah in qiyas can be necessary, accidental, a ruling, or a number—its validity is known by its effect, not its type.

In this passage, Imam al-Bazdawi defines the pillar (rukn) of qiyas and describes the various kinds of 'illah (cause) that may serve as that pillar. Understanding this flexibility is key to grasping Hanafi legal reasoning, where the cause is not limited to intrinsic attributes but can include rulings and other characteristics.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of rukn al-qiyas
The rukn is the attribute made a sign of the original ruling and then found in the branch. This is the core of analogical reasoning.
2
Identify the types of 'illah mentioned
The passage lists necessary descriptions, accidental descriptions, rulings, individuals/numbers, and causes outside the text but established by it. Each type is illustrated with examples.
3
Recognize the unifying principle
All types are equal because the validity of the 'illah is known by its effect (athar). This refutes the idea that some causes are inherently more valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الركن في القياس] ركن القياس : ما جُعل عَلَماً على حكم النص مما اشتمل عليه النص وجعل الفرع نظيراً له في حكمه بوجوده فيه. وهو جائز أن يكون وصفاً لازماً. مثل الثمنية جعلناها علةً للزكاة في الحلي، والطَّعم جعله الشافعي رحمه الله علةً للربا، ووصفاً عارضاً واسماً. كقول النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام في المستحاضة : إنه دمُ عِرْقٍ انفجر»(۱) ، وهو اسمٌ عَلَمٌ، و انفجر: صفة عارضة، غير لازمة. وعللنا بالكيل، وهو غير لازم، ويكون جلياً وخفياً، ويجوز أن يكون حكماً، كقول النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام في التي سألته عن الحج: «أرايتِ لو كان على أبيك دَيْنُ (۳) ، وهذا حكم. وكقولنا في المدبّر : إنه مملوك تعلّق عتقه بمطلق موت المولى، وهذا حكم أيضاً. ويجوز أن يكون فرداً وعدداً، كما في باب الربا. ويجوز أن يكون في النص، وهذا لا يُشكل. ويجوز في غيره إذا كان ثابتاً به كما جاء في الحديث: «أنه رخص السلم) ، وهو معلول بإعدام العاقد)، وليس في النص ذلك. والنهي عن بيع الآبق (۳) معلول بالجهالة، أو العجز عن التسليم، وليس في النص. وعلَّل الشافعي رحمه الله في نكاح الأمة على الحرة بإرقاق جزء منه، وليس في النص، ولكنه ثابت به. وإنما استوت هذه الوجوه ؛ لأن العلة إنما تُعرف صحتها بأثرها، وذلك لا يوجب الفصل.
Chapter of the pillar in analogy. The pillar of analogy is that which is made a sign of the ruling of the text from what the text contains, and the branch is made its counterpart in ruling due to its presence in it. It is permissible for it to be a necessary description. Like price/value we made it a cause for zakat on jewelry, and edibility al-Shafi'i made it a cause for riba. And an accidental description and name. Like the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him, regarding the mustahada: 'It is blood from a vessel that burst,' which is a proper noun, and 'burst' is an accidental description, not necessary. And we caused by measurement (kayl), which is not necessary, and it can be clear and hidden. And it is permissible for it to be a ruling, like the saying of the Prophet regarding the woman who asked him about Hajj: 'What do you think if your father had a debt?' and this is a ruling. And like our saying regarding the mudabbar: he is a slave whose emancipation is attached to the absolute death of the master, and this is also a ruling. And it is permissible for it to be an individual and a number, as in the chapter of riba. And it is permissible for it to be in the text, and this is not problematic. And it is permissible for it to be other than that if it is established by it, as came in the hadith: 'He permitted salam,' and it is caused by the lack of the contracting party, and that is not in the text. And the prohibition of selling the runaway slave is caused by ignorance or inability to deliver, and it is not in the text. And al-Shafi'i caused in the marriage of a slave woman over a free woman by enslaving part of him, and it is not in the text, but it is established by it. And these types are equal because the validity of the cause is only known by its effect, and that does not necessitate a distinction.
Source pages p. 588

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the pillar (rukn) of qiyas (analogy) and a discussion of the various types of 'illah (cause) that may serve as that pillar. The author, Imam al-Bazdawi, begins by defining the rukn of qiyas: it is what is made a sign ('alam) of the ruling of the original text (nas) from among the attributes contained in that text, and then the branch (far') is made analogous in ruling because that same attribute is found in it. The author then explains that the 'illah can be of several types: 1) A necessary description (wasf lazim), like price/value (thumaniyya) as the cause for zakat on jewelry, or edibility (t'am) as al-Shafi'i's cause for riba. 2) An accidental description (wasf 'arid) and a name (ism), as in the hadith about the mustahada (woman with chronic uterine bleeding): the Prophet described it as 'blood from a vessel that burst' – 'burst' (infajara) is an accidental description. 3) Measurement (kayl), which is not necessary and can be clear or hidden. 4) A ruling (hukm) itself, like the analogy from the hajj hadith: the Prophet asked 'What if your father had a debt?' which is a ruling, and also the case of the mudabbar (a slave promised freedom upon master's death) whose ruling is used as an 'illah. 5) An individual (fard) or number ('adad), as in riba. 6) The 'illah may be explicitly in the text, which is straightforward, or outside the text but established by it, as in the license for salam (forward sale) which is attributed to the need of the contracting party (i'dam al-'aqid) though not mentioned in the text; the prohibition of selling a runaway slave is attributed to ignorance or inability to deliver; and al-Shafi'i's cause for marrying a slave woman over a free woman is reducing one's freedom (irqaq). The author concludes that all these types are equal because the validity of the 'illah is known only by its effect (athar), and that does not necessitate a distinction between them. This passage is crucial for understanding the flexibility of qiyas in Hanafi usul, where the 'illah need not be a necessary attribute or confined to the text.

Key Terms
ركن القياس (rukn al-qiyas): The pillar of analogy: the attribute made a sign of the ruling of the text, by which the branch is made analogous
علة ('illah): The cause or effective attribute that justifies extending the ruling from the original to the branch
وصف لازم (wasf lazim): A necessary description inseparable from the thing, like price/value for zakat
وصف عارض (wasf 'arid): An accidental description that may change, like the bursting of a blood vessel

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a valid type of 'illah in qiyas?
A) Only necessary descriptions
B) Only textual statements
C) A ruling from another case
D) Only accidental descriptions

Key Takeaway
The validity of an 'illah is determined by its effect, not by its inherent type—Hanafi usul embraces a wide range of causes for qiyas.

Validity of 'Illah from Non-Textual Sources
Examples of 'illah derived from non-textual sources

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain how Hanafi usul validates 'illah derived from non-textual sources, and apply the principle that the validity of an 'illah is known by its effect.

Core Idea
The validity of a legal cause is determined by its effect on the ruling, not by its textual origin.

In this passage, the author presents several examples to demonstrate that a legal cause ('illah) can be established from non-textual sources, such as the nature of a contract or practical necessity. The key principle is that the validity of a cause is recognized by its effect—the ruling it produces—not by whether it is explicitly mentioned in the primary texts. This lesson will walk you through these examples and the underlying methodology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the examples of non-textual 'illah
Locate the four specific examples given: mudabbar, salam, bai' al-abiq, and Shafi'i's example of marrying a slave woman over a free woman.
The mudabbar example is based on the nature of the contract, not a text.
2
Analyze the cause assigned to each ruling
For each example, note the ruling and the cause ('illah) provided. For salam, the cause is 'lack of the contracting party'; for bai' al-abiq, it is 'ignorance or inability to deliver'; for Shafi'i's example, it is 'enslaving a part of the free woman'.
These causes are not explicitly stated in the primary texts.
3
Understand the author's conclusion
The author asserts that all these forms of cause are equivalent because the validity of a cause is known by its effect (the ruling it produces), not by its textual origin.
This underscores the Hanafi acceptance of istihsan and non-textual reasoning.
4
Compare with the Shafi'i position
Note that Imam al-Shafi'i himself used a non-textual cause in the example given, showing that even opponents accept this principle in practice.
The difference lies in methodology: Hanafi usul explicitly allows non-textual causes via istihsan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكقولنا في المدبر: إنه مملوك تعلق عتقه بمطلق موت المولى، وهذا حكم أيضاً.
ويجوز أن يكون فرداً وعدداً، كما في باب الربا.
ويجوز أن يكون في النص، وهذا لا يشكل.
ويجوز في غيره إذا كان ثابتاً به كما جاء في الحديث: "أنه رخص في السلم" وهو معلول بإعدام العاقد، وليس في النص ذلك. والنهي عن بيع الآبق معلول بالجهالة، أو العجز عن التسليم، وليس في النص.
وعلل الشافعي رحمه الله في نكاح الأمة على الحرة بإرقاق جزء منه، وليس في النص، ولكنه ثابت به.
وإنما استوت هذه الوجوه؛ لأن العلة إنما تعرف صحتها بأثرها، وذلك لا يوجب الفصل.
[ص١٣٦]، وباب تقسيم السنة [ص٥١٥].
(1) قوله: كما جاء في الحديث أنه رخص في السلم: تقدم في العزيمة والرخصة ص 336، والحديث الذي أشار إليه أخرجه الخمسة، وصححه الترمذي عن حكيم بن حزام أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال له: "لا تبع ما ليس عندك".
(2) أي بفقر العاقد، أو احتياجه. سائد.
(3) حديث النهي عن بيع الآبق: عن أبي سعيد الخدري رضي الله عنه أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم نهى عن بيع العبد وهو أبق. رواه إسحاق بن راهويه، وأخرجه ابن ماجه وابن أبي شيبة والبزار وأبو يعلى والدارقطني بلفظ: "نهى عن شراء العبد وهو آبق"، وفي سنده ضعف.
And like our statement concerning the mudabbar: that he is a slave whose emancipation is tied to the absolute death of the master, and this is also a ruling. And it is permissible for it to be singular and plural, as in the chapter on riba. And it is permissible for it to be in the text, and this is not problematic. And it is permissible in other than it if it is established by it, as came in the hadith: 'that he permitted salam' and it is caused by the lack of the contracting party, and that is not in the text. And the prohibition of selling a runaway slave is caused by ignorance or inability to deliver, and it is not in the text. And al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, gave a cause in the marriage of a slave woman over a free woman by enslaving a part of him, and it is not in the text, but it is established by it. And these aspects are equal only because the validity of the cause is known only by its effect, and that does not necessitate differentiation.
Source pages p. 589

Commentary
This passage illustrates that a legal cause ('illah) can be established from non-textual sources, not just from explicit textual evidence. The author gives several examples: First, the ruling concerning a mudabbar (a slave promised freedom upon the master's death) is based on the cause of being tied to the master's death, which is a ruling derived from the nature of the contract, not necessarily from a specific text. Second, in the chapter on riba (usury), the cause can be generic (singular) or specific (plural). Third, the cause may be directly from the text, which is straightforward. Fourth, the cause may be established from something else, like the hadith on salam (advance payment) where the cause is the lack of the contracting party (or his need), not explicitly mentioned in the text. Similarly, the prohibition of selling a runaway slave is caused by ignorance or inability to deliver, again not textually specified. Fifth, Imam al-Shafi'i's ruling on the invalidity of marrying a slave woman over a free woman is caused by 'enslaving a part' of the free woman, not found in the text but established by analogy. The key point is that all these forms of cause are equivalent because the validity of a cause is determined by its effect (the ruling it produces), not by its textual origin. This underscores the Hanafi approach that 'illah can be derived through reasoning and istihsan.

Key Terms
العلة (al-‘illah): The effective cause that necessitates a legal ruling; its validity is known by its effect.
المدبر (al-mudabbar): A slave who is promised freedom upon the death of his master.
السلم (al-salam): Advance payment sale where the price is paid upfront and delivery is deferred.
الآبق (al-abiq): A runaway slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are all the mentioned forms of cause ('illah) considered equivalent?
A) Because they are all explicitly mentioned in the Quran and Sunnah.
B) Because the validity of the cause is known by its effect, not by its textual origin.
C) Because they all involve the same attribute of ignorance.
D) Because they are all derived from analogical reasoning (qiyas).

Key Takeaway
The validity of a legal cause ('illah) is determined by its effect on the ruling, not by its textual origin, allowing non-textual causes in Hanafi usul.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

The Validity of Consistent Attributes as Legal Causes
Analysis of disagreement on whether mere consistency of a descriptive trait makes it a valid ʿilla

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi position from Ahl al-Ṭard and Shafiʿi views on the validity of consistent attributes as legal causes, and apply the Hanafi criterion of 'athar' to evaluate potential ʿillas.

Core Idea
A consistent attribute without a rationally understood effect is not a valid legal cause in Hanafi usul.

This passage from Al-Bazdawi's Kanz al-Wusul presents the foundational dispute over what makes a descriptive attribute a valid ʿilla for qiyas. The Ahl al-Ṭard argue that mere consistency suffices, while the Hanafis and many Shafiʿis demand a rationally meaningful basis. We will unpack the concepts of suitability (ṣalāḥ) and integrity (ʿadāla), focusing on the Hanafi requirement that the attribute have an established effect (athar) in the Shariʿa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main positions
Who are Ahl al-Ṭard and what is their view? Who are the 'jurist imams' and what do they require? Note the key terms: iṭṭirād, ṣalāḥ, ʿadāla.
2
Understand the witness analogy
How does the condition for a witness (suitability + integrity) map onto the attribute's conditions (ṣalāḥ + ʿadāla)? Why must both be present?
3
Distinguish definitions of integrity
How do Hanafis (athar) and Shafiʿis (mukhīl or presentation to principles) differ in defining ʿadāla? What is the practical consequence for when the attribute can be used?
4
Follow the Hanafi argument from effect
Why does the Hanafi rely on 'athar' as evidence? How is it analogous to knowing the Creator through His creation or a witness through conduct?
5
Apply the Hanafi criterion
Given a hypothetical attribute (e.g., 'intoxicating'), test whether it satisfies ṣalāḥ (consonance with transmitted causes) and ʿadāla (having an athar).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واتفقوا أن كل أوصاف النص بجملتها لا يجوز أن تكون علةً. واختلفوا في دلالة كونه علةً على قولين : فقال أهل الطرد : إنه يصير حجةً بمجرد الاطراد، من غير معنى يُعقل. وقال أئمة الفقه من السلف والخلف رحمهم الله : إنه لا حجة إلا يصير بمعنى يُعقل، وهذا المعنى هو صلاح الوصف، ثم عدالته. وذلك على مثال الشاهد، لا بدَّ من صلاحه بما يصير به منه أداء الشهادة، ثم لا يصح الأداء إلا بلفظ للشهادة، ثم عدالته؛ ليصح. واتفقوا في صلاحه أنه إنما يُراد به ملاءمته، وذلك أن يكون على موافقة ما جاء عن السلف رضي الله عنهم من العلل المنقولة؛ لأنه أمر شرعي، فيتعرف منه ولا يصح العمل به قبل الملاءمة، كما لا العمل بشهادة الشاهد قبل الأهلية، لكن لا يجب العمل به إلا بعد العدالة والعدالة عندنا الأثر، وإنما نعني بالأثر : ما جُعل له أثر في الشرع. وقال بعض أصحاب الشافعي رحمه الله : عدالته بكونه مُخيلاً، ثم العَرضُ على الأصول احتياطاً؛ لسلامته عن المناقضة والمعارضة. وقال بعض أصحاب الشافعي رحمه الله : بل عدالته بالعرض على الأصول، فإن لم يرده أصل مناقض ولا معارض صار معدلاً، وإنما يعرض على أصلين فصاعداً. فعلى القول الأول: يصح العمل به قبل العرض، وعلى الثاني : لا يصح؛ لأنه به يصير حجة. وعلى القول الأول : صار حجةً بكونه مُخيلاً، وإنما النَّقصُ جَرَحٌ، والمعارضة دفع. واحتج أهلُ المقالة الأولى: أن الأثر معنى لا يُعقل، فنقل عنه إلى شهادة القلب، وهو الخيال، وهو كالتحري جُعل حجةً بشهادة القلب عند تعذر العمل بسائر الأدلة، ثم العرض بعد ذلك؛ للاحتياط. بخلاف الشاهد؛ لأنه يُتوهم أن يعترض فيه بعد أصل الأهلية ما يُبطل الشهادة من فسق أو غيره، فأما الوصف فلا يحتمل مثله، فإذا كان ملائماً غير ناب: صار صالحاً، وإذا كان مخيلاً: كان معدلاً. ووجه القول الآخر: أنه إذا كان على مثال العلل الشرعية: كان صالحاً، كالشاهد، ثم قد يحتمل أن يكون مجروحاً، فلا بد من العرض على المزكين. وهم الأصول ها هنا، وأدنى ذلك أصلان، ولا يُعتبر وراء ذلك؛ لأن التزكية بالاحتمال لا تُردُّ. ووجه قولنا : أنا احتجنا إلى إثبات صحة ما لا يُحس ولا يعاين، وهو الوصف الذي جعل عَلَماً على الحكم في النص، وما لا يُحس ولا يُعايَن فإنما يُعلَم بأثره الذي ظهر في موضع من المواضع. ألا يُرى أنا تعرَّفنا صدق الشاهد باحترازه عن محظور دينه، وذلك مما يُعرف وجوده بأثره، وكذلك يُعرف الصانع استدلالاً بآثار صنعه، وذلك مما يُعرف بالبيان والوصف بوجه مُجمع عليه، على ما نبيِّن إن شاء الله تعالى، فوجب المصير إليه كالأثر المحسوس الدال على غير المحسوس.
They agree that all the attributes of the text in their entirety cannot be a cause. And they disagreed regarding the indication of its being a cause on two views: The Ahl al-Ṭard said: it becomes a proof by mere consistency, without a rational meaning. And the jurist imams from the predecessors and successors, may Allah have mercy on them, said: it is not a proof except by becoming a rational meaning, and this meaning is the suitability of the attribute, then its integrity. This is according to the example of a witness: his suitability is necessary such that he becomes fit to perform testimony, then the performance is not valid except with the utterance of testimony, then his integrity so that it is valid. And they agreed regarding its suitability that what is meant by it is its consonance, and that is by being in accordance with what has come from the predecessors, may Allah be pleased with them, of transmitted causes; because it is a legal matter, so it is learned from it and action with it is not valid before consonance, just as action with the testimony of a witness is not valid before eligibility, but action with it is not obligatory except after integrity. And integrity according to us is the effect, and by 'effect' we mean: that which has an effect for it in the law. And some of the companions of al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, said: its integrity is by its being suggestive [mukhīl], then presenting it to the principles as a precaution, for its safety from contradiction and opposition. And some of the companions of al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, said: rather its integrity is by presenting it to the principles; if no contradictory or opposing principle rejects it, it becomes integral, and it is presented to two or more principles. So according to the first view: action with it is valid before presentation; according to the second: it is not valid, because by that it becomes a proof. According to the first view: it became a proof by being suggestive, and deficiency is a defect, and opposition is a repulsion. And the proponents of the first article argued: the effect is a meaning not rationally understood, so one moves from it to the testimony of the heart, which is the imagination, and it is like seeking the best [taḥarrī] made a proof by the testimony of the heart when action with all other evidences is impossible, then presentation after that as a precaution. Contrary to the witness, because it is imagined that after the original eligibility something may occur that invalidates the testimony from sin or other; as for the attribute, it does not admit something similar. So if it is consonant and not repulsive, it becomes suitable; if it is suggestive, it becomes integral. The justification of the other view: if it is on the model of legal causes, it is suitable, like a witness, then it may be defective, so presentation to the approvers is necessary. And they are the principles here, and the minimum is two principles, and beyond that is not considered, because approval by probability is not rejected. And the justification of our view: we needed to establish the validity of what is not sensed nor perceived, which is the attribute made a sign of the ruling in the text, and what is not sensed nor perceived is only known by its effect that appears in some place. Do you not see that we have known the truthfulness of a witness by his avoidance of the prohibitions of his religion, and that is known to exist by its effect. And likewise the Creator is known by inference from the effects of His creation. And that is known by clarification and description in a consensual manner, as we will explain if Allah wills, so it becomes necessary to resort to it like the sensed effect indicating the non-sensed.
Source pages p. 590

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bazdawi's 'Kanz al-Wusul' addresses a fundamental dispute in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: whether a descriptive attribute (waṣf) found in a text can serve as the effective cause (ʿilla) for a ruling solely based on its consistency (iṭṭirād) across cases, or whether it requires a rationally understood meaning (maʿnā maʿqūl) and additional validation. The author begins by noting agreement that not all attributes of a text can be causes. The disagreement centers on the evidentiary value of a consistent attribute. 'Ahl al-Ṭard' hold that mere consistency makes it a proof (ḥujja), while the majority of jurists (a'immat al-fiqh) insist that a rational meaning is necessary, comprising two components: suitability (ṣalāḥ) and integrity (ʿadāla). The author draws an analogy to a witness: a witness must be suitable (eligible) for testimony, and then must have integrity (ʿadāla), which is confirmed. For the attribute, suitability is its consonance (mulā'ama) with precedents from the Salaf; integrity, according to the Hanafis, is defined as 'athar' — having an effect in the Shari'a. In contrast, some Shafiʿis hold that integrity is achieved through being suggestive (mukhīl) or by presenting the attribute to legal principles (uṣūl) for verification. The debate then examines whether action with the attribute is valid before such presentation. The Hanafi position defended here relies on the concept of 'athar': the attribute's validity is known through its effect, just as a witness's truthfulness is inferred from his conduct, or the Creator is known through His creation. The author concludes that resorting to the effect is necessary, analogizing it to a sensed effect indicating the non-sensed. This passage establishes the Hanafi criterion for ʿilla, emphasizing rationality and systematic verification over mere empirical regularity.

Key Terms
الاطراد (al-iṭṭirād): Consistency of the attribute across different cases without exception.
العدالة (al-ʿadāla): The integrity of the attribute, defined by Hanafis as having an effect (athar) in the law; other schools define it as being suggestive (mukhīl) or verified by principles.
الصلاح (al-ṣalāḥ): Suitability of the attribute, meaning consonance (mulā'ama) with transmitted causes from the Salaf.
المخيل (al-mukhīl): A suggestive attribute that appeals to the mind as a likely cause, even if not rationally proven.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the defining condition for the 'integrity' (ʿadāla) of a descriptive attribute serving as a legal cause?
A) The attribute must be consistent (muṭṭarid).
B) The attribute must be suggestive (mukhīl).
C) The attribute must have an effect (athar) in the Sharīʿa.
D) The attribute must be presented to two or more legal principles.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, mere consistency is insufficient; a valid legal cause must have a rationally understood meaning and an established effect in the law.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

Khiyal vs. Athar in Qiyas: The Hanafi Rejection of Fanciful Suitability
Dispute over khiyal as valid basis for illa in qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on using khiyal (fanciful suitability) as a basis for illa in qiyas, explain the opposing arguments, and apply the Hanafi critique using the concept of athar (effect).

Core Idea
In qiyas, a valid illa must be based on an observable effect (athar), not mere fanciful suitability (khiyal).

This passage presents a classical usul dispute over the validity of khiyal (fanciful suitability) as a basis for the illa in qiyas. The Hanafi school firmly rejects khiyal, arguing that an attribute must be supported by a perceptible consequence (athar) to be a valid cause. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly performing qiyas and evaluating competing legal analogies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions on illa validity
The passage presents three views: (1) an attribute is valid if it is mukhayyil (fanciful), (2) it must be on the model of shar'i causes and then presented to principles, and (3) it must be based on athar (effect). Recognize which corresponds to which school if mentioned.
The first opinion is associated with some scholars who accept khiyal; the second with those who require model-based suitability; the third is the Hanafi view.
2
State the Hanafi view on athar
The Hanafi position insists that since the attribute is not directly sensed, its validity must be inferred from a perceptible effect (athar) that appears in some context. Without athar, the attribute remains a subjective claim.
Athar is the observable consequence that demonstrates the attribute's suitability as a cause.
3
Analyze the opponent's reliance on khiyal
The first opinion argues that when other proofs fail, one resorts to the testimony of the heart (khiyal), similar to taharri (seeking truth by conjecture). They also use presentation to principles as a safeguard. Understand their reasoning.
Taharri is the process of making a best guess when evidence is ambiguous.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi critique of khiyal
The Hanafi response argues that khiyal is false because it is mere conjecture (zan), internal, cannot serve as a proof against an opponent, and shari'ah proofs are based on outward appearances. Also, presentation to principles is ineffective because principles cannot certify without knowledge.
The critique also notes that khiyal leads to mutual contradiction between opponents.
5
Apply the dispute to the hadith of the cat
The Prophet's ta'lil (reasoning) for the cat's purity was based on the athar of necessity (the cat's frequent movement). This example illustrates the Hanafi method: the cause is identified by its observable effect, not by fanciful suitability.
The hadith is: 'It is not impure; it is among those who go around among you.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يصح ؛ لأنه به يصير حجة. وعلى القول الأول : صار حجةٌ بكونه مُخيلاً، وإنما النَّقصُ جَرَحٌ، والمعارضة دفع. واحتج أهلُ المقالة الأولى: أن الأثر معنى لا يُعقل، فنقل عنه إلى شهادة القلب، وهو الخيال، وهو كالتحري جُعل حجةً بشهادة القلب عند تعذر العمل بسائر الأدلة، ثم العرض بعد ذلك؛ للاحتياط. بخلاف الشاهد ؛ لأنه يُتوهم أن يعترض فيه بعد أصل الأهلية ما يُبطل الشهادة من فسق أو غيره، فأما الوصف فلا يحتمل مثله، فإذا كان ملائماً غير ناب: صار صالحاً، وإذا كان مخيلاً : كان معدلاً. ووجه القول الآخر: أنه إذا كان على مثال العلل الشرعية: كان صالحاً، كالشاهد، ثم قد يحتمل أن يكون مجروحاً، فلا بد من العرض على المزكين. وهم الأصول ها هنا، وأدنى ذلك أصلان، ولا يُعتبر وراء ذلك ؛ لأن التزكية بالاحتمال لا تُردُّ. ووجه قولنا : أنا احتجنا إلى إثبات صحة ما لا يُحس ولا يعاين، وهو الوصف الذي جعل عَلَماً على الحكم في النص، وما لا يُحس ولا يُعايَن فإنما يُعلَم بأثره الذي ظهر في موضع من المواضع. ألا يُرى أنا تعرَّفنا صدق الشاهد باحترازه عن محظور دينه، وذلك مما يُعرف وجوده بأثره، وكذلك يُعرف الصانع استدلالاً بآثار صنعه، وذلك مما يُعرف بالبيان والوصف بوجه مُجمع عليه، على ما نبيِّن إن شاء الله تعالى، فوجب المصير إليه كالأثر المحسوس الدال على غير المحسوس. وأما الخيال: فأمر باطل؛ لأنه ظن، لا حقيقة له، ولأنه باطن لا يصلح دليلاً على الخصم ولا دليلاً شرعياً؛ لأن الله تعالى أجرى الحكم على الظاهر، ولأنه دعوىً لا ينفك عن المعارضة ؛ لأن كل خصم يحتج بمثله فيما يدعيه على خصمه ودلائل الشرع لا تحتمل لزوم المعارضة كما لا تحتمل لزوم المناقضة. وأما العرض على الأصول : فلا يقع به التعديل ؛ لأن الأصول شهود، لا مزكون وأنَّى لها التزكية من غير دَرْكِ لأحوال الشاهد ومعانيه، وهل تصح التزكية ممن لا خبرة له، ولا معرفة له بالشهود؟ فأما فَرقهم : بأن الشاهد مبتلى بالطاعة، منهي عن المعصية : فيتوهم سقوط شهادته، بخلاف الوصف : فليس بصحيح؛ لأن الوصف مع كونه ملائماً يجوز أن يكون غير علة؛ لأنه لا يصير علة بذاته، بل بجعل الشرع إياه علة، فكان الاحتمال في أصله أولى من الاحتمال في المعترض على أصله ، ألا يُرى أن الوصف لا يبقى علة مع الرد مع قيام الملاءمة. والجواب عن كلامه: أن الأثر معقول من كل محسوس ومن كل مشروع معقول دلالةً، على ما بينا. وإنما يظهر ذلك بأمثلته. - لغةً وعياناً، وذلك مثل قول النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام في الهرة: «إنها ليست بنجسة، وإنما هي من الطوافين عليكم، والطوافات»(۱): تعليل للطهارة بما ظهر أثره، وهو الضرورة، فإنها من أسباب التخفيف، وسقوط (۱) حديث : إنها ليست بنجسة : تقدم في باب القياس [ص٥٥٧].
It is valid because by it it becomes a proof. According to the first opinion, it became a proof by being fanciful, and the deficiency is a defect, and objection is a rebuttal. The proponents of the first opinion argued: The effect (athar) is a meaning that is not rationally perceivable, so they transferred from it to the testimony of the heart, which is fancifulness (khiyal), and it is like taharri (seeking the truth by conjecture), made a proof by the testimony of the heart when action upon all other proofs is impossible, then presentation (to principles) afterwards as a precaution. Unlike a witness, because it is possible that after initial eligibility, something that invalidates the testimony such as sin or other may occur; as for the attribute, it does not admit such possibility. So if it is suitable and not repugnant, it becomes valid; if it is fanciful, it becomes just. The other opinion: if it is on the model of shar'i causes, it becomes valid, like a witness; then it is possible that it is defective, so it must be presented to those who certify integrity. They are the principles (usul) here, and the minimum is two principles, and beyond that is not considered, because certification upon probability is not rejected. Our opinion: we need to prove the validity of what is not sensed nor perceived, which is the attribute made an indicator of the ruling in the text. That which is not sensed nor perceived is known only by its effect that appears in some place. Do you not see that we recognize the truthfulness of a witness by his refraining from what his religion prohibits, and that is known to exist by its effect; likewise the Creator is known by inference from the effects of His creation. That is known by exposition and description in a unanimously agreed manner, as we will explain if Allah wills. Therefore, it is necessary to resort to it like a sensed effect indicating the non-sensed. As for fancifulness: it is false because it is conjecture with no reality; because it is internal and not suitable as a proof against an opponent nor as a legal proof, because Allah Most High has made rulings based on outward appearances; and because it is a claim that cannot be free from objection, since every opponent uses the like of it in his claim against his opponent, and the proofs of the shari'ah do not admit necessary objection just as they do not admit necessary contradiction. As for presentation to principles: certification does not occur thereby because the principles are witnesses, not those who certify; how can they certify without knowledge of the conditions of the witness and his attributes? Can certification be valid from one who has no expertise and no knowledge of witnesses? As for their distinction that the witness is tested with obedience, forbidden from sin, so it is possible that his testimony falls, unlike the attribute: it is not correct, because the attribute, while being suitable, may not be the cause (illa) because it does not become a cause by itself, but rather by the shari'ah making it a cause. Thus the possibility in its very foundation is more likely than the possibility of an objection to its foundation. Do you not see that the attribute does not remain a cause when rejected despite existing suitability? The answer to his statement: the effect (athar) is rationally perceivable from every sensed and from every legislated thing, as indicative, as we have explained. And that is evident through examples - linguistically and evidentially. Such as the statement of the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, concerning the cat: 'It is not impure, it is indeed among those who go around among you.' This is a cause (ta'lil) for purity by what appears of its effect, which is necessity, for it is among the causes of leniency and removal... (1) Hadith: 'It is not impure' preceded in the chapter on Qiyas [p. 557].
Source pages p. 591

Commentary
This passage is a dispute in usul al-fiqh about the validity of khiyal (fanciful suitability) as a basis for establishing the illa (cause) in qiyas. The author presents three positions: the first holds that an attribute becomes a valid hujja if it is mukhayyil (fanciful); the second holds that it must be on the model of shar'i causes; and the third (the author's own) insists on reliance on athar (effect) rather than khiyal. The author argues that khiyal is bātil because it is mere conjecture, not objective, and cannot withstand challenge from an opponent. It is internal and not verifiable, whereas athar is a perceptible consequence that demonstrates the attribute's suitability. For example, the cat's frequent movement is an athar that indicates necessity, which justifies leniency. The author also criticizes the practice of 'ard ala al-usul (presentation to principles) for verifying the attribute, because principles are like witnesses who cannot certify without direct knowledge. The hadith of the cat is cited as a proof that the Prophet used an effect (necessity) as the illa for purity, not a fanciful attribute. This passage represents the Hanafi preference for objective, externally verifiable indicators in qiyas over subjective imaginative suitability.

Key Terms
الأثر (al-athar): The perceptible consequence or effect of an attribute that indicates its suitability as a cause.
الخيال (al-khiyal): Fanciful suitability; a subjective impression that an attribute is appropriate without empirical basis.
العرض على الأصول (al-ard ala al-usul): Presenting an attribute to established principles (usul) for verification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why is khiyal (fanciful suitability) invalid as a basis for an illa in qiyas?
A) Because it is not mentioned in the Quran or Sunnah.
B) Because it is mere conjecture and cannot be proven objectively to an opponent.
C) Because it requires presentation to principles, which is impossible.
D) Because it is only valid in cases of necessity.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school insists that a valid illa in qiyas must be based on a perceptible effect (athar), not on fanciful suitability (khiyal).

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
8 min
Invalid Methods and Valid Ta'lil in Hanafi Usul
Refutation of two invalid proof methods and demonstration of valid ta'lil via hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between invalid methods (khayal and 'ard) and valid ta'lil using effective causes in Hanafi usul, and apply this distinction to evaluate arguments.

Core Idea
Imagination and abstract principles cannot prove or refute a ruling — only effective causes designated by the Lawgiver can.

This passage from Usul al-Bazdawi confronts two flawed methods of legal reasoning: pure imagination (al-khayal) and appealing to general principles to disqualify witnesses (al-ard 'ala al-usul). The author argues these have no place in Hanafi usul. He then illustrates valid ta'lil through three prophetic examples, showing how the Lawgiver designates effective causes. Understanding this distinction is essential for constructing sound qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two invalid methods
The text first refutes al-khayal and al-ard 'ala al-usul. Note why each is rejected: khayal is baseless conjecture with no outward evidence, and 'ard fails because principles cannot act as modifiers of witnesses.
Khayal is internal and subjective; 'ard is abstract and lacks direct knowledge.
2
Analyze the opponent's argument
The opponent tries to distinguish between a witness and a description, claiming a suitable description remains a valid cause. State the opponent's position clearly.
The opponent's distinction: witness prone to sin vs. description unaffected.
3
Understand the Hanafi response
The response: a description does not become a cause by itself; it requires the Lawgiver's designation. The possibility of non-causality in the origin is stronger than any objection.
A suitable description (wasf mula'im) is not automatically a cause (illa).
4
Examine the prophetic examples of valid ta'lil
For the cat, mustahadah, and kissing fasting examples, identify the effective cause (athar) that the Prophet used.
Focus on the perceptible effect: necessity (darurah), persistent illness (infijar), non-fulfillment of appetite.
5
Apply the rule
Recognize that effective causes are perceptible or legally meaningful impacts, not mere suitability or imagination.
This prevents arbitrary analogies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الخيال: فأمر باطل؛ لأنه ظن، لا حقيقة له، ولأنه باطن لا يصلح دليلاً على الخصم ولا دليلاً شرعياً؛ لأن الله تعالى أجرى الحكم على الظاهر، ولأنه دعوىً لا ينفك عن المعارضة ؛ لأن كل خصم يحتج بمثله فيما يدعيه على خصمه ودلائل الشرع لا تحتمل لزوم المعارضة كما لا تحتمل لزوم المناقضة.
وأما العرض على الأصول : فلا يقع به التعديل ؛ لأن الأصول شهود، لا مزكون وأنَّى لها التزكية من غير دَرْكِ لأحوال الشاهد ومعانيه، وهل تصح التزكية ممن لا خبرة له، ولا معرفة له بالشهود؟!
فأما فَرقهم : بأن الشاهد مبتلى بالطاعة، منهي عن المعصية : فيتوهم سقوط شهادته، بخلاف الوصف : فليس بصحيح؛ لأن الوصف مع كونه ملائماً يجوز أن يكون غير علة؛ لأنه لا يصير علة بذاته، بل بجعل الشرع إياه علة، فكان الاحتمال في أصله أولى من الاحتمال في المعترض على أصله ، ألا يُرى أن الوصف لا يبقى علة مع الرد مع قيام الملاءمة.
والجواب عن كلامه: أن الأثر معقول من كل محسوس ومن كل مشروع معقول دلالةً، على ما بينا.
وإنما يظهر ذلك بأمثلته. لغةً وعياناً،
وذلك مثل قول النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام في الهرة: «إنها ليست بنجسة، وإنما هي من الطوافين عليكم، والطوافات»: تعليل للطهارة بما ظهر أثره، وهو الضرورة، فإنها من أسباب التخفيف، وسقوط الحظر بالكتاب.
قال الله تعالى: ﴿فَمَنِ اضْطُرَّ فِي مَخْمَصَةٍ غَيْرَ مُتَجَانِفِ لِإِثْمِ فَإِنَّ اللَّهَ غفُورٌ رَحِيمُ﴾ المائدة / ٣.
والطواف من أسباب الضرورة، فصح التعليل به؛ لما يتصل به من الضرورة.
ومثل قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم للمستحاضة : «إنه دم عرق انفجر، فتوضئي لكل صلاة»: أوجب بهذا النص الطهارة بالدم بمعنى النجاسة، ولقيام النجاسة أثر في وجوب التطهير، وعلقه بالانفجار.
وله أثر في الخروج ؛ لأنه غير معتاد والانفجار آفة ومرض لازم، فكان له أثر في التخفيف في قيام الطهارة مع وجوده في وقت الحاجة.
ومثل قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام لعمر رضي الله عنه وقد سأله القُبلة من الصائم، فقال: «أرايت لو تمضمضت بماء، ثم مَجَجته: أكان يضرك ؟!» : تعليل بمعنى مؤثر ؛ لأن الفطر نقيض الصوم، والصوم كف النفس عن شهوة البطن والفرج، وليس في القبلة قضاؤها، لا صورة ولا معنى، مثل المضمضة.
وقال عليه الصلاة والسلام في تحريم الصدقة على بني هاشم: (الحديث)
As for imagination: it is an invalid matter; because it is conjecture, having no reality, and because it is hidden, not suitable as evidence against an opponent nor as a legal evidence; because Allah Almighty has made rulings based on what is apparent, and because it is a claim that cannot be free from opposition; because every opponent uses similar in what he claims against his opponent, and the evidences of the Law do not bear necessary opposition just as they do not bear necessary contradiction. As for presentation to the principles: ta'dil (disqualification) does not occur by it; because the principles are witnesses, not modifiers, and how can they have modification without knowledge of the witness's conditions and meanings, and is modification valid from one who has no experience and no knowledge of the witnesses? As for their distinction: that the witness is tried with obedience and prohibited from disobedience, so one might imagine his testimony falls, unlike the description, it is not correct; because the description, despite being suitable, may not be the cause; for it does not become a cause by itself, but by the Law making it a cause. So the probability in its origin is more likely than the probability in the objector against its origin. Do you not see that the description does not remain a cause with rejection despite suitability? The answer to his statement: that the effect is understood from every perceptible and from every legally intelligible thing as evidence, as we have explained. That becomes clear through examples, linguistically and evidently. For example, the Prophet's saying, peace be upon him, regarding the cat: 'It is not impure; it is among those who go around you and those who go around you' – a ta'lil (causation) for purity by what has an apparent effect, which is necessity, for it is among causes of lightening and removal of prohibition by the Book. Allah Almighty said: 'But whoever is forced by extreme hunger, not inclined to sin, then indeed Allah is Forgiving and Merciful.' Al-Ma'idah 5:3. And going around is among causes of necessity, so the ta'lil by it is valid due to the necessity connected to it. And like his saying, peace be upon him, to the woman with chronic bleeding: 'It is a blood from a burst vein, so make wudu for each prayer' – by this text he obligated purification despite blood in the sense of impurity, and because the existence of impurity has an effect in the obligation of purification, and he linked it to the bursting. And it has an effect in the exit because it is unusual, and bursting is an affliction and a necessary illness, so it had an effect in lightening by maintaining purity despite its existence at a time of need. And like his saying, peace be upon him, to 'Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, when he asked about kissing while fasting: 'What if you rinsed your mouth with water and then spat it out, would it harm you?' – a ta'lil by an effective meaning; because breaking the fast contradicts fasting, and fasting is restraining the soul from desire of stomach and private parts, and in kissing there is no fulfillment of that, neither in form nor meaning, like rinsing the mouth. And he said, peace be upon him, regarding the prohibition of charity for Banu Hashim: (the hadith).
Source pages p. 592

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Bazdawi is a refutation of two invalid methods that some might use to establish or challenge legal rulings. The first is al-khayal (imagination or conjecture). The author argues that imagination is baseless because it is pure speculation without reality, and since it is internal and subjective, it cannot serve as evidence against an opponent or as a valid legal proof. The Shari'ah operates on outward appearances, and such conjectures are inevitably countered by opposing conjectures from the other party. The second invalid method is al-ard 'ala al-usul (presentation to the principles). Here, the author criticizes the idea that one can disqualify a witness by simply comparing his situation with general principles (usul) that describe the typical behavior of witnesses. He says that the principles (usul) act as witnesses themselves, not as 'modifiers' (muzakkin). To modify or certify a witness, one must have direct knowledge of that witness's conditions and character; mere appeal to abstract principles cannot substitute for this knowledge. The author then engages with an opponent's argument that distinguishes between a witness (who is commanded to obey and forbidden from sin) and a description (wasf) used in qiyas. The opponent claimed that since a witness is liable to sin, one might imagine his testimony falls, but a description (if suitable) remains valid. The author refutes this, stating that a description, even if suitable (mula'im), does not automatically become a 'illa (cause). It becomes a cause only if the Lawgiver so designates it. Hence, the possibility of being a non-cause remains, and this possibility in the origin (asl) is more serious than any possibility in the objector's challenge. Moreover, a description does not persist as a cause if it is rejected, even with suitability. After this refutation, the author presents his positive argument: the effect (athar) is perceived from every sensible matter and from every legally valid matter as an indication of causation, as previously explained. He then provides several prophetic examples to illustrate valid ta'lil (causation) using effective meanings. The first example is the hadith about the cat: the Prophet, peace be upon him, declared the cat not impure and gave the reason that it is among those that go around (in houses). The author explains that this is a ta'lil by an apparent effect: necessity (darurah). Necessity is a cause of lightening (takhfif) and removal of prohibition, as supported by the Quranic verse on necessity (al-Ma'idah 5:3). The cat's frequent going around creates necessity, so the ruling of purity is based on that. The second example is the hadith of the mustahadah (woman with chronic bleeding): the Prophet said it is a blood from a burst vein, and he ordered her to make wudu for each prayer. Here, the blood is impure, but the ruling of obligation of wudu despite impurity is linked to the bursting (infijar). The bursting has an effect (athar) in that it is an unusual occurrence and a persistent illness, thus it is a cause for lightening: purity (i.e., wudu) remains despite the presence of impurity at the time of need. The third example is the hadith about the fasting person kissing: the Prophet compared it to rinsing the mouth and spitting out. The author says this is a ta'lil by an effective meaning: breaking the fast (futur) contradicts fasting, and fasting is refraining from the desires of the stomach and private parts. Kissing does not fulfill that desire, neither in form nor meaning, just as rinsing the mouth does not break the fast. The effective meaning is the non-fulfillment of the appetite. Finally, the passage begins the example of the prohibition of charity for Banu Hashim, but the text cuts off, presumably referring to a known hadith. In summary, the passage firmly establishes that valid legal reasoning (ta'lil) must be based on effective causes (al-ma'na al-mu'aththir) that have a perceptible effect, not on mere imagination or abstract appeal to principles.

Key Terms
الخيال (al-khayal): Imagination or conjecture that has no basis in reality or outward evidence; considered invalid as a legal proof.
العرض على الأصول (al-ard 'ala al-usul): Presenting a witness's situation to general principles to disqualify him; deemed invalid because principles cannot replace direct knowledge of the witness.
المعنى المؤثر (al-ma'na al-mu'aththir): The effective cause or attribute that has a perceptible legal effect and is designated by the Lawgiver as the basis for a ruling.
الوصف الملائم (al-wasf al-mula'im): A suitable description that appears appropriate for the ruling but is not necessarily a cause unless designated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a valid method of legal proof according to the passage?
A) Analogy based on effective cause
B) Imagination (khayal)
C) Presentation to principles ('ard)
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
Imagination and abstract principles cannot establish or refute a ruling; only effective causes designated by the Lawgiver are valid for ta'lil.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
