Legal Reasoning with Effective Causes (Ta'lil bi Ma'na Mu'aththir)
Exemplifying legal reasoning through effective causes from Prophetic and Companion precedents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the method of ta'lil bi ma'na mu'aththir by identifying effective causes in Prophetic and Companion precedents and deriving analogous rulings.

Core Idea
Ta'lil bi ma'na mu'aththir: effective causes are manifest attributes with a clear legal effect, enabling analogical extension.

This passage presents a series of Prophetic and Companion examples demonstrating how rulings are derived by identifying an effective cause (ma'na mu'aththir). These examples form the backbone of Hanafi qiyas, showing that a ruling's 'illah must be both manifest (zahir) and productive of the legal effect. By studying these precedents, you will learn to recognize and apply this method in new cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and the stated cause
For each example, extract the original ruling and the attribute the Prophet or Companion used to explain it.
E.g., in the mustahada hadith: ruling = wudu for each prayer; cause = bursting of a vein.
2
Assess the effectiveness of the cause
Determine why the attribute qualifies as mu'aththir: does it have a clear, demonstrable impact on the ruling?
The bursting is abnormal and necessitates leniency, so it is effective.
3
Trace the analogical extension
How does the cause link the original case to the analogous case? What common element justifies the extension?
Kissing is compared to rinsing: both lack consumption, so the cause of breaking fast (entry of substance) is absent.
4
Apply the method to a new case
Using the same pattern, reason by an effective cause for a hypothetical scenario, e.g., continuous nosebleed.
The cause (continuous blood from a vessel) extends the mustahada ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحظر بالكتاب.
قال الله تعالى: ﴿فَمَنِ اضْطُرَّ فِي مَخْمَصَةٍ غَيْرَ مُتَجَانِفِ لِإِثْمِ فَإِنَّ اللَّهَ غفُورٌ رَحِيمُ﴾ المائدة / ٣.
والطواف من أسباب الضرورة، فصح التعليل به؛ لما يتصل به من الضرورة.
ومثل قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم للمستحاضة : «إنه دم عرق انفجر، فتوضئي لكل صلاة»: أوجب بهذا النص الطهارة بالدم بمعنى النجاسة، ولقيام النجاسة أثر في وجوب التطهير، وعلقه بالانفجار. وله أثر في الخروج؛ لأنه غير معتاد والانفجار آفة ومرض لازم، فكان له أثر في التخفيف في قيام الطهارة مع وجوده في وقت الحاجة.
ومثل قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام لعمر رضي الله عنه وقد سأله القُبلة من الصائم، فقال: «أرايت لو تمضمضت بماء، ثم مَجَجته: أكان يضرك؟!»: تعليل بمعنى مؤثر؛ لأن الفطر نقيض الصوم، والصوم كف النفس عن شهوة البطن والفرج، وليس في القبلة قضاؤها، لا صورة ولا معنى، مثل المضمضة.
وقال عليه الصلاة والسلام في تحريم الصدقة على بني هاشم: «أرايت لو تمضمضت بماء، ثم مججته: أكنت شاربه؟!»، فعلل بمعنى مؤثر، وهو أن الصدقة مطهرة للأوزار، فكانت وسخاً كالماء المستعمل. واختلف أصحاب النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام في الجد، فضربوا فيه بالأمثال، مثل فروع الشجر، وشعوب الوادي، والأنهار، والجداول. واحتج ابن عباس رضي الله عنهما فيه بقرب أحد طرفي القرابة، وهذه أمور معقولة بآثارها.
وقد قال عمر رضي الله عنه لعبادة بن الصامت حين قال له ما أرى: النارَ تُحلُّ شيئاً: أليس يكون خمراً، ثم يصير خلا، فناكله؟!، فعلل بمعنى مؤثر، وهو تغير الطباع.
وقال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله: في اثنين اشتريا عبداً وهو قريب أحدهما: إنه لا يضمن لشريكه؛ لأنه أعتقه برضاه وللرضا أثر في سقوط العدوان. وقال محمد رحمه الله: في إيداع الصبي؛ لأنه سلطه على استهلاكه.
Prohibition by the Book. Allah the Exalted said: 'But whoever is forced by severe hunger with no inclination to sin, then indeed Allah is Forgiving and Merciful' (al-Ma'idah 5:3). And circumambulation is among the causes of necessity, so it is sound to reason by means of it due to the necessity connected to it. And similar is his saying, peace be upon him, to the woman with chronic uterine bleeding: 'It is a blood from a vein that burst, so perform ablution for every prayer.' By this text he made purity obligatory due to blood in the sense of impurity, and the existence of impurity has an effect on the obligation of purification, and he linked it to the bursting. And it has an effect on exit because it is not normal, and bursting is an affliction and a necessary illness, so it had an effect on leniency in maintaining purity with its existence at a time of need. And similar is his saying, peace be upon him, to Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, when he asked about kissing while fasting: 'Do you think if you rinsed your mouth with water and then spat it out, would it harm you?' This is reasoning by an effective cause because breaking the fast is the opposite of fasting, and fasting is restraining the soul from the desire of the stomach and private parts, and in kissing there is no fulfilling of it, neither in form nor meaning, like rinsing the mouth. And he said, peace be upon him, regarding the prohibition of charity upon the Banu Hashim: 'Do you think if you rinsed your mouth with water and then spat it out, would you drink it?' So he reasoned by an effective cause, which is that charity purifies from sins, so it is like filth like used water. And the Companions of the Prophet, peace be upon him, differed regarding the grandfather, and they gave examples for it, such as branches of a tree, and tributaries of a valley, and rivers, and streams. And Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them both, argued in it by the closeness of one end of kinship, and these are matters understood by their effects. And Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, said to Ubadah ibn al-Samit when he said to him: 'I do not think that fire can make something lawful': 'Does it not become wine, then become vinegar, and we eat it?' So he reasoned by an effective cause, which is the change of nature. And Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, said: Regarding two people who bought a slave who is a relative of one of them: He is not liable to his partner because he emancipated him with his consent, and consent has an effect in dropping the claim of wrong. And Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, said: Regarding the deposit with a child because he empowered him to consume it.
Source pages p. 593

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings and reasoning (ta'lil) exemplifying how Hanafi jurists identify effective causes ('illah) in legal rulings. The author begins by citing the Quranic verse on necessity, then connects it to circumambulation (tawaf) as a cause of necessity, showing that the permissibility of breaking prohibitions is tied to genuine need. Next, the hadith of the mustahada (woman with irregular bleeding) is presented: the Prophet attributes the blood to a burst vein, linking the ruling of wudu for each prayer to this physical cause. The commentary notes that the bursting is an abnormal, continuous affliction, so the law grants leniency. Then, the hadith about kissing while fasting uses the analogy of rinsing the mouth: since neither involves actual consumption, the effective cause (consumption of food/drink) is absent, so kissing does not break the fast. Similarly, the hadith on charity for Banu Hashim uses the analogy of rinsing water: charity is like filth that purifies, so it is not consumed. The Companions' dispute over the grandfather's share is resolved via analogies (branches, rivers) and Ibn Abbas's reasoning by proximity of kinship. Umar's argument to 'Ubadah shows that fire changes the nature of wine into vinegar, making it lawful—the effective cause is transformation. Finally, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad give two case rulings: when two jointly buy a slave who is a relative of one, the relative's consent in emancipating causes no liability; and a child's deposit is valid because he empowers the depository to consume it. All examples illustrate that rulings are grounded in manifest, effective attributes (ma'na mu'aththir) that bear a clear connection to the legal effect.

Key Terms
التعليل (al-ta'lil): The process of identifying and applying a legal cause (ʿillah) to derive or analogize a ruling.
معنى مؤثر (maʿnā muʾaththir): An effective attribute or cause that has a clear impact on the legal ruling, used for analogical reasoning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the hadith about kissing while fasting, what analogy did the Prophet use to show that kissing does not break the fast?
A) The analogy of eating
B) The analogy of rinsing the mouth with water
C) The analogy of drinking
D) The analogy of sleeping

Key Takeaway
Effective causes (ma'na mu'aththir) are manifest attributes that produce a legal effect, enabling analogical extension of rulings to new cases.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate

Ta'lil bi al-Athar: Valid Analogical Reasoning Through Effective Attributes
Demonstrating validity of ta'lil bi al-athar via examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a 'ma'na mu'aththir' (effective attribute) in a ruling and apply the Hanafi principle that ta'lil bi al-athar constitutes valid qiyas without an explicit original case.

Core Idea
An effective attribute derived from a ruling's effect suffices for qiyas; no explicit asl needed.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul demonstrates a fundamental Hanafi doctrine: causation by effective attribute (ta'lil bi al-athar) is a legitimate form of qiyas. Through numerous examples from the Companions, Abu Hanifa, and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the author shows that the effective cause need not be explicitly stated in an original case; it can be inferred from the observable effect of the ruling. This lesson unpacks these examples and equips you to recognize and apply this methodology.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the core claim
The passage asserts that ta'lil bi al-athar is valid qiyas. Identify what is being proved: that an effective attribute (ma'na mu'aththir) derived from the effect of a ruling can serve as the cause for analogy, even without a direct textual original case.
This is a distinct Hanafi position; the Shafi'i school generally requires an explicit asl.
2
Examine the first example (mouth rinsing)
The Prophet asked: 'If you rinse your mouth and spit, would you be considered its drinker?' The reasoning used is that charity purifies sins, so used water is like filth. Here, the effect (purification) provides the effective cause, not an explicit original ruling.
The attribute 'being used to purify' is inferred from the outcome.
3
Analyze the guardianship example
The Hanafi view on marriage guardianship: it is legislated due to the minor's inability (al-ajz). This cause is derived from the effect (the minor's need for protection), analogous to maintenance for the incapable. The effective attribute is the inability itself.
The asl here is the ruling on maintenance, but the cause is not explicitly stated in the source; it is inferred from the purpose.
4
Contrast with the Shafi'i objection
The passage ends with the objection: 'Reasoning by effect is not qiyas because qiyas is only with an original case.' Recognize that this is a methodological disagreement. The Hanafi response, though implicit, is that the examples themselves demonstrate the validity of ta'lil bi al-athar.
The objection is not explicitly refuted in the text, but the whole passage serves as a counterargument.
5
Synthesize the Hanafi response
By examining the examples collectively, we see that effective attributes function as valid causes. The objection is unfounded because the ma'na mu'aththir is sufficient for analogy, even without a textual asl. This allows for broader application of qiyas in new cases.
This principle is foundational for Hanafi legal reasoning, especially in areas where explicit texts are absent.

«أرايت لو تمضمضت بماء، ثم مججته: أكنت شاربه؟!»، فعلَّل بمعنى مؤثر، وهو أن الصدقة مطهرة للأوزار، فكانت وسخاً كالماء المستعمل. واختلف أصحاب النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام في الجد، فضربوا فيه بالأمثال، مثل فروع الشجر (۱)، وشعوب الوادي، والأنهار، والجداول. واحتج ابن عباس رضي الله عنهما فيه بقرب أحد طرفي القرابة، وهذه أمور معقولة بآثارها. وقد قال عمر رضي الله عنه لعبادة بن الصامت حين قال له ما أرى: النارَ تُحلُّ شيئاً: أليس يكون خمراً، ثم يصير خلا، فناكله؟! (۳)، فعلل بمعنى مؤثر، وهو تغير الطباع. وقال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله: في اثنين اشتريا عبداً وهو قريب أحدهما: إنه لا يضمن لشريكه؛ لأنه أعتقه برضاه وللرضا أثر في سقوط العدوان. وقال محمد رحمه الله: في إيداع الصبي؛ لأنه سلطه على استهلاكه.
(۱) قوله: فضربوا فيه مثالاً بالشجرة: ذكره أبو حنيفة عند جعفر بن محمد الصادق أن عمر شاور علياً وزيد بن ثابت في الجد مع الإخوة، فقال له علي: أرأيت يا أمير المؤمنين! لو أن شجرةً انشعب منها غصن، ثم انشعب من الغصن غصنان، أيهما أقرب إلى أحد الغصنين؟ أصاحبه الذي خرج منه، أم الشجرة؟ وقال زيد: لو أن جدولاً انبعثت فيه ساقيةٌ، ثم انبعثت من الساقية ساقيتان: أيهما أقرب؟ إحدى الساقيتين أقرب إلى صاحبها أم الجدول؟» أخرجه طلحة أبي حنيفة، والله أعلم.
(۲) قوله: وقد قال عمر العيادة بن الصامت حين قال: ما أرى النار تُحل شيئاً: أليس يكون خمراً، ثم يصير خلا، فنأكله. بياض في الأصل.
- وقال الشافعي رحمه الله في الزنا: لا يوجب حرمة المصاهرة؛ لأنه أمرٌ رجمت عليه، والنكاح أمر حمدت عليه، وهذه أوصاف ظاهرة الآثار.
- وقال الشافعي رحمه الله: في النكاح لا يثبت بشهادة النساء مع الرجال؛ لأنه ليس بمال، ولذلك أثر في هذا الحكم؛ لأن المال هو المبتذل، فاحتيج فيه إلى الحجة الضرورية. وأما ما ليس بمال فغير مبتذل فيجب إثباته بالحجة الأصلية، وليزداد خطره على ما هو مبتذل. وعلى هذا الأصل جرينا في الفروع.
فقلنا في مسح الرأس: إنه مسح، فلا يسن تثليثه، كمسح الخف؛ لأن معنى المسح معنى مؤثر في التخفيف في فرضه، حتى لم يستوعب محله، ففي سنته أولى. فأما قول الخصم: إنه ركن في الوضوء: فغير مؤثر في إبطال التخفيف.
وعللنا في ولاية النكاح بالصغر والبلوغ، وهو المؤثر؛ لأنها ما شرعت إلا حقاً للعاجز، كالنفقة، فصح التعليل بالعجز والقدرة للوجود والعدم، ولم يكن للبكارة والثيابة في ذلك أثر.
وقلنا في صوم رمضان إنه عين، وهذا مؤثر؛ لأن النية في الأصل للتعيين والتمييز، وذلك يُحتاج إلى ذكره عند المزاحمة، دون الانفراد. وعلل بأنه فرض، ولا أثر للفرضية إلا في إصابة المأمور. وهذا أكثر من أن يُحصى.
فإن قيل: التعليل بالأثر لا يكون قياساً؛ لأنه لا قياس إلا بالأصل.
‘Do you think if you rinsed your mouth with water and then spit it out, would you be considered its drinker?’ So he reasoned with an effective meaning, which is that charity purifies sins, so it is like filth, like used water. And the Companions of the Prophet (peace be upon him) disagreed regarding the grandfather, and they struck examples for it, like branches of a tree, tributaries of a valley, rivers, and streams. And Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with him) argued in it by the closeness of one side of kinship, and these are matters understood by their effects. And Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) said to Ubadah ibn al-Samit when he said to him, ‘I do not think fire makes something permissible’: ‘Does it not become wine, then become vinegar, and we eat it?’ So he reasoned with an effective meaning, which is the change of nature. And Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) said regarding two who bought a slave who is a relative of one of them: He does not guarantee for his partner because he freed him with his consent, and consent has an effect in removing aggression. And Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said regarding deposit by a minor: Because he authorized him to consume it. (1) His statement: They struck an example with a tree: Abu Hanifa mentioned from Ja'far al-Sadiq that Umar consulted Ali and Zaid ibn Thabit regarding the grandfather with siblings, and Ali said to him: ‘Do you see, O Commander of the Faithful, if a tree branched out a branch, then from the branch branched two branches, which is closer to one of the two branches? Its companion that came out of it, or the tree?’ And Zaid said: ‘If a stream ran a canal, then from the canal ran two canals, which is closer? One of the two canals is closer to its owner, or the stream?’ Reported by Talha ibn Abi Hanifa, and Allah knows best. (2) His statement: And Umar said to Ubadah ibn al-Samit when he said: ‘I do not think fire makes anything permissible’: ‘Does it not become wine, then become vinegar, and we eat it?’ Blank in the original. - And al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) said regarding fornication: It does not necessitate the prohibition of marriage by affinity because it is a matter for which one is stoned, and marriage is a matter for which one is praised, and these are attributes with apparent effects. - And al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) said regarding marriage: It is not established by the testimony of women with men because it is not property, and thus there is an effect in this ruling because property is that which is common, so a necessary proof is needed for it. And as for what is not property, it is not common, so it must be established by the original proof, and its importance increases over what is common. And upon this principle we proceeded in the branches. So we said regarding wiping the head: It is wiping, so repeating it three times is not recommended, like wiping the khuff; because the meaning of wiping is an effective meaning in lightening its obligation, such that its place is not completely covered, so in its sunnah it is more appropriate. As for the opponent's statement that it is a pillar of wudu: it does not affect the negation of lightening. And we reasoned in the guardianship of marriage by minority and maturity, which is the effective; because it was legislated only as a right for the incapable, like maintenance, so reasoning by inability and ability for existence and non-existence is valid, and virginity and non-virginity have no effect in that. And we said regarding fasting Ramadan that it is specific, and this is effective; because the intention essentially is for specification and distinction, and that needs to be mentioned when there is competition, not when alone. And he reasoned that it is obligatory, and obligation has no effect except in fulfilling the command. And this is more than can be enumerated. If it is said: Reasoning by effect is not qiyas because qiyas is only with an original case.
Source pages p. 594

Commentary
This passage is a demonstration of the Hanafi usul principle that 'ta'lil bi al-athar' (causation by an effective attribute) is a valid form of qiyas (analogical reasoning). The author, likely al-Bazdawi or a commentator, presents a series of examples from the Companions and later jurists to show that effective attributes ('ilal mu'aththira) can be derived directly from the effects of a ruling without explicit mention of an original case (asl). The first example is from the Prophet (peace be upon him) regarding rinsing the mouth: the water after rinsing is considered used (like filth) because it has been in the mouth, analogous to charity purifying sins—the effect (purification) is the effective attribute. Then the Companions' dispute on the grandfather's inheritance: they used analogies like tree branches and river tributaries, which are based on the effect of proximity in kinship, not on a specific asl. Umar's example of wine turning into vinegar shows that the transformation of nature (change) is the effective cause, not the original prohibition. Abu Hanifa's ruling on a jointly purchased slave who is a relative of one: freeing him with consent removes liability, because consent is an effective cause in dropping aggression. Muhammad al-Shaybani ruled that a deposit by a minor is valid because the minor authorized its consumption—the effect is the authorization. Then al-Shafi'i's views are cited: he uses explicit attributes (stoning for zina, praise for marriage) and the non-property nature of marriage, but the Hanafi author notes that these too are effective attributes. The Hanafi rulings on wiping the head (not repeating three times because the meaning of wiping itself implies lightness), marriage guardianship (based on inability, not virginity), and fasting Ramadan (intention needed only for specificity when competing with other fasts) all derive their causes from the effects of the rulings themselves, not from a literal original. Finally, an objection is anticipated: some say ta'lil bi al-athar is not qiyas because qiyas requires an asl. The passage implies that this objection is unfounded, as the examples show that the effective cause is sufficient for analogy. The commentary follows the author's order and only explains what is in the text.

Key Terms
التعليل بالأثر (al-ta'lil bi al-athar): Reasoning by an effective attribute derived from the effect or consequence of a ruling, without explicit reference to an original case.
معنى مؤثر (ma'na mu'aththir): An effective meaning or attribute that serves as the cause (illah) in legal reasoning, derived from the observable effect of the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the effective cause (ma'na mu'aththir) in the example of mouth rinsing?
A) The water becomes impure after contact with the mouth.
B) The act of rinsing is similar to charity.
C) The used water is like filth because charity purifies sins, so the effect (purification) is the cause.
D) The Prophet explicitly stated the cause.

Key Takeaway
Ta'lil bi al-athar is a valid form of qiyas in the Hanafi school, where the effective cause is derived from the ruling's own effect.

Ta'lil bi al-Athar as Valid Qiyas: The Hanafi Proof
Defending ta'lil bi al-athar as a legitimate form of qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafis consider ta'lil bi al-athar a valid form of qiyas and identify the key objections and responses.

Core Idea
A ruling's observable effect (athar) can itself reveal its cause, making ta'lil bi al-athar a valid qiyas without explicit mention of the original case.

The Hanafi school of usul al-fiqh recognizes a distinctive form of qiyas known as ta'lil bi al-athar—causation by effect. This method derives the effective cause (illa) of a ruling from the apparent characteristics of the act itself, even when no explicit original case (asl) is cited. In this passage, the author defends this method against Shafi'i objections, illustrating it with examples from wudu, marriage guardianship, and fasting. Understanding this argument is key to appreciating how Hanafi jurists expand rulings based on inferred causes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two Shafi'i rulings presented
Note al-Shafi'i's claims: zina does not cause prohibition of marriage by affinity, and marriage cannot be proved by women's testimony alone. Identify the reasoning behind each.
These serve as contrasting points to the Hanafi approach.
2
Extract the Hanafi counter-examples
Examine the three Hanafi examples: wiping the head (masah) – tripling not recommended because wiping implies lightening; guardianship in marriage (wilaya) – tied to incapacity not virginity; fasting Ramadan (sawm) – intention for specification only when overlap occurs. Identify the effective cause (al-mu'aththir) in each.
These examples show ta'lil bi al-athar in practice.
3
Analyze the objection and response
Consider the objection: ta'lil bi al-athar is not qiyas because qiyas requires an original case (asl). Study the Hanafi response: the effect itself presupposes an agreed original, as in the deposit example. Also note that even without explicit asl, the cause is valid and effectively qiyas.
The response clarifies that ta'lil bi al-athar is a subtype of qiyas.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference: Hanafis extend rulings based on inferred causes (e.g., prohibition from zina), while Shafi'is restrict such extensions requiring explicit asl.
This difference has widespread impact on furu' al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الشافعي رحمه الله في الزنا : لا يوجب حرمة المصاهرة؛ لأنه أمرٌ رُجمت عليه، والنكاح أمر حمدت عليه، وهذه أوصاف ظاهرة الآثار. وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : في النكاح لا يثبت بشهادة النساء والرجال؛ لأنه ليس بمال، ولذلك أثر في هذا الحكم؛ لأن المال هو المبتذل، فاحتيج فيه إلى الحجة الضرورية. وأما ما ليس بمال فغيرُ مبتذل فيجب إثباته بالحجة الأصلية، وليزداد خطره على ما هو مبتذل. وعلى هذا الأصل جَرَيْنا في الفروع. فقلنا في مسح الرأس: إنه مسح، فلا يُسن تثليثه، كمسح الخف ؛ لأن معنى المسح معنى مؤثر في التخفيف في فرضه، حتى لم يستوعب محله، ففي سنته أولى. فأما قول الخصم : إنه ركن في الوضوء : فغير مؤثر في إبطال التخفيف. وعللنا في ولاية النكاح بالصَّغَر ،والبلوغ، وهو المؤثر؛ لأنها ما شرعت إلا حقاً للعاجز، كالنفقة ، فصح التعليل بالعجز والقدرة للوجود والعدم، ولم يكن للبكارة والثيابة في ذلك أثر . وقلنا في صوم رمضان إنه عين، وهذا مؤثر؛ لأن النية للتعيين والتمييز، وذلك يُحتاج إلى ذكره عند المزاحمة، دون الانفراد. وعلَّل بأنه فرض، ولا أثر للفرضية إلا في إصابة المأمور. وهذا أكثر من أن يُحصى. فإن قيل : التعليل بالأثر لا يكون قياساً؛ لأنه لا قياس إلا بالأصل. قيل له: الأثر لا يكون إلا بأصل مُجمع عليه، مثل قولنا في إيداع الصبي إنه سلطه على استهلاكه ؛ لأن أصله إباحة الطعام. على أنا نسمي ما لا أصل له علةً شرعية، لا قياساً، والصحيح أنه قياس، على ما قلنا لكنه مسكوت عنه؛ لوضوحه، والله أعلم.
And al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, said regarding zina: It does not cause the prohibition of marriage by affinity because it is a matter for which stoning is prescribed, while marriage is a matter for which praise is given, and these are descriptions whose effects are apparent. And al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, said regarding marriage: It is not established by the testimony of women and men because it is not property, and that has an effect in this ruling because property is freely dealt with, so there is need for a necessary proof. As for what is not property, it is not freely dealt with, so it must be established by the original proof, so that its importance increases over what is freely dealt with. And upon this principle we have proceeded in the sub-branches. So we said regarding wiping the head: It is wiping, so tripling it is not recommended, like wiping the khuff, because the meaning of wiping is an effective meaning in lightening its obligation, such that it does not encompass its place, so in its recommended act it is more fitting. As for the adversary's statement: It is a pillar of ablution, that is not effective in invalidating the lightening. And we have reasoned regarding guardianship in marriage by being a minor and reaching puberty, and that is the effective cause because it was only legislated as a right for the incapable, like maintenance, so reasoning by incapacity and capability is sound for existence and absence, and there is no effect of virginity or defloration in that. And we said regarding fasting Ramadan that it is specific, and this is effective because intention is for specification and discernment, and that needs to be mentioned when there is overlap, not when alone. And he reasoned that it is obligatory, and there is no effect of obligatoriness except in performing the commanded. And this is more than can be counted. If it is said: Reasoning by effect is not qiyas because there is no qiyas except with an original case. It is said to him: The effect only exists with an original case that is agreed upon, like our statement regarding the deposit of a child that he is empowered to consume it because its original case is the permissibility of food. Moreover, we call what has no original case a legal cause, not qiyas, and the correct view is that it is qiyas, as we said, but it is unmentioned due to its clarity. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 595

Commentary
This passage is an argument defending the Hanafi method of legal reasoning known as ta'lil bi al-athar (causation by effect) as a valid form of qiyas. The author first presents two rulings of al-Shafi'i to contrast with the Hanafi approach: (1) Al-Shafi'i holds that zina does not create prohibition of marriage by affinity because zina is a crime punished by stoning, while marriage is commendable; the Hanafi position, relying on the effect (athar) of zina in causing prohibition, disagrees. (2) Al-Shafi'i argues that marriage cannot be proven by the testimony of women alone because it is not property; property is commonly transacted so it requires stringent proof, whereas non-property matters need original proof. The author states that Hanafis follow a different principle: they consider the effective cause (al-mu'aththir) in each ruling. Three examples are given: (a) Wiping the head (masah) in wudu: because it is wiping, tripling is not recommended, as with wiping the khuff; the ruling is based on the essence of wiping which implies lightening, even though it is a pillar. (b) Guardianship in marriage: it is tied to incapacity (minority) not to virginity, because guardianship is a right for the incapable. (c) Fasting Ramadan: intention is required for specification only when there is competition (overlap with other fasts); when alone, no specific intention is needed. The author then answers an objection: some claim that ta'lil bi al-athar is not qiyas because qiyas requires an original (asl). The Hanafi response is that the effect (athar) itself presupposes an agreed original case, as in the example of a child's deposit where consumption is allowed based on the original permissibility of food. Even if no explicit original exists, the author holds that such a cause is still a valid legal cause and is effectively qiyas, though it may be left unmentioned due to its obviousness.

Key Terms
الأثر (al-athar): The effective cause or legal effect that indicates a ruling; the observable consequence used to infer the underlying cause.
التعليل بالأثر (al-ta'lil bi al-athar): Legal reasoning that identifies the cause (illa) of a ruling through its apparent effect rather than a textual indication.
التثليث (al-tathlith): Performing an act three times, here referring to wiping the head three times in wudu.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because wiping is a pillar of wudu and pillars cannot be tripled.
B) Because the nature of wiping implies lightening, so tripling contradicts that.
C) Because the Prophet never wiped his head three times.
D) Because tripling is only recommended in washing, not wiping.

Key Takeaway
Ta'lil bi al-athar is a valid Hanafi qiyas method where the effect of a ruling reveals its cause, even without explicit mention of an original case.

The 'Illa Without an Original: Still Qiyas
Clarifying that a cause without a known original is still qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a cause ('illa) without an explicit original (asl) is still considered qiyas in Hanafi usul, and apply this to the example of a child consuming a deposit.

Core Idea
A cause lacking an explicit textual original remains valid qiyas; its clarity does not exclude it from analogical reasoning.

This passage addresses a key distinction in Hanafi usul: some scholars argue that a cause without a known original (asl) is merely a 'shar'i cause' and not qiyas. The author rejects this, affirming that such a cause is indeed qiyas, even if the analogy is so clear that it goes unmentioned. The example of a child being allowed to consume a deposit—by analogy to the general permissibility of food—illustrates this principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing claim
The passage begins with a reported objection: an effect (athar) requires an agreed-upon original (asl). Some scholars therefore say that a cause without a textual original should be called a shar'i cause, not qiyas.
2
State the correct Hanafi position
The author then declares that the correct view is that such a cause is indeed qiyas, though it may be left unspoken due to its obviousness.
3
Analyse the exemplary case
The example given: a child is allowed to consume a deposit (wadi'a) because he is empowered to do so; the original (asl) for this is the general permissibility of food (ibahah al-ta'am).
The general permissibility serves as an implicit asl, even if not a specific text.
4
Reconstruct the qiyas structure
Asl: general permission of food. Far': the child's consumption of the deposit. 'Illa: the empowerment (or need) to consume. Hukm al-asl: permissibility. Hukm al-far': permissibility. This is a valid qiyas even though the asl is a general principle rather than a detailed textual ruling.
5
Conclude the lesson
Thus, a cause without a specific textual asl is still a valid qiyas, provided it can be traced to a recognized shar'i principle. The Hanafi school accepts such reasoning as part of its usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل له: الأثر لا يكون إلا بأصل مُجمع عليه، مثل قولنا في إيداع الله و الصبي إنه سلطه على استهلاكه؛ لأن أصله إباحة الطعام. على أنا نسمي ما لا أصل له علةً شرعية، لا قياساً، والصحيح أنه قياس، على ما قلنا لكنه مسكوت عنه؛ لوضوحه، والله أعلم.
It was said to him: The effect (athar) does not exist except with an original (asl) that is agreed upon, like our statement regarding the deposit of Allah and the child: that He empowered him to consume it, because its original is the permission of food. However, we call that which has no original a shar‘i cause, not qiyas. And the correct view is that it is qiyas, as we have said, but it is unspoken due to its clarity. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 596

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the classification of a legal cause ('illa) that lacks a known original (asl) in the primary sources. The author responds to an objection by giving an example: the ruling that a child is allowed to consume a deposit (wadi'a) because its original is the general permissibility of food—this is a valid qiyas even though the specific cause is not explicitly stated. Some scholars label such a cause as a 'shar‘i cause' and deny it is qiyas, but the correct Hanafi position is that it is indeed qiyas, albeit obvious and therefore not explicitly mentioned. This clarifies that the absence of an explicitly stated asl does not remove it from the category of qiyas.

Key Terms
علة شرعية (‘illa shar‘iyya): A cause for a ruling that is derived from shar‘ but does not have a specific textual original (asl).
أصل (asl): The original case or textual basis from which a ruling is extended via qiyas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It is qiyas, even if obvious.
C) It is invalid and cannot be used.
D) It is a form of istihsan.

Key Takeaway
A cause without an explicit textual original is still valid qiyas in Hanafi usul, provided it is grounded in a recognized shar‘i principle.

Tard (Coextensiveness) in Qiyas: Definition and Validity
Definition and status of tard in qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three definitions of tard, state the Hanafi position that tard is not a proof, and evaluate the opposing arguments.

Core Idea
Tard (coextensiveness) alone is not sufficient proof for the validity of a legal cause in Hanafi usul.

This passage addresses the second thesis (al-maqala al-thaniya) regarding tard in qiyas. The author begins by firmly rejecting the use of tard as a valid proof, calling it a sign of deficiency. He then explains that the proponents of tard agree it is a proof of validity, but they differ in its interpretation. Understanding this disagreement is crucial for grasping the Hanafi method of 'illa verification, which requires more than mere coextensiveness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's thesis
State the author's initial ruling on arguing by tard: it is arguing by what is not a proof or evidence.
This sets the tone for the entire discussion.
2
Understand the proponents' agreement
What do the proponents of tard (ahl hadhihi al-maqala) agree upon? They agree that tard is a proof of validity.
Despite their agreement, they differ on the precise definition.
3
Distinguish the three definitions of tard
List the three interpretations: (1) existence of the description when the ruling exists; (2) adding absence when ruling is absent; (3) the text remains in both states without a ruling.
The third definition is the most inclusive.
4
Analyze the proponents' argument
What is their proof? They argue that the proofs of qiyas do not favor one description over another, so every description is like a textual indicator, and because 'ilal are signs.
This is a rational argument, not based on a specific text.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi response
Why does the author reject tard as a proof? He calls it deficient and says it is arguing by what is not a proof. The Hanafi school requires additional methods like munasabah (suitability) to validate a cause.
The author does not provide a detailed rebuttal here, but the rejection is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب بيان المقالة الثانية وتقسيم وجوهه، وهو الطرد اعلم بأن الاحتجاج بالطرد احتجاج بما ليس بدليل ولا حجة، ومَن عن طريق الفقه إلى الصورة: أفضى به تقصيره إلى أن قال: لا دليل على الحكم يصلح دليلاً، وكفى به فساداً. والكلام في الباب قسمان : قسم: في بيان الحجة. والثاني: في تقسيم الجملة. وقد اتفق أهل هذه المقالة أن الاطراد دليل الصحة. لكنهم اختلفوا في تفسيره فقال بعضُهم : هو الوجود عند الوجود في الأصول. وزاد بعضهم : العدم مع العدم أيضاً. وزاد بعضهم : أن يكون النص قائماً في الحالين، ولا حكم له. واحتجوا جميعاً بأن دلائل صحة القياس لا تخص وصفاً دون وصف، وكل وصف بمنزلة نص من النصوص ؛ ولأن علل الشرع أمارات
Chapter: Explanation of the second thesis and division of its aspects, and it is tard (coextensiveness). Know that arguing by tard is arguing by what is not a proof or evidence. And whoever [takes] the path of fiqh to the form, his deficiency leads him to say: There is no proof for the ruling that is suitable as a proof. Sufficient it is as corruption. The discussion in this chapter is two parts: Part on the explanation of the proof, and the second on the division of the sentence. The proponents of this thesis have agreed that tard is a proof of validity. However, they differed in its interpretation. Some said: It is existence with existence in the principles (usul). Some added: Non-existence with non-existence as well. Some added: That the text remains in both states, and no ruling is attached to it. And they all argued that the proofs of the validity of qiyas do not specify one description over another, and every description is like a text among texts; and because the causes (ilal) of the Shar‘ are signs.
Source pages p. 597

Commentary
This passage is an usul dispute regarding the concept of 'tard' (coextensiveness) as a proof for the validity of a legal cause ('illa) in qiyas. The author opens by firmly rejecting tard as a valid proof, calling it a sign of deficiency. He then divides the chapter into two parts: one explaining the proof, and the other dividing the sentence. The proponents of tard (called 'ahl hadhihi al-maqala') agree that tard is a proof of validity, but differ in defining it. Three definitions are given: (1) the description exists whenever the ruling exists in the original cases; (2) adding that it is absent whenever the ruling is absent; (3) that the text (of the ruling) remains applicable in both states but with no ruling assigned. Their proof is that the evidences for the validity of qiyas do not favor one description over another, and that all descriptions are like textual indicators. The author, however, maintains that this is insufficient. This reflects a fundamental disagreement in usul about whether coextensiveness alone validates a cause.

Key Terms
الطرد (al-tard): Coextensiveness: the existence of a description whenever the ruling exists (and some add absence with absence).
المقالة (al-maqala): The school or thesis (here referring to the proponents of tard as a proof).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the author's initial position on arguing by tard?
A) It is a valid proof.
B) It is arguing by what is not a proof.
C) It is the most correct method.
D) It is required for all qiyas.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, coextensiveness (tard) is not a sufficient proof for the validity of a legal cause; it is merely a condition.

The Proponents' Claim for Tard as a Proof for the Cause
Presentation of the proponents' argument for tard

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the proponents' argument for tard as a proof for the illa and identify the three definitions of tard.

Core Idea
Proponents claim any consistent attribute is a valid cause for a ruling, but Hanafis require a rational connection.

This lesson introduces the first part of the usul dispute on tard (consistency). The proponents argue that if an attribute always accompanies a ruling, that proves it is the cause. They define tard differently but agree it is a proof. We will examine their arguments before analyzing the Hanafi refutation in the next lesson.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
State that the proponents argue tard (consistency) is a valid proof for the cause in qiyas. Then note the three different interpretations of tard.
The three interpretations: existence at existence, existence and non-existence, and existence with text standing.
2
Extract the proponents' collective evidence
Read the passage where they argue: 'Proofs of qiyas do not specify attributes' and 'Shariah causes are indicators (amarat), not necessitating, so no rational meaning is needed.'
Their argument relies on the idea that any attribute can be a cause if consistent.
3
Understand the 'amarat' reasoning
Note that proponents claim Shariah causes are mere signs, not rationally necessarily linked to rulings. Thus, any consistent attribute qualifies.
This implies humans do not need to understand the rationale behind causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب بيان المقالة الثانية وتقسيم وجوهه، وهو الطرد. اعلم بأن الاحتجاج بالطرد احتجاج بما ليس بدليل ولا حجة، ومن عن طريق الفقه إلى الصورة: أفضى به تقصيره إلى أن قال: لا دليل على الحكم يصلح دليلاً، وكفى به فساداً. والكلام في الباب قسمان : قسم: في بيان الحجة. والثاني: في تقسيم الجملة. وقد اتفق أهل هذه المقالة أن الاطراد دليل الصحة. لكنهم اختلفوا في تفسيره فقال بعضُهم : هو الوجود عند الوجود في الأصول. وزاد بعضهم : العدم مع العدم أيضاً. وزاد بعضهم : أن يكون النص قائماً في الحالين، ولا حكم له. واحتجوا جميعاً بأن دلائل صحة القياس لا تخص وصفاً دون وصف، وكل وصف بمنزلة نص من النصوص ؛ ولأن علل الشرع أمارات غير موجبة، فلا حاجة بنا إلى معنى يُعقل. والجواب : أن الشرع جعل الأصل شاهداً، وذلك لا يقتضي الشهادة من كل وصف، كما جعل كامل الحال من الناس شاهداً، ثم لم يجب أن يكون كل لفظ شهادةً إلا بمعنى معقول يوجب تمييزاً. وأما قوله : إنها أمارات فكذلك في حق الله تعالى، فأما في حق العباد فلا، فإنهم مُبْتَلَوْن بنسبة الأحكام إلى العلل، كما نُسبت الأجزيةُ إلى أفعالهم، ونُسب الملك إلى البيع، والقصاص إلى القتل، وما يجري مجراه، فكانت غير موجبةٍ في الأصل، ولكنها جعلت موجِبةً شرعاً في حقنا، على ما يليق بها، وهو النسبة. أليس أنه وجب القصاص على القاتل وقد مات القتيل بأجله، وإذا كان كذلك : لم يكن بد من التمييز بين العلل والشروط، ومجرد الاطراد لا يميز. وكذلك العدم عند عدمه ؛ لأنه يزاحمه الشرط فيه، ولأن نهاية الطرد الجهلُ ؛ لأنه يُقال له : وما يدريك أنه لم يبق له أصل مناقض أو معارض، وهل ثبت ذلك لك إلا بأن وقفت عن الطلب، وقد كان يتأتّى لك ذلك قبل الطرد ؟ ! وأما العدم : فليس بشيء، فلا يصلح دليلاً، وكيف يصلح مع احتمال أن يثبت بعلةٍ أخرى، فلا يصلح شرط عدمه، ألا يُرى أن مثل هذا لا يوجد في علل السلف. وأما من شرط أن يكون النص قائماً في الحالين، ولا حكم له: فقد احتج بآية الوضوء، وبقول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: «لا يقضي القاضي
Chapter: Explanation of the second statement and division of its aspects, which is consistency (tard). Know that arguing by consistency is arguing by what is not a proof or evidence, and whoever [takes] the path of fiqh to the form, his deficiency led him to say: there is no proof for the ruling that is suitable as evidence, and sufficient is its corruption. The discussion in the chapter is two parts: Part on explaining the evidence, and second on division of the sentence. Those who hold this view have agreed that consistency is a proof of validity, but they differed in its interpretation. Some said: it is existence at existence in the [legal] principles. Some added: non-existence at non-existence as well. Some added: that the text stands in both states, and no ruling for it. They all argued that the proofs of validity of qiyas do not specify one attribute over another, and every attribute is like a text among texts; and because the causes of the Shariah are indicators (amarat) that are not necessitating, so we have no need for a rational meaning. The answer: The Shariah made the original (asl) a witness, and that does not necessitate witness from every attribute, just as it made an upright person from among people a witness, then it is not necessary that every word be a witness except with a rational meaning that necessitates distinction. As for his saying: they are indicators, that is so with respect to Allah, but with respect to servants it is not; for they are tested by attributing rulings to causes, just as requitals are attributed to their actions, and ownership is attributed to sale, and retaliation to killing, and what runs in their course. So they were not necessitating in origin, but they were made necessitating legally in our regard, in a manner befitting them, which is attribution. Is it not that retaliation became obligatory upon the killer while the killed died at his appointed time? If so, there is no escape from distinguishing between causes and conditions, and mere consistency does not distinguish. Similarly, non-existence at its non-existence, because the condition interferes with it, and because the end of consistency is ignorance; for it is said to him: How do you know that there remains no contradictory or opposing origin? And is that established for you except by your stopping from seeking, and that was possible for you before consistency?! As for non-existence: it is nothing, so it is not suitable as a proof, and how can it be suitable when it is possible that it is established by another cause? So its non-existence is not suitable as a condition. Do you not see that such is not found in the causes of the predecessors? As for the one who stipulated that the text stands in both states, and no ruling for it: he argued with the verse of ablution, and with the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'The judge should not judge...'
Source pages p. 597

Commentary
This passage is a classic usul dispute about the validity of 'tard' (consistency) in identifying the cause (illa) for qiyas. The author, Imam al-Bazdawi, states that arguing by tard is arguing without evidence and is corrupt. He notes that proponents of tard agree it is a proof for validity but differ on its interpretation: some define it as existence with existence in the principles, others add non-existence with non-existence, and others stipulate that the text applies in both states without a ruling. Their collective argument is that proofs of qiyas do not privilege one attribute over another, and since Shariah causes are mere indicators (amarat) and not necessitating, no rational explanation is needed. The Hanafi response refutes this: the Shariah made the original (asl) a witness, but not every attribute is a witness—only those with a rational basis for distinction. Regarding the claim that causes are amarat, this holds for Allah's knowledge, but for humans, causes are made legally necessitating (like retaliation attached to killing). The author illustrates with the example of qisas: it is obligatory on the killer even though the victim died at his appointed time, showing that mere consistency does not distinguish between true cause and condition. The discussion also critiques the condition of non-existence by noting that it is impossible to prove non-existence absolutely, and the condition of the text standing in both states is refuted by examples from ablution and the Prophet's hadith. The passage firmly establishes the Hanafi position that tard is an insufficient and invalid method for proving the illa.

Key Terms
الطرد (al-tard): Consistency: the existence of a ruling with an attribute in all cases, used by some as proof for the validity of that attribute as a cause.
العلة (al-illa): The cause or effective attribute that necessitates a ruling in qiyas, according to Hanafi usul, must be rationally meaningful and consistent but not merely consistent.
الأمارة (al-amarah): An indicator or sign that points to a ruling but does not necessarily cause it in reality; used by proponents to argue that causes need not be rationally necessitating.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main reason proponents of tard give for not needing a rational meaning for the cause?
A) Because qiyas is based on ijtihad, not reason.
B) Because Shariah causes are indicators (amarat) that are not rationally necessitating.
C) Because the Quran and Sunnah do not mention rational causes.
D) Because all attributes are equally likely to be causes.

Key Takeaway
Tard (consistency) alone is not a sufficient proof for establishing a cause in Hanafi usul; the proponents' view that no rational meaning is needed is rejected.

The Hanafi Refutation: The Insufficiency of Tard
Hanafi refutation of tard using witness analogy and qisas example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi response to the tard argument, using the witness analogy and the qisas example to show why mere consistency cannot distinguish cause from condition.

Core Idea
Mere consistency cannot distinguish between a cause and a condition, as shown by qisas being obligatory despite the victim's appointed death.

This lesson presents the Hanafi refutation of tard. The response uses a powerful analogy: the Shariah made the original (asl) a witness, but not every attribute testifies without rational qualification. The qisas example illustrates that consistency alone fails to separate true causes from mere conditions, such as the appointed time of death.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi response's opening claim
The author responds that the Shariah made the asl a witness, but not every attribute serves as a witness; just as in human testimony, only qualified persons can testify, attributes need rational qualification.
This counters the proponents' claim that any attribute is like a text.
2
Analyze the response to the 'amarat' argument
The author distinguishes between Allah's knowledge (where causes are amarat) and human duty: humans must attribute rulings to causes with rational connection, as seen in sale, retaliation, etc.
Causes are made necessitating legally for humans, even if not in reality.
3
Examine the qisas example
The killer is subject to qisas even though the victim died at his appointed time. This shows that consistency (killing always leads to qisas) does not distinguish between cause (killing) and condition (time of death). Mere tard cannot differentiate.
This is the core proof: tard alone cannot separate illa from shart.
4
Understand the critique of non-existence and text-standing
The author argues that non-existence at non-existence is problematic because the condition interferes, and absolute non-existence is unknowable. The condition of text standing is refuted by examples like ablution verse and judge's hadith.
These additional points strengthen the refutation.

The author, Imam al-Bazdawi, states that arguing by tard is arguing without evidence and is corrupt. الطرد (al-tard): Consistency: the existence of a ruling with an attribute in all cases, used by some as proof for the validity of that attribute as a cause.
العلة (al-illa): The cause or effective attribute that necessitates a ruling in qiyas, according to Hanafi usul, must be rationally meaningful and consistent but not merely consistent.
الشرط (al-shart): A condition that is necessary for the ruling but not its cause; e.g., the appointed time of death is a condition, not the cause of qisas.

B) Because tard cannot distinguish between a true cause and a condition, as shown by the qisas example.

Key Takeaway
Tard (mere consistency) is insufficient to establish a cause in Hanafi usul; it must be accompanied by rational appropriateness (munasib).

Classification of Arguments in Usul al-Fiqh: The Seven Types
Listing of argument types with initial critique of ittiraad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the seven types of arguments listed in the passage and explain why ittiraad alone is insufficient as a proof in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Validity of an argument is not determined by frequency or repetition.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's usul work enumerates a classification of arguments (adilla) used in legal reasoning. The author lists seven types, from the most to least probative, beginning with ittiraad (consistency) and ending with the argument of no evidence. We will parse each type and understand the Hanafi critique of the first type in particular.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sequential structure
The author uses 'the first', 'next' etc. to list seven types. Note the descending order of strength or validity.
The text uses Arabic ordinal phrases: 'فإن أول أقسامه', 'والذي يليه' etc.
2
Define each of the seven types
From the passage: 1) Ittiraad (consistency), 2) Istishab al-hal (presumption of continuity), 3) Ta'arud al-ashbah (conflict of similarities), 4) Argument that does not stand alone except by a distinguishing description, 5) Argument that the description is clearly different, 6) That which is undoubtedly invalid, 7) Argument that there is no evidence.
These correspond to typical categories of qiyas and istidlal.
3
Analyze the first type: Ittiraad
The author states that ittiraad is established only by a large number of witnesses or recurring testimony, but validity of testimony is not known by multitude or repetition. This critiques using mere frequency as a proof.
Ittiraad here refers to consistency of an attribute in qiyas; the Hanafi position is that it is necessary but not sufficient.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi stance
The Hanafi school holds that ittiraad is a condition (shart) for a valid illah but not a proof of its existence. Without additional evidence, sheer recurrence does not establish legal causation.
This distinguishes Hanafi usul from some other schools that may give ittiraad more weight.
5
Consider the incomplete explanation
The text cuts off at 'but...' (بل). Reflect on what further critique might follow: perhaps the necessity of a sabab or hikmah, or the difference between ittiraad and tard.
In later works, ittiraad is often contrasted with 'aks (counterevidence) and discussed in relation to tanqih al-manat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تقسيم هذه الجملة: 1 - فإن أول أقسامه: الاطراد، وجوداً، أو وجوداً وعدماً. 2- والذي يليه: الاحتجاج باستصحاب الحال. 3- والذي يليه: الاحتجاج بتعارض الأشباه. 4- والذي يليه: الاحتجاج بما لا يستقل إلا بوصف يقع به الفرق. 5- والذي يليه: الاحتجاج بأن يكون الوصف مختلفاً ظاهر الاختلاف. 6- والذي يليه: ما لا يُشك في فساده. 7- والذي يليه: الاحتجاج بأن لا دليل. أما الأول: فلأن الاطرادَ لا يثبت به إلا كثرة الشهود، أو كثرة أداء الشهادة، وصحة الشهادة لا تُعرف بكثرة العدد ولا بتكرير العبارة، بل
As for the division of this sentence: 1 - The first of its divisions is consistency (ittiraad), in existence, or in existence and non-existence. 2 - Next is argument using istishab al-hal (presumption of continuity). 3 - Next is argument using conflict of similarities. 4 - Next is argument using that which does not stand alone except by a description that effects distinction. 5 - Next is argument that the description is clearly different. 6 - Next is that which there is no doubt about its invalidity. 7 - Next is argument that there is no evidence. As for the first: because consistency is established only by a large number of witnesses, or by a large number of testimonies, and the validity of testimony is not known by the multitude of number nor by repetition of expression, but...
Source pages p. 600

Commentary
This passage enumerates a classification of arguments (possibly in the context of qiyas or istidlal). The author lists seven types: 1) Ittiraad (consistency of the description across cases), 2) Istishab al-hal (presumption of continuity of a state), 3) Ta'arud al-ashbah (conflict of similar evidences), 4) Argument that relies solely on a distinguishing description, 5) Argument that the description is clearly different, 6) That which is undoubtedly invalid, and 7) Argument that there is no evidence. The author then begins to explain the first type: ittiraad is established only by a large number of witnesses or recurring testimony, but the validity of testimony is not known by sheer number or repetition. The text cuts off at 'بل' (but...), indicating the explanation continues beyond the window.

Key Terms
الاطراد (al-ittiraad): The consistency of a description across all cases; used as a criterion for validity of an illah.
استصحاب الحال (istishab al-hal): Presumption of continuity of a prior state until a change is proven.
تعارض الأشباه (ta'arud al-ashbah): Conflict of similar evidences; when two seemingly analogous cases yield contradictory indications.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first type of argument in the classification?
A) Istishab al-hal
B) Ittiraad
C) Ta'arud al-ashbah
D) Argument with no evidence

Key Takeaway
Ittiraad (consistency) is a necessary condition for a valid illah but not a proof; validity is not established by frequency or repetition.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

Reasoning by Negation is Not a Proof for Establishment
Explaining Hanafi rule that negation cannot prove a ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the Hanafi rule that al-ta'lil bi al-nafi is not a valid proof for establishing a ruling, and distinguish it from valid qiyas.

Core Idea
Negation is not a cause—absence does not establish presence.

In this lesson, we examine a fundamental Hanafi usul principle: reasoning by negation (al-ta'lil bi al-nafi) cannot serve as a proof for establishing a positive ruling. Al-Bazdawi presents the rational argument and contrasts it with Shafi'i examples. Understanding this principle helps you avoid a common logical error in legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
State the Hanafi rule: negation is not a proof for establishment (ithbat). It is only a defensive argument (hujja dafi'a).
2
Trace the rational argument
Al-Bazdawi argues: existence of a thing is not a cause for its own continuation, so how can it be a cause for another thing's existence? Similarly, the absence of a condition cannot prove a ruling.
This is a qiyas argument: analogy from self to other.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i examples
Examine the examples given: marriage not proven by women's testimony because it is not property; brother not freed because no parentage; irrevocably divorced not affected by divorce because no marriage. These appear like causes but are mere negations.
4
Understand the Hanafi response
Negation does not prevent existence from another cause unless the ruling is unique with a consensus proof. Marriage is not like property; it can be established by women's testimony with doubts.
Imam Muhammad's examples (usurped child, khums) are exceptions where negation works because the ruling is unique.
5
Apply the principle
Given a modern claim: 'This contract is valid because it does not involve riba.' Apply the Hanafi rule: absence of riba alone does not establish validity; a positive cause must be identified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بأهلية الشاهد وعدالته واختصاص أدائه ولأن الوجود قد يكون اتفاقاً، والعدمَ قد يقع ؛ لأنه شرط. ألا يُرى أن وجودَ الشيء ليس بعلة لبقائه، فكيف يصلح علةً للوجود في غيره بنفسه ؟! وكذلك وجودُ الحكم ولا علة لا يصلح دليلاً لجواز وجوده بغيره، ووجود العلة ولا حكم بنفسه : لا يصلح مناقضاً؛ لجواز أن يقف الحكم لفَوْت وصف من العلة ليس بعلة بنفسه، فلا يكون مناقضة، ولا ذكره وقد دل عليه التعليل تخصيصاً، على ما نُبيّن إن شاء الله تعالى، إلا أن هذا على نهج العلل ظاهراً، فكان مقدماً في أقسامه، ثم التعليل بالنفي. مثل قول الشافعي رحمه الله في النكاح لا يثبت بشهادة النساء الرجال ؛ لأنه ليس بمال. وفي الأخ: لا يعتق؛ لأنه ليس بينهما بعضية. ولا يلحق المبتوتة طلاق؛ لأنه لا نكاح بينهما. مع ويجوز إسلام المروي في المَرْوي؛ لأنهما مالان لا يجمعهما طُعم ولا ثمنية وهذا في الظاهر خرج على مثال العلل، لكنه لما كان عدماً : لم يكن شيئاً، فلا يصلح حجةً للإثبات. و ألا ترى أن استقصاء العدم : لا يمنعُ الوجود من وجه آخر إلا أن يقع الاختلافُ في حكم بسبب معين. وفي حكم ثبت دليله بالإجماع ،واحداً، لا ثاني له، مثل قول محمد رحمه الله في ولد الغصب ؛ لأنه لم يغصب الولد. و ومثل قوله فيما لا خُمس فيه من اللؤلؤ ؛ لأنه لم يوجف عليه المسلمون بخيل ولا ركاب ؛ لأن ذلك الخُمس لا يوجد بغيره. فأما قوله : ليس بمال : فلا يَمنع قيامَ وصف له أثر في صحة الإثبات أن النكاح من جنس ما لا يسقط الرجال، وهو بشهادة النساء مع بالشبهات، بل هو من جنس ما يثبت بها، فصار فوق الأموال في هذا بدرجة، وكذلك في أخواتها، على ما عرف.
By the competence of the witness, his integrity, and the specificity of his testimony, and because existence may be coincidental, and non-existence may occur because it is a condition. Do you not see that the existence of a thing is not a cause for its own continuation, so how can it be a cause for existence in another thing by itself? Likewise, the existence of a ruling without a cause is not valid as a proof for the possibility of its existence by something else, and the existence of a cause without a ruling by itself is not valid as a contradiction; because the ruling may be delayed due to the loss of a description of the cause that is not a cause by itself, so there is no contradiction, nor its mention when the ta'lil indicates specification, as we will explain if Allah wills, except that this is on the pattern of causes apparently, so it is prioritized in its divisions, then reasoning by negation. For example, the statement of al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, concerning marriage: it is not established by the testimony of men and women because it is not property. And concerning the brother: he does not become free because there is no parentage between them. And the divorced irrevocably is not affected by divorce because there is no marriage between them. And it is permissible to sell the white silver coin for the white silver coin because they are two properties that are not combined by food nor by monetary value, and this apparently came out on the model of causes, but since it was non-existence, it was not a thing, so it is not valid as a proof for establishment. Do you not see that exhaustive negation does not prevent existence from another perspective except when a difference occurs in a ruling due to a specific cause. And concerning a ruling whose proof is established by consensus, singular, having no second, like the statement of Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, concerning the child of usurpation: because he did not usurp the child. And like his statement concerning what has no khums from pearls: because the Muslims did not campaign upon it with horses and saddles; because that khums is not found otherwise. As for his statement 'it is not property': it does not prevent the existence of a description that has an effect on the validity of establishment, that marriage is of the type that is not dropped by men, and it is by the testimony of women with doubts, but rather it is of the type that is established by it, so it became above properties in this by one degree, and likewise for its sisters, as is known.
Source pages p. 601

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul presents the Hanafi principle that reasoning by negation (al-ta'lil bi al-nafi) is not a valid proof for establishing a ruling. The author first explains a general logical principle: the existence of a thing is not a cause for its own continuation, so it cannot be a cause for another thing's existence. Similarly, the absence of a condition (like 'not being property') does not prove a ruling (like 'marriage cannot be established by women's testimony'). The text then lists examples from Shafi'i who use such negative reasoning, and refutes them. A key point is that exhaustive negation does not prevent existence from another perspective unless the ruling is unique with a consensus proof. Examples from Imam Muhammad on usurpation and khums show that negation can be used only when the ruling is isolated. Finally, the author defends the Hanafi position on marriage testimony: marriage is not like property; it is established by women's testimony even with doubts, so the negative claim 'not property' does not negate that. Throughout, the emphasis is that negation is a defensive tool, not a method to establish rulings.

Key Terms
التعليل بالنفي (al-ta'lil bi al-nafi): Deducing a legal ruling based on the absence or negation of a quality (e.g., 'because it is not property').
العلة (al-illa): The effective cause (illat) that grounds a legal ruling via qiyas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position presented in this passage, is reasoning by negation (al-ta'lil bi al-nafi) a valid proof for establishing a ruling?
A) Yes, it is a valid proof.
B) No, it is not valid for establishment, only defensive.
C) It is valid only in cases of consensus.
D) It is valid only for negative rulings.

Key Takeaway
Al-ta'lil bi al-nafi is not a proof for establishing a ruling; it is only a defensive argument and cannot serve as a positive cause in qiyas.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

8 min
Evidence for Origin Does Not Guarantee Continuation
Argues that evidence establishing a ruling does not necessitate its continuation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that the evidence establishing a ruling does not necessitate its continuation, and distinguish rulings that are inherently perpetual (like purity and ownership) from those that are not.

Core Idea
Evidence for a ruling's origin does not guarantee its persistence; only inherently perpetual rulings remain by their own evidence.

This lesson addresses a foundational usul question: does the proof that first established a legal ruling also guarantee that ruling lasts forever? The Hanafi school says no, using the analogy of creation (ijad) not necessitating continued existence (ibqa'), and the possibility of abrogation (naskh) to prove that rulings are not inherently permanent by their initial proof. Understanding this distinction is critical for properly applying istishab and for resolving cases like the missing person, the conditional freeing of a slave, and the admission of freedom followed by purchase.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Question
Recognize the central issue: does the evidence that establishes a ruling (al-dalil al-mujib) also necessitate its continuance (al-baqa bi-dalilihi)? The text opens with a concrete case to frame this.
The case of the master and slave's conditional freedom sets the stage.
2
State the Opponent's Argument
The opponent (likely a Shafi'i) argues that the same evidence that establishes a ruling also keeps it in effect, citing Quranic rulings remaining after the Prophet, istishab in wudu/hadath, and examples of pre-emption and witness testimony.
Key examples: purity after certainty of wudu, ownership established by admission.
3
Extract the Hanafi Response
The Hanafi response uses the analogy of creation (ijad) not guaranteeing continued existence (ibqa), and points out that abrogation (naskh) would be impossible if every evidence guaranteed permanence. They argue that non-ownership does not prevent later ownership, and existing ownership does not prevent removal.
The key phrase: 'كالإيجاد لا يوجبُ البقاء، حتى صح الإفناء'.
4
Distinguish Inherently Perpetual Rulings
The text distinguishes rulings that are inherently perpetual (e.g., purity, ownership by purchase, marriage) from those that are not. Perpetual rulings remain by their own evidence because they cannot be timed; non-perpetual rulings (like the life of a missing person) require fresh evidence to continue.
Rulings like wudu and hadath do not admit timing, so they persist by their nature.
5
Apply the Principle to the Freedom Admission Case
The passage applies this to the case where a man admits a slave is free and then buys him: the purchase is valid (Hanafi) because the admission of freedom lacks an original basis (slavery is presumed), while the seller's claim of ownership has an original basis (ownership proven by his statement).
This illustrates the practical thamarah al-khilaf of the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك رجلٌ قال لعبده : إن لم تدخل الدار اليوم فأنت حر، فمضى اليوم ، ولا يُدرى أدخل أم لا ، ثم اختلفا فإن القول قول المولى عندنا ؛ لما ذكرنا.
واحتج بأن الحكم إذا ثبت بدليله : بقي بذلك الدليل أيضاً، ألا يُرى أن حكم النص يبقى به بعد وفاة النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام حتى تعذر نسخه.
واحتج بإجماعهم على أن من تيقّن بالوضوء: لم يلزمه وضوء آخر، ولزمه أداء الصلاة بما علمه وإن شك في الحدث.
وإذا علم بالحدث، ثم شك في الوضوء: بقي الحدث.
ولو ثبت ملك الشفيع بإقرار المشتري أنه كان له، أو أنه اشتراه من فلان وفلانٌ كان يملكه : وجبت الشفعة، وإنما يبقى ملكه لعدم ما يُزيله ، ومع ذلك قد صلح حجةً موجبة.
وكذلك لو شهد شهود المدعي أن هذا الشي كان ملكاً له: صار حجةً موجبة.
ولنا أن الدليل الموجب لحكم لا يوجب بقاءه، كالإيجاد لا يوجبُ البقاء، حتى صح الإفناء، وهذا لأن ذلك بمنزلة أعراض تحدث، فلا يصلح أن يكون وجود شيء علةً لوجود غيره.
ألا يُرى أن عدم الملك : لا يمنعُ الملك، وعدم الشراء: لا يمنع حدوث الشراء، ووجودَ الملك : لا يمنع الزوال، وهذا لا يُشكل، ألا يُرى أن النسخ في دلائل الشرع إنما صح لما ذكرنا.
ولما صارت الدلائل موجبةً قطعاً بوفاة النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام على تقريرها : لم تحتمل النسخ ؛ لبقائها بدليل موجب.
وأما فصل الطهارة والملك بالشراء، وما أشبه ذلك : فلا يُشبه هذا الباب، وذلك من جنس ما بقي بدليله ؛ لأن حكم الشراء الملك المؤبد. كذلك حكم النكاح، وكذلك حكم الوضوء والحدث، ألا يُرى أنه لا يصح توقيته صريحاً، لكنه يحتمل السقوط بالمعارضة على سبيل المناقضة، فقبل المعارِضُ له حكم التأبيد ، فكان البقاء بدليله، وكلامنا فيما ثبت بقاؤه بلا دليل كحياة المفقود.
وكذلك الأمر المطلق في حياة الرسول عليه الصلاة والسلام إنما يتناول حكماً يحتمل التوقيت، فيصيرُ في البقاء احتمال، فأما حكم الوضوء والطهارة، وحكم الحدث فلا يحتمل التوقيت.
ولذلك قلنا جميعاً في رجل أقر بحرية عبد، ثم اشتراه: إنه صحيح على اختلاف الأصلين. [يعدو؟] قائله ، أما عندنا فلما قلنا إن قول كل واحد من المتعاقدين لا وعلى قوله : قول البائع يرجع إلى ما عُرف بدليله، وهو الملك، فصار حجة على خصمه. وأما قول المشتري : إنه حر: فليس يرجع إلى أصل عرف بدليله، فلم يكن حجة على خصمه. ٣- وأما الاحتجاج بتعارض الأشباه فمثل قول زفر رحمه الله: إن غسل المرافق في الوضوء ليس بفرض؛ لأن من الغايات ما يدخل، ومنها
And likewise, a man said to his slave: 'If you do not enter the house today, you are free,' and the day passed, and it is not known whether he entered or not, then they differed. The statement of the master is accepted according to us, due to what we mentioned. And he argued that when a ruling is established by its evidence, it remains by that same evidence. Do you not see that the ruling of the text remains by it after the death of the Prophet, peace be upon him, until its abrogation becomes impossible? And he argued by their consensus that whoever is certain of ablution is not obligated to perform another ablution, and he is obligated to perform prayer with what he knows, even if he doubts about the event. And if he is certain of the event, then doubts about ablution, the event remains. And if the right of pre-emption is established by the purchaser's admission that it belonged to him, or that he bought it from so-and-so who owned it, then pre-emption is due, and his ownership remains due to the absence of anything that removes it, and yet it becomes a binding proof. And likewise, if the plaintiff's witnesses testify that this thing was his property, it becomes a binding proof. And we say that the evidence that necessitates a ruling does not necessitate its continuance, just as bringing into existence does not necessitate continued existence, until annihilation is valid. This is because that is like accidental attributes that occur, so the existence of one thing cannot be the cause of the existence of another. Do you not see that non-ownership does not prevent ownership, and non-purchase does not prevent the occurrence of purchase, and the existence of ownership does not prevent removal? This is not problematic. Do you not see that abrogation in religious evidences is only valid due to what we have mentioned? And when the evidences became definitively necessitating after the death of the Prophet, peace be upon him, upon their establishment, they did not admit abrogation, because they remain by a necessitating evidence. As for the case of purity and ownership by purchase and the like, these do not resemble this section. They are of the type that remains by its evidence, because the ruling of purchase is perpetual ownership. Likewise the ruling of marriage, and likewise the ruling of ablution and event. Do you not see that it cannot be explicitly timed, but it admits of being invalidated by opposition in a contradictory manner. So the opponent accepted its perpetual ruling, so its continuance is by its evidence. Our discussion concerns that whose continuance is established without evidence, like the life of the missing person. And likewise, the absolute command during the life of the Messenger, peace be upon him, only concerns a ruling that admits timing, so there is a possibility of continuance. As for the ruling of ablution and purity, and the ruling of event, they do not admit timing. And for that reason we all say regarding a man who admitted the freedom of a slave and then bought him: it is valid, despite the difference of the two principles. [?] its speaker. As for according to us, we say that the statement of each of the two contracting parties is not... and according to his statement: the statement of the seller refers to what is known by its evidence, which is ownership, so it becomes a proof against his opponent. As for the statement of the purchaser that he is free, it does not refer to an origin known by its evidence, so it was not a proof against his opponent. 3- And as for the argument by conflicting analogies, like the statement of Zufar, may Allah have mercy on him: that washing the elbows in ablution is not obligatory because some of the limits are included and some are not.
Source pages p. 603

Commentary
This passage is a sustained usul argument about the principle of continuity of rulings. It begins with a concrete fiqh example: a master tells his slave 'if you do not enter the house today, you are free.' The day passes, and neither knows if the slave entered. The Hanafi ruling is that the master's word is accepted (i.e., he is not liable for the slave's freedom) because the original freedom is not established without proof. This introduces the core issue: does the evidence that established a ruling also guarantee its continuance? The opponent argues yes, citing that Quranic rulings remain after the Prophet's death until abrogation, and using the principle of istishab (presumption of continuity) in purity and impurity. For example, certainty of wudu remains until doubt about hadath. He also uses the example of the pre-emptor's ownership being established by the buyer's admission, and witnesses' testimony. The Hanafi response is that the evidence (dalil) for a ruling's origin does not necessitate its persistence—just as creation (ijad) does not guarantee continued existence (ibqa'). They illustrate with the fact that non-ownership does not prevent later ownership, and existing ownership does not prevent its removal. They explain that abrogation is possible precisely because rulings are not inherently permanent by their initial evidence. The passage then distinguishes between rulings like purity and ownership which are inherently perpetual (they cannot be explicitly timed) and thus remain by their own evidence, versus cases like the missing person's life where continuance is presumed without evidence. Further, they distinguish the absolute command during the Prophet's life which admits timing (so its continuance is uncertain) from rulings like wudu and hadath which do not admit timing. Finally, they apply this to a case: if a man admits a slave is free and then buys him, the purchase is valid (Hanafi position) because the admission of freedom does not have an original basis (since slavery is presumed), whereas the seller's claim of ownership does. The passage ends with Zufar's opinion on wudu of elbows, using conflicting analogies (ta'arud al-ashbah) as an example of another usul argument.

Key Terms
الدليل الموجب (al-dalīl al-mūjib): The evidence that necessitates a ruling's existence at the moment of establishment.
البقاء بدليله (al-baqā' bi-dalīlihi): The continuation of a ruling by virtue of the same evidence that initially established it, which the text argues is not automatic except for inherently perpetual rulings.
النسخ (al-naskh): Abrogation of a ruling by a later evidence; possible only if the initial evidence does not necessitate continuance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why does the evidence that establishes a ruling not necessarily guarantee its continuation?
A) Because rulings are always temporary by nature.
B) Because the analogy with creation shows that bringing into existence does not ensure continued existence, as seen in the validity of annihilation.
C) Because the Prophet's death ended all legal continuity.
D) Because istishab only applies to acts of worship.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the evidence establishing a ruling does not automatically guarantee its continuance; only inherently perpetual rulings (like purity and ownership) remain by their own evidence.
