Invalid Ta'lil: The Description-Dependent Analogy
Critique of analogical reasoning relying on a differentiating description

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an invalid ta'lil that depends on a description requiring independent proof, and distinguish it from valid analogies.

Core Idea
An analogy fails if the description used as the cause is not independently proven.

In Hanafi usul, not every shared attribute between two cases makes a valid analogy. The attribute itself must be a recognized legal cause (illa) or at least one that can be independently established. This lesson examines a type of invalid ta'lil where the proposed 'illa' is merely a description that, to be accepted, would need its own separate proof—making the analogy circular or groundless. We will analyze the principle and its application through two concrete fiqh examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the invalid ta'lil pattern
Recognize that the author states a general rule: any ta'lil that 'does not stand except by a description by which the distinction occurs' is invalid. This means the analogy hinges on a description that itself is not a proven cause.
The description (wasf) is supposed to be the differentiating factor, but it requires its own proof.
2
Analyze the first example: touching the penis
The Shafi'i argument claims touching the penis is like touching it while urinating: both are 'touching the private part' and thus nullify wudu. The Hanafi critique points out that 'touching the private part' is not an independently established cause of hadath (ritual impurity).
The analogy relies on a description that is not a known legal cause; it merely asserts that because both involve touching a private part, they share a ruling. This is invalid because the description itself is not a valid illa.
3
Analyze the second example: freeing a mukatib
Some argue that a master cannot free a mukatib (a slave under contract of manumission) because he is like one who has partly paid the kitaba (where the master's right to free is blocked). The Hanafi response: the condition of having paid part creates a 'preventing substitute' (badal mani') only in the Hanafi view. The analogy depends on this disputed description, which is not independently agreed upon.
The description 'having paid part of the kitaba' is itself a matter of ikhtilaf; basing an analogy on it is invalid because the description is not a settled cause.
4
Extract the Hanafi rule
The rule: For a ta'lil to be valid, the description used must be an appropriate and proven cause (illa) that is established by sound evidence. If it requires a separate proof to be accepted as a cause, the ta'lil is invalid.
This is a key filtering tool in Hanafi usul to reject weak analogies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الذي لا يَستقل إلا بوصف يقع به الفرق: فباطل، مثل قول بعض أصحاب الشافعي رحمه الله في مس الذكر: إنه حدث؛ لأنه مس الفرج، فكان حدثاً، كما إذا مسه وهو يبول. وليس هذا بتعليل، لا ظاهراً، ولا باطناً، ولا رجوعاً إلى أصل. وكذلك قولهم : هذا مكاتب، فلا يصح التكفير بإعتاقه، كما إذا أدى بعض بدل الكتابة؛ لأن أداء بعض البدل عوض مانع عندنا، فلا يبقى إلا الدعوى.
As for that which does not stand except by a description by which the distinction occurs: it is invalid, like the saying of some of the followers of al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) regarding touching the penis: that it is an event (invalidates wudu) because it is touching the private part, so it is an event, just as if one touches it while urinating. And this is not a valid reasoning, neither outwardly nor inwardly, nor a return to an original principle. And similarly their saying: This is a mukatib, so it is not valid to be freed by his emancipation, just as if he has paid part of the kitaba price; because paying part of the price is a preventing substitute according to us, so nothing remains but the claim.
Source pages p. 605

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth type of invalid analogical reasoning (ta'lil) according to the Hanafi usul tradition. The rule is that any analogy that relies on a description (wasf) that itself requires a separate proof to establish the distinction (farq) is invalid. The first example critiques the Shafi'i position that touching the penis nullifies wudu because it is touching a private part, analogizing it to touching while urinating—but the description 'touching the private part' is not necessarily the effective cause (illa) since there is no clear textual indication. The second example concerns a mukatib (a slave who has contracted manumission by payment): some argue that the master's emancipation of him is invalid because he is like one who has partly paid (where the master loses the right to free him), but this analogy fails because the payment part creates a preventing substitute (badal) only in the view of Hanafis, so the analogy is based on a disputed basis. Both examples show that the supposed illa is not independently established, making the analogical reasoning groundless.

Key Terms
تعليل (ta'lil): Analogical reasoning that seeks to establish a legal cause (illa) for a ruling.
وصف (wasf): The description or attribute used as the potential cause in analogy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi critique in this lesson, why is the analogy regarding the mukatib (slave under contract) invalid?
A) Because the mukatib is not a slave.
B) Because the description 'having paid part of the kitaba' is itself a disputed cause.
C) Because the ruling on freeing a mukatib is not known.
D) Because the analogy involves a different realm of fiqh.

Key Takeaway
A valid ta'lil requires that the description used as the cause be an independently proven appropriate illa; otherwise the analogy is groundless.

The Contradictory Type of Invalid Ta'lil
Critique of contradictory analogical reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a contradictory ta'lil and explain why it is invalid in Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
A ta'lil with contradictory descriptions nullifies itself: no consistent illa remains.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Kanz al-Wusul critiques a specific type of invalid analogical reasoning: the contradictory ta'lil. Two examples are given: the case of owning one's brother and the case of a current kitaba contract. In each, the description used to support the ruling contradicts itself, leaving no valid basis for the analogy. Understanding this type of invalidity is crucial for distinguishing sound qiyas from flawed reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the analogy
Find the original case (asl) and the new case (far') in the ta'lil. For the brother example: the asl is the cousin (ibn al-‘amm) who is not freed by ownership; the far' is the brother. For the kitaba example: the asl is kitaba with wine (invalid); the far' is current kitaba.
2
Extract the proposed illa
Determine the description claimed to be the effective cause. In the brother example: 'a person whose emancipation is valid for expiation.' In the kitaba example: 'a kitaba contract that does not prevent expiation.'
3
Test for contradiction
Check if the illa logically conflicts with the ruling or with itself. For the brother: if emancipation is valid for expiation, then the brother can be freed, so why is he not freed? The illa suggests the opposite of the ruling. For the kitaba: calling it a kitaba contract implies validity, but the ruling is invalidity.
4
Conclude invalidity
If the illa leads to a contradiction, the ta'lil is invalid. No consistent description remains, so the analogy collapses. The passage calls this 'at the extreme of corruption.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٥- وأما الذي يكون مختلفاً : فمثل قولهم فيمن مَلَكَ أخاه : إنه شخص يصح التكفير بإعتاقه، فلا يعتق في الملك، كابن العم. وقولهم في الكتابة الحالة : إنه عقد كتابة، لا يمنع من التكفير، فكان فاسداً، كالكتابة بالخمر، وهذا في نهاية الفساد ؛ لأن الاختلاف في ذلك ظاهر، فلا يبقى وصف أصلاً.
5. As for that which is contradictory: like their saying regarding one who owns his brother: that he is a person whose emancipation is valid for expiation, so he is not freed by ownership, like the son of an uncle. And their saying regarding the current kitaba: that it is a contract of kitaba that does not prevent expiation, so it is invalid, like kitaba with wine. This is at the extreme of corruption, because the contradiction in that is apparent, so no description remains at all.
Source pages p. 605

Commentary
This passage explains the fifth type of invalid analogical reasoning: contradictory ta'lil. The examples show analogies that contain internal contradictions. The first example: someone argues that owning one's brother does not result in emancipation because the brother is a person whose emancipation is valid for expiation (kaffarah) and therefore he is not freed by ownership, analogizing to a cousin (ibn al-‘amm) where ownership does not free. But if emancipation is valid for expiation, that indicates the person is eligible to be freed, contradicting the claim that ownership does not free. The second example: arguing that a current (hala) kitaba contract is invalid because it is a kitaba contract that does not prevent expiation, analogizing to kitaba with wine which is invalid. But if it is a kitaba contract, it should be valid; the description of being a kitaba contract contradicts the claim of invalidity. Such ta'lil is at the extreme corruption because the conflicting descriptions leave no consistent attribute that can serve as a sound cause.

Key Terms
مختلف (mukhtalif): Contradictory; an analogy that includes conflicting descriptions that negate each other.
كتابة (kitaba): A contract by which a slave purchases his freedom by paying installments or a lump sum.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the kitaba contract analogy extremely corrupt?
A) Because the comparison to wine is invalid.
B) Because the description of being a 'kitaba contract' contradicts the ruling of invalidity.
C) Because the contract is void ab initio.
D) Because the analogy is based on a weak hadith.

Key Takeaway
A ta'lil is invalid if its description (illa) leads to an internal contradiction, as it leaves no consistent effective cause.

Invalid Ta'lil: The Obviously Invalid Type
Critique of obviously invalid analogical reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify obviously corrupt analogies (ta'lil fasid) and explain why they fail the conditions of valid qiyas.

Core Idea
An analogy is obviously invalid when its 'illa is arbitrary and the cases have no genuine shared cause.

Not every comparison qualifies as valid qiyas. The Hanafi usul tradition emphasizes that the 'illa must be a genuinely operative cause. This lesson examines examples of ta'lil that are so clearly corrupt they require no detailed refutation, helping you sharpen your ability to spot flawed reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the type of invalid analogy
The passage deals with analogies that are obviously corrupt (لا يُشك في فساده). Identify that these require no detailed proof of invalidity.
2
Analyze the first example: number seven
Someone argues that because seven is a number in the fast of tamattu', it becomes a condition for prayer. Note the arbitrary connection and lack of a consistent 'illa between fasting and prayer.
3
Examine the second example: number three
Another analogy: the number three is the period for wiping (masah), so prayer is not valid with one but valid with three, and then extends to seven. Flaw: the 'illa (number) is not operative in the far' and is inconsistent.
4
Review the hajj analogy
A third analogy: because both prayer and hajj have tahlil wa tahrim, and hajj has a pillar with number seven (tawaf, sa'y), prayer must also have a pillar with number seven. The flaw: ignoring the specific rulings of each worship.
5
Critique the wudu example
Some Hanafi mashayikh argued that wudu does not require intention because it is an action on limbs, analogizing to qisas or theft where intention is not a condition. The corruption is obvious because intention is a requirement for acts of worship, unlike penal acts.
6
Conclude the criteria
Valid qiyas requires a sound 'illa that is recognized by Shariah and consistently applies. These analogies fail because the alleged 'illa is either arbitrary, inconsistent, or applies to a different category.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٦- وأما الذي لا يُشك في فساده فمثل قول بعضهم: إن السَّبْعَ أحدُ عددي صوم المتعة، فكان شرطاً لجواز الصلاة، كالثلاث يريد به قراءة الفاتحة، ولأن الثلاث أحد عددي مدة المسح، فلا تصح به الصلاة، كالواحد. ولأن الثلاث، أو الآيةَ ناقص العدد عن السبع، فلا تتأدى به الصلاة، كما دون الآية، ولأن هذه عبادة لها تحليل وتحريم، فكان من أركانها : ما له عدد سبعة، كالحج. وكما قال بعض مشايخنا رحمهم الله : إن فرض الوضوء فعل يُقام في أعضائه، فلم تكن النية شرطاً في أدائه كالقطع قصاصاً، أو سرقة، وهذا مما لا يخفى فساده.
6. As for that which is not doubted in its corruption: like the saying of some: that seven is one of the numbers of the fast of tamattu', so it is a condition for the validity of prayer, like three (meaning the recitation of al-Fatiha), and because three is one of the numbers of the period of wiping, so prayer is not valid with it, like one. And because three or the verse is deficient in number from seven, so prayer is not performed with it, like less than a verse. And because this is an act of worship that has opening and closing (tahlil wa tahrim), so among its pillars is that which has the number seven, like hajj. And as some of our mashayikh (may Allah have mercy on them) said: that the obligation of wudu is an action performed on its parts, so intention is not a condition for its performance, like cutting for retaliation or theft. And this is something whose corruption is not hidden.
Source pages p. 605

Commentary
This passage discusses the sixth type of invalid ta'lil: analogies whose corruption is immediately obvious. The first set of examples twists numerical arguments: someone tries to analogize that because seven is a number related to fasting (the days of tamattu' fast) and is a condition for prayer (since three verses are a condition, and three is a number, so seven is analogous), but the reasoning is nonsensical. Similarly, they compare the number three to the wiping period (masah) arguing that prayer is invalid with one but not three, extending to seven. Another attempts to analogize acts of worship that have tahlil and tahrim (like hajj) to prayer, arguing that pillars with number seven are required. Finally, a saying from some of the Hanafi mashayikh is cited: that wudu is an action performed on its limbs, so intention is not a condition, analogizing to qisas or theft where intention is not a condition. This analogy is obviously corrupt because intention is required for many acts of worship, and the comparison to penal acts is inappropriate. The text emphasizes that such ta'lil is so clearly invalid that no one doubts its corruption.

Key Terms
فساد (fasad): Corruption or invalidity of the analogy; so obvious that it requires no detailed proof.
تحليل وتحريم (tahlil wa tahrim): The opening and closing (takbirat al-ihram and taslim) of prayers, or generally the beginning and end of a ritual.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi critique consider the analogy that wudu does not require intention because it is an action on its parts (like qisas) as obviously corrupt?
A) Because intention is required for all acts of worship according to all schools.
B) Because qisas and theft are not acts of worship and have no relation to wudu.
C) Because the analogy is based on a weak hadith.
D) Because wudu requires intention only if there is no water.

Key Takeaway
An analogy that lacks a Shariah-recognized 'illa or draws a parallel between fundamentally different categories is obviously invalid and rejected.

The Invalidity of Arguing with Absence of Evidence
Refutation of using absence of evidence as a proof for negation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why the Hanafi school rejects the argument from absence of evidence (hujjat al-nafi) and distinguish it from the Lawgiver's authoritative statement of non-existence.

Core Idea
For human scholars, 'no evidence' proves nothing—only the infallible Lawgiver's statement of non-existence is binding.

In legal reasoning, some argue that if no evidence is found for a ruling, the ruling must be absent. The Hanafi school rejects this completely for human scholars. This lesson unpacks the reasoning: the analogy of 'no man in the house', the case of ambergris and khums, and the decisive exception of the Lawgiver's own testimony.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim
Identify the argument that some scholars make: absence of evidence after thorough search is a proof of non-existence.
This is called hujjat al-nafi.
2
Critique the analogy
The text says 'no evidence' is like saying 'no man in the house'—the statement does not prove non-existence; it merely reflects the speaker's limited knowledge.
The analogy exposes the fallacy.
3
Examine the ambergris example
Imam Muhammad's ruling on ambergris is not based on absence of evidence but on qiyas (analogy to fish and water). The text clarifies that he used qiyas, not la daleel.
This prevents a counterexample.
4
Identify the epistemological difference
Humans differ in knowledge; only the Lawgiver is free from forgetfulness and incapability. Quran 6:145 shows the Prophet's statement of non-finding is decisive because he is infallible.
The exception proves the rule.
5
Apply the rule
State the ruling: absence of evidence is not a valid argument for negation for human mujtahids.
This is a key usul principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الاحتجاج بلا دليل فقد جعله بعضهم حجةً للنافي، وهذا باطل بلا شبهة ؛ لأن لا دليل بمنزلة : لا رجل في الدار، وهذا لا يحتمل وجوده، فلا دليل كيف يحتمل وجوده؟ وكيف صار دليلاً؟ ولا يلزم ما ذَكَر محمد رحمه الله في العنبر أنه لا خُمس فيه ؛ لأنه لم فيه الأثر؛ لأنه قد ذكر أنه بمنزلة السمك، والسمك بمنزلة الماء، ولا خُمس في الماء. يعني أن القياس ينفيه، ولم يرد أثرٌ يُترك به القياس أيضاً، فوجب العمل بالقياس، وهو أنه لم يُشرع الخمس إلا في الغنيمة، ولم توجد. ولأن الناس يتفاوتون في العلم والمعرفة بلا شبهة، فقول القائل : لم يقم الدليل، مع احتمال قصوره عن غيره في دَرْك الدليل : لا يصلح حجة. ولهذا صح هذا النوع من صاحب الشرع بقوله تعالى: ﴿ قُل لَّا أَجِدُ في ما أوحى إلى محرما عَلَى طَاعِمٍ يَطْعَمُهُ ﴾ ( الأنعام / ١٤٥)؛ لأنه هو الشارع، فشهادته بالعدم دليل قاطع على عدمه، إذ لا يجري عليه السهو، ولا يوصف بالعجز، فأما البَشَرُ فإن صفة العجز تُلازمهم والسهو يعتريهم.
And as for arguing with the absence of evidence, some have made it a proof for the negator, and this is false without doubt. Because 'no evidence' is like saying 'no man in the house' – and this does not admit of its existence – so how can 'no evidence' admit its existence? And how can it become a proof? And no attention is paid to what Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) mentioned regarding ambergris, that there is no khums on it because he did not find any transmitted evidence (athar); for he mentioned that it is like fish, and fish is like water, and there is no khums on water. Meaning that analogy negates it, and also no transmitted evidence has come that would make us abandon the analogy, so it is necessary to act on the analogy, which is that khums is only legislated for booty (ghanimah), and it does not exist. And because people differ in knowledge and understanding without doubt, so the statement of one who says 'no evidence has been established' while there is a possibility that he is deficient compared to others in comprehending evidence, such a statement is not a valid proof. For this reason, this type is valid from the Lawgiver, as He said: 'Say: I do not find in what has been revealed to me anything forbidden to a eater who eats it' (Al-An'am 6:145); because He is the Lawgiver, so His testimony of non-existence is a decisive proof of its non-existence, since forgetfulness does not occur on Him nor is He described as incapable. As for humans, the attribute of incapability accompanies them and forgetfulness affects them.
Source pages p. 606

Commentary
This passage discusses a common fallacy: using the absence of evidence as proof that a ruling does not exist. The Hanafi position is that this is invalid for human scholars because they lack complete knowledge. The analogy of 'no man in the house' illustrates that 'no evidence' is a statement about the knowledge of the speaker, not about reality. The text then addresses a statement by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding ambergris ('anbar) where he said there is no khums (one-fifth tax) on it because he found no transmitted evidence (athar). The author explains that Muhammad's statement is not a case of arguing from absence of evidence; rather, it is based on qiyas: ambergris is like fish and water, which have no khums, so the analogy negates khums. Since there is no contrary athar, the analogy stands. The general principle is that people vary in knowledge, so a claim of 'no evidence' may reflect the speaker's ignorance. The only exception is the Lawgiver (Allah and the Prophet in matters of revelation), as shown by Quran 6:145, where the Prophet's statement 'I do not find' is authoritative because he is infallible and comprehensive in knowledge. For humans, it is not a valid argument.

Key Terms
لا دليل (la daleel): The statement that there is no evidence for a ruling.
حجة النافي (hujjat al-nafi): An argument used by the one who negates a ruling, claiming lack of evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi scholar reject the argument that 'no evidence exists' is a valid proof for negation?
A) Because the person making the claim may be unaware of existing evidence.
B) Because evidence is not required for negative claims.
C) Because the Quran prohibits such arguments.
D) Because analogy always overrides absence of evidence.

Key Takeaway
Only the infallible Lawgiver's statement of non-existence is conclusive; human claims of 'no evidence' are not valid proofs for negation.

Why 'No Evidence' Is Not a Valid Proof
Refuting the argument that absence of evidence is a valid proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why 'absence of evidence' (la dalil) is not a valid proof in Hanafi usul, and identify the sole exception of Prophetic testimony of absence.

Core Idea
Absence of evidence is not evidence of absence—except from the infallible Lawgiver.

In this passage, the author of Usul al-Bazdawi refutes the erroneous claim that the mere lack of evidence for a ruling can itself serve as proof. He argues that human ignorance cannot be a basis for legal determination, and that only the Prophet's testimony of absence carries weight due to his infallibility. This lesson examines the rational basis for this principle and its implications for legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of refutation
The author begins by dismissing the argument that 'no evidence' (la dalil) can be a proof for negating a ruling.
This is a common fallacy in usul.
2
Understand the linguistic analogy
He compares 'no evidence' to saying 'there is no man in the house' — a statement that does not prove non-existence but merely reflects the speaker's limited observation.
The analogy shows the logical gap.
3
Examine the amber objection and its resolution
An objector might cite Imam Muhammad's ruling on amber having no khums, thinking it rests on absence of evidence. The author clarifies that it is based on qiyas (analogy), not absence.
This shows that scholars rely on positive evidence, not absence.
4
Grasp the rational argument about human limitation
People differ in knowledge; one person's claim 'no evidence exists' may reflect his own ignorance. Therefore it cannot be a proof.
This is the core rational (aqli) argument.
5
Recognize the exception: Prophetic testimony
The author cites Quran 6:145 where the Prophet says 'I do not find...', which is authoritative because he is infallible and knows all.
This is the only valid form of shahadat al-'adam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولأن الثلاث، أو الآيةَ ناقص العدد عن السبع، فلا تتأدى به الصلاة، كما دون الآية، ولأن هذه عبادة لها تحليل وتحريم، فكان من أركانها : ما له عدد سبعة، كالحج. وكما قال بعض مشايخنا رحمهم الله : إن فرض الوضوء فعل يُقام في أعضائه، فلم تكن النية شرطاً في أدائه كالقطع قصاصاً، أو سرقة، وهذا مما لا يخفى فساده.
وأما الاحتجاج بلا دليل فقد جعله بعضهم حجةً للنافي، وهذا باطل بلا شبهة ؛ لأن لا دليل بمنزلة : لا رجل في الدار، وهذا لا يحتمل وجوده ، فلا دليل كيف يحتمل وجوده؟ وكيف صار دليلاً؟.
وولا يلزم ما ذَكَر محمد رحمه الله في العنبر أنه لا خُمس فيه ؛ لأنه لم فيه الأثر؛ لأنه قد ذكر أنه بمنزلة السمك، والسمك بمنزلة الماء، ولا خُمس في الماء.
يعني أن القياس ينفيه، ولم يرد أثرٌ يُترك به القياس أيضاً، فوجب العمل بالقياس، وهو أنه لم يُشرع الخمس إلا في الغنيمة، ولم توجد. ولأن الناس يتفاوتون في العلم والمعرفة بلا شبهة، فقول القائل : لم يقم الدليل، مع احتمال قصوره عن غيره في دَرْك الدليل : لا يصلح حجة. ولهذا صح هذا النوع من صاحب الشرع بقوله تعالى: ﴿ قُل لَّا أَجِدُ في ما أوحى إلى محرما عَلَى طَاعِمٍ يَطْعَمُهُ ﴾ ( الأنعام / ١٤٥ )؛ لأنه هو الشارع، فشهادته بالعدم دليل قاطع على عدمه، إذ لا يجري عليه السهو، ولا يوصف بالعجز ، فأما البَشَرُ فإن صفة العجز تُلازمهم والسهو يعتريهم.
ومَن ادعى أنه يعرف كل شيء: نُسب إلى السَّفَه ، أو العَتَه، فلم يُناظَر. ومَن شَرَعَ في العمل بلا دليل: اضطر إلى التقليد الذي هو باطل، والله أعلم.
And because three (verses), or an aya deficient in number from seven, prayer is not performed with it, just as less than an aya. And because this is an act of worship having tahlil and tahrim, so among its pillars is that which has the number seven, like Hajj. And as some of our shaykhs (may Allah have mercy on them) said: The obligatory act of wudu is an action performed on its limbs, so intention is not a condition in its performance, like cutting off (the hand) as retaliation, or theft. And this is clearly unsound. As for arguing with 'no evidence', some have made it a proof for the negator, and this is false without doubt. Because 'no evidence' is like saying 'there is no man in the house', and this does not admit its existence, so how can 'no evidence' admit its existence? And how can it be a proof? And it is not required what Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) mentioned regarding amber, that there is no khums on it, because he did not bring the tradition; because he mentioned that it is like fish, and fish is like water, and there is no khums in water. He meant that analogy negates it, and no tradition came to leave the analogy as well, so acting on analogy became obligatory, which is that khums was only legislated in war booty, and it is not present. And because people differ in knowledge and awareness without doubt, so the statement of one saying 'The evidence has not been established', while he may be deficient compared to others in perceiving the evidence, is not valid as a proof. And for this reason, this type (of statement) is valid from the Lawgiver by His statement: 'Say, I do not find in what has been revealed to me anything prohibited for an eater to eat' (Al-An'am 6:145); because He is the Lawgiver, so His testimony of absence is a definitive proof of its absence, since forgetfulness does not occur to Him, nor is He described as incapable. As for humans, the attribute of incapacity accompanies them and forgetfulness affects them. And whoever claims that he knows everything is attributed to foolishness or stupidity, so he is not debated. And whoever begins to act without evidence is forced to follow blind imitation (taqlid), which is false. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 606

Commentary
This passage is a usul rule establishing that the mere absence of evidence (la dalil) cannot be used as a proof in legal reasoning. The author begins by refuting a hypothetical argument that lacking evidence is itself evidence, comparing it to a statement 'there is no man in the house'—which does not establish the actual non-existence of a man. He then addresses an objection based on Imam Muhammad's ruling about amber (amber) having no khums. The objection claimed that the lack of evidence for khums on amber proves its absence. The author clarifies that Muhammad's ruling was based on analogy (qiyas): amber is analogous to fish, which is like water, and water has no khums. The ruling was not based on the absence of evidence but on a positive analogy. Hence, acting on analogy is obligatory when no text overrides it. The author then provides a rational argument: people differ in their ability to perceive evidence, so an individual's claim that no evidence exists is unreliable due to potential ignorance. However, this type of argument (testimony of absence) is valid from the Lawgiver (the Prophet) because he is infallible and knows all. As proof, he cites Quran 6:145, where the Prophet states he finds no prohibition—this is definitive because he cannot err. In contrast, human testimony of absence is not definitive. The passage concludes by noting that claiming absolute knowledge is foolish, and acting without evidence leads to taqlid (blind following), which is invalid.

Key Terms
لا دليل (la dalil): The absence of evidence; a claim that no proof exists for a particular ruling.
شهادة العدم (shahadat al-'adam): Testimony of absence; declaring that something does not exist because no evidence for it has been found.
تقليد (taqlid): Blind following of a scholar without evidence or independent reasoning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the testimony of absence (shahadat al-'adam) from a human scholar not a valid proof in Hanafi usul?
A) Because humans are always mistaken.
B) Because the scholar might be unaware of existing evidence due to human limitation.
C) Because the Quran prohibits such testimony.
D) Because it leads to innovation.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the mere absence of evidence is not a valid proof; only the infallible Lawgiver's testimony of absence carries authoritative weight.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

8 min
The Four Divisions of Ta'lil: Valid and Invalid Causal Reasoning
Defines the valid scope of ta'lil in qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four divisions of ta'lil and apply the Hanafi rule that only the fourth division (extension via ta'diya) is valid.

Core Idea
Ta'lil can only extend an existing ruling, not create a new cause, condition, or ruling.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul al-Fiqh establishes a critical boundary for causal reasoning (ta'lil) in qiyas. The author first clarifies that the ruling established by ta'lil is the extension of a textual ruling to an unmentioned case, based on predominant opinion. He then divides possible objects of ta'lil into four categories and declares the first three invalid. Understanding this division is essential for correct application of qiyas: it is a tool for applying existing Shari'ah principles, not for legislating new ones.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central ruling on ta'diya
Recognize that ta'diya (extension) is the outcome of ta'lil. Note the Hanafi position that it is necessary (lazim), contrasting with the Shafi'i view that it is permissible (ja'iz).
This sets the epistemological weight: qiyas is a binding proof in Hanafi usul.
2
Enumerate the four divisions of ta'lil
List the four possible objects: (1) establishing the cause or its description, (2) establishing the condition or its description, (3) establishing the ruling or its description, (4) extending a known ruling by its cause and condition.
The fourth is the only valid one.
3
Analyze why the first three are invalid
Understand that ta'lil is a means to discover Shari'ah rulings, not to create them. Using it to establish a cause or condition would be tantamount to legislation, and using it to establish a ruling would be setting law by opinion. Establishing a condition would invalidate an existing ruling, which is naskh (abrogation) and thus prohibited.
The key is that ta'lil derives from an original text; it cannot be a source of new rules.
4
Grasp the validity of the fourth division
The fourth division – extending an existing ruling to a new case based on a shared illah and shart – is valid because it merely applies known Shari'ah principles. It does not create a new cause, condition, or ruling.
This is the standard qiyas operation.
5
Reflect on the epistemic status
Note that the resulting ruling is established by predominant opinion (ghalib al-ra'y) with the possibility of error. This is consistent with the Hanafi view that qiyas yields zanni knowledge.
This humility is built into the usul framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب
حكم العلة
فأما الحكم الثابت بتعليل النصوص : فتعديةُ حكم النص إلى ما لا نص فيه ؛ ليثبت فيه بغالب الرأي، على احتمال الخطأ.
وقد ذكرنا أن التعدية حكم لازم عندنا، جائز عند الشافعي رحمه الله. وإذا ثبت ذلك : قلنا : إن جملة ما يُعلَّل له أربعة أقسام :
١ - إثباتُ الموجب، أو وصفه.
٢ - وإثبات الشرط، أو وصفه.
وإثباتُ الحكم، أو وصفه.
٤- والرابع : هو تعدية حكم معلوم بسببه وشرطه بأوصاف معلومة. والتعليل للأقسام الثلاثة الأُول باطل؛ لأن التعليل شرع مَدْرَكاً لأحكام الشرع، على ما بينا، وفي إثبات الموجب وصفته: إثبات الشرع، وفي إثبات الشرط وصفته : إبطال الحكم ،ورفعه وهذا نسخ، ونصب أحكام الشرع بالرأي باطل، فكذلك رفعها.
وما القياس إلا اعتبار بأمرٍ مشروع ، فبطل التعليل لهذه الأقسام جملةً، وبطل التعليل لنفيها أيضاً؛ لأن نفيها ليس بحكم شرعي، فبطلت هذه الوجوه كلها، فلم يبق إلا الرابع.
Chapter: The Ruling of the Cause
As for the ruling established by reasoning from texts: it is the extension of the ruling of a text to that which has no text, so that it is established in it by predominant opinion, with the possibility of error.
And we have mentioned that extension is a necessary ruling according to us, and permissible according to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him. And when that is established, we say: Indeed, the totality of what is reasoned with a cause is four divisions:
1. Establishing the cause (al-mujib) or its description.
2. Establishing the condition (al-shart) or its description.
3. Establishing the ruling (al-hukm) or its description.
4. And the fourth: it is the extension of a known ruling by its cause and its condition, with known descriptions. And the reasoning for the first three divisions is invalid; because reasoning has been legislated as a means for the laws of the Shari'ah, as we have explained, and in establishing the cause and its description: it is establishing the Shari'ah, and in establishing the condition and its description: it is invalidating the ruling and removing it, and this is abrogation (naskh), and establishing the laws of the Shari'ah by opinion is invalid, so likewise is their removal.
And qiyas is nothing but a consideration of a legislated matter, so reasoning for these divisions altogether is invalid, and reasoning for their negation is also invalid; because their negation is not a legal ruling. So all these aspects are invalid, and nothing remains except the fourth.
Source pages p. 608

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul al-Fiqh is a ruling defining the valid scope of ta'lil (causal reasoning) in qiyas. The author begins by stating that the ruling established by ta'lil from texts is the extension (ta'diya) of a textual ruling to an unmentioned case, based on predominant opinion (ghalib al-ra'y) with the possibility of error. He notes that such extension is necessary (lazim) in the Hanafi school, while Imam al-Shafi'i considers it permissible (ja'iz). This highlights a key usul difference: Hanafis hold that qiyas is a binding source (hujjah) in the absence of a text, while Shafi'is maintain that it is a valid but not obligatory method. The author then identifies four possible objects of ta'lil: (1) establishing the cause (mujib) or its description, (2) establishing the condition (shart) or its description, (3) establishing the ruling (hukm) or its description, and (4) extending a known ruling by its cause and condition with known descriptions. He declares the first three invalid because ta'lil is a means (madrak) for discovering Shari'ah rulings, and using it to establish the cause itself would be tantamount to legislating (the cause is a sabab which is part of Shari'ah); using it to establish a condition would invalidate or repeal a ruling, which is naskh (abrogation); and using it to establish a ruling would be setting a legal rule by mere opinion—all of which are impermissible. Only the fourth division—extending an existing ruling to a new case based on shared cause and condition—is valid, as it merely applies known Shari'ah principles. The author emphasizes that qiyas is an 'i'tibar (consideration) of a mashru' (legislated matter), not a source of legislation itself.

Key Terms
العلة (al-illah): The cause or effective reason for a ruling, the attribute that is the basis for analogy.
التعليل (al-ta'lil): The process of reasoning by cause to extend a ruling from an original text to a new case.
تعدية (ta'diya): Extension of a ruling from the original case (asl) to a new case (far') based on a shared illah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Bazdawi, which of the following is a valid object of ta'lil (causal reasoning)?
A) Establishing the cause (mujib) or its description
B) Establishing the condition (shart) or its description
C) Establishing the ruling (hukm) or its description
D) Extending a known ruling by its cause and condition to a new case

Key Takeaway
Only the extension of an existing ruling using its cause and condition is valid; causal reasoning cannot establish new causes, conditions, or rulings.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

8 min
The Four Divisions of Ta'lil and the Invalidity of the First Three
Classification of ta'lil and validation of the fourth division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the four divisions of ta'lil and state why only the fourth is valid for qiyas in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Only the extension of a known ruling through its cause and conditions is valid ta'lil; the first three divisions attempt to legislate by opinion.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Kanz al-Wusul lays the foundational rule of what can be subject to causal reasoning (ta'lil). He first defines ta'liyah and then divides all possible objects of causation into four. By examining each, he demonstrates that only the fourth—extending a ruling from its known cause and conditions—is legitimate. Understanding this classification is critical for correctly applying qiyas without overstepping into legislating by opinion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of ta'liyah
Read the opening lines: ta'liyah is the extension of a textual ruling to an unmentioned case, based on preponderant opinion with possibility of error.
This definition sets the scope of the entire discussion.
2
Recognize the Hanafi position on ta'diyah
Al-Bazdawi states that extension (ta'diyah) is necessary (lazim) according to Hanafis, while al-Shafi'i considers it permissible. This difference affects when qiyas must be applied.
The dispute is about the binding nature of qiyas once the cause is identified.
3
List the four divisions of ta'lil
The author enumerates: (1) establishing the efficient cause or its description, (2) establishing the condition or its description, (3) establishing the ruling or its description, (4) extending a known ruling through its cause and condition with known descriptions.
These are all possible objects of causal reasoning.
4
Understand why the first three are invalid
Al-Bazdawi argues that ta'lil was legislated as a means for deriving rulings, not for creating or negating causes, conditions, or rulings themselves. Doing so would be legislating by opinion (ra'y), which is invalid for both establishing and abrogating rulings.
The first three divisions would involve either establishing Shari'ah (by creating a cause/ruling) or nullifying it (by negating a condition/ruling), both of which are legislation.
5
Confirm the validity of the fourth division
Only the fourth division remains: taking a known ruling that already has a known cause and condition, and extending it to a new case that shares those. This is the proper use of qiyas.
This is the standard Hanafi position on what qiyas can and cannot do.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب حكم العلة
فأما الحكم الثابت بتعليل النصوص : فتعديةُ حُكم النص إلى ما لا نص فيه ؛ ليثبت فيه بغالب الرأي، على احتمال الخطأ.
وقد ذكرنا أن التعدية حكم لازم عندنا، جائز عند الشافعي رحمه الله. وإذا ثبت ذلك : قلنا : إن جملة ما يُعلَّل له أربعة أقسام :
١ - إثباتُ الموجب، أو وصفه.
٢ - وإثبات الشرط، أو وصفه.
٣ - وإثباتُ الحكم، أو وصفه.
٤- والرابع : هو تعدية حكم معلوم بسببه وشرطه بأوصاف معلومة. والتعليل للأقسام الثلاثة الأُول باطل؛ لأن التعليل شرع مَدْرَكاً لأحكام الشرع، على ما بينا، وفي إثبات الموجب وصفته: إثبات الشرع، وفي إثبات الشرط وصفته : إبطال الحكم ،ورفعه وهذا نسخ، ونصب أحكام الشرع بالرأي باطل، فكذلك رفعها.
وما القياس إلا اعتبار بأمرٍ مشروع ، فبطل التعليل لهذه الأقسام جملةً، وبطل التعليل لنفيها أيضاً؛ لأن نفيها ليس بحكم شرعي، فبطلت هذه الوجوه كلها، فلم يبق إلا الرابع.
فأما تفسير القسم الأول : فمثل قولهم في الجنس بانفراده: إنه يُحرِّم النسيئة، فهذا خلافٌ وقع في الموجب للحكم، فلم يصح إثباته بالرأي، ولا نفيه ،به ، إنما يجب الكلامُ فيه بإشارة النص، أو دلالته، أو اقتضائه.
وكذلك اختلافُهم في السفر أنه مُسقِطٌ لشطر الصلاة، أم لا؟ لا يصح التكلم فيه بالقياس، بل بما ذكرنا.
فقلنا في مسألة الجنس إنا وجدنا الفضل الذي لا يقابله عوض عقد المعاوضة محرَّماً بما ذكرنا من العلة، ووجدنا هذا حكماً تستوي شبهته بحقيقته حتى لا يجوز البيع مجازفة؛ لاحتمال الربا.
وقد وجدنا في النسيئة شبهة الفضل، وهو الحلول المضاف إلى صنع العباد، وقد وجدنا شبهة العلة وهو أحد وصفي العلة، فأثبتناه بدلالة النص.
وكذلك فعلنا في السفر ؛ لأن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام قال: «إن الله تعالیٰ تصدق عليكم، فاقبلوا صدقته».
وذلك إسقاط محض، فلا يصح ردُّه ، ولأن القصر تعيّن تخفيفاً، بخلاف الفطر في السفر.
ولأن التخيير على وجه لا يتضمن رفقاً بالعبد ونفعاً : من صفات العبودية، لا من صفات الألوهية، على ما عُرف فهذه دلالات النصوص.
Chapter: The Ruling of the Cause
As for the ruling established by the causation of texts: it is the extension of the ruling of a text to what no text contains, so that it is established therein by preponderant opinion, with the possibility of error.
We have already mentioned that extension is a necessary ruling according to us, permissible according to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him. When that is established, we say: the totality of what is subject to causation is four divisions:
1. Establishing the efficient cause (al-mujib) or its description.
2. Establishing the condition (al-shart) or its description.
3. Establishing the ruling (al-hukm) or its description.
4. The fourth: it is the extension of a known ruling through its cause and condition with known descriptions. Causation for the first three divisions is invalid; because causation was legislated as a means for the rulings of the Shari'ah, as we have explained, and in establishing the efficient cause and its description: is establishing the Shari'ah, and in establishing the condition and its description: is nullifying the ruling and removing it, and this is abrogation. And establishing the rulings of the Shari'ah by opinion is invalid, and likewise removing them.
And qiyas is nothing but considering something legislated. So causation for these divisions is altogether invalid, and causation for negating them is also invalid; because their negation is not a legal ruling. So all these aspects are invalid, and nothing remains except the fourth.
As for the interpretation of the first division: such as their statement regarding the genus by itself: that it prohibits delay (nasī'ah). This is a disagreement that occurred regarding the efficient cause of the ruling, so it is not valid to establish it by opinion, nor to negate it by opinion. Rather, it must be discussed through the indication of the text, or its implication, or its requirement.
And similarly their disagreement regarding travel: whether it drops half of the prayer or not? It is not valid to discuss it by qiyas, but by what we have mentioned.
So we said regarding the issue of genus: we found that surplus (al-fadl) that is not met by a countervalue in a commutative contract is prohibited by what we mentioned of the cause, and we found this ruling such that its similarity and its reality are equal such that selling with uncertainty is not permitted due to the possibility of ribā.
And we found in delay (nasī'ah) a resemblance of surplus, which is the payment term added to the act of servants, and we found a resemblance of the cause, which is one of the two attributes of the cause, so we established it by the indication of the text.
And we did similarly regarding travel; because the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, said: 'Indeed Allah has given you charity, so accept His charity.'
And that is pure dropping, so it is not valid to reject it; and because shortening (qasr) is determined as a concession, unlike fasting during travel.
And because choosing (takhyīr) in a manner that does not contain kindness and benefit for the servant is from the attributes of servitude, not from the attributes of divinity, as is known. So these are the indications of the texts.
Source pages p. 608

Commentary
This passage is a rule in uṣūl al-fiqh from al-Bazdawi’s Kanz al-Wuṣūl, clarifying the legitimate scope of qiyas (analogical deduction). The author first defines the ruling established through ta‘līl (causation of texts) as the extension of a textual ruling to an unmentioned case, based on predominant opinion and admitting the possibility of error. He then asserts that according to the Ḥanafī school, such extension (ta‘diyah) is necessary (lāzim) in the sense that once the cause is identified, the ruling must follow, whereas al-Shāfi‘ī considers it merely permissible (jā’iz) — meaning the mujtahid may choose to extend or not. After settling that point, al-Bazdawi lists four possible objects of ta‘līl: (1) establishing the efficient cause (mūjib) or its description, (2) establishing the condition (sharṭ) or its description, (3) establishing the ruling (ḥukm) or its description, and (4) extending a known ruling through its cause and known descriptions. He argues that ta‘līl is invalid for the first three because ta‘līl was legislated as a source of legal rulings only for deriving new ones, not for creating or negating original causes, conditions, or rulings; doing so would effectively be legislating by opinion (ra’y) — which is invalid whether one is establishing or abrogating a ruling. The fourth division is the only valid one: taking a known ruling (e.g., prohibition of ribā in a sale) along with its known cause (e.g., surplus without countervalue) and condition (e.g., delay) and extending it to a new case that shares that cause and condition. Al-Bazdawi then exemplifies the first division (which is invalid) by the disagreement whether the genus alone (e.g., wheat for wheat) causes the prohibition of delay (nasī’ah). He says such a question cannot be settled by qiyas but only by textual indication. Similarly, the dispute whether travel drops half of prayer cannot be solved by qiyas; he then provides the Ḥanafī proofs for these issues from textual indications and the hadith about travel.

Key Terms
التعدية (al-ta‘diyah): Extension: applying the ruling of a text to an unmentioned case on the basis of a shared cause.
الموجب (al-mūjib): The efficient cause of the ruling; the attribute that necessitates the ruling.
العلة (al-‘illah): The cause (in qiyas); the attribute that serves as the basis for extending the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Bazdawi, which of the four divisions of ta'lil is valid?
A) Establishing the efficient cause or its description
B) Establishing the condition or its description
C) Establishing the ruling or its description
D) Extending a known ruling through its cause and condition with known descriptions

Key Takeaway
Only the fourth division—extending a known ruling through its cause and conditions—is valid for qiyas; the first three amount to legislating by opinion.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

Examples of Invalid Ta'lil: Genus and Travel Disputes
Application of the rule through two examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why disputes about the efficient cause (mujib) cannot be resolved by qiyas but only by textual indication, using the examples of genus and travel.

Core Idea
Textual indication, not qiyas, determines the existence of an efficient cause or condition; qiyas only extends rulings already known.

Al-Bazdawi now applies the rule from the previous lesson through concrete examples. He takes two cases—the disagreement whether the genus alone prohibits delay (nasī'ah) and whether travel drops half the prayer—to illustrate that such foundational questions about the cause itself cannot be decided by qiyas. Instead, they must be resolved by direct reference to texts (dilalat al-nusus).

Guided Path
1
Recognize that both examples fall under the first division
The first division is establishing the efficient cause or its description. The disputes whether genus alone prohibits delay and whether travel drops prayer are about the existence of a cause (mujib) for those rulings.
These are precisely the kind of issues that al-Bazdawi declared invalid for ta'lil.
2
Understand why qiyas cannot be used here
Using qiyas to determine the existence of a cause would mean creating or negating a cause by opinion, which is legislation. Qiyas can only extend rulings that are already established through a known cause.
The cause itself must be known from the text, not derived by analogy.
3
Follow the Hanafi reasoning for the genus case
Al-Bazdawi states that for the genus issue, the Hanafis found that surplus without a countervalue in a commutative contract is prohibited by the textually established cause. They did not use qiyas to prove this; they relied on textual indication.
The phrase 'fa-athbatnahu bi-dalālat al-naṣṣ' shows they used textual implication.
4
Examine the travel example and the hadith
The dispute about travel dropping half the prayer is resolved by citing the hadith: 'Indeed Allah has given you charity, so accept His charity.' This shows the concession is a pure gift, not subject to causal reasoning or qiyas.
The hadith indicates that the ruling is based on divine grant, not on a cause that can be reasoned.
5
Conclude that textual indications are the proper method
Both examples demonstrate that for first-division issues, we must use textual indications (ishāra, dalāla, iqtidā') rather than qiyas. The mujtahid must derive the cause from the texts, not construct it by analogy.
This reinforces the Hanafi principle that causes are known from Shari'ah, not from reason alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب حكم العلة
فأما الحكم الثابت بتعليل النصوص : فتعديةُ حُكم النص إلى ما لا نص فيه ؛ ليثبت فيه بغالب الرأي، على احتمال الخطأ.
وقد ذكرنا أن التعدية حكم لازم عندنا، جائز عند الشافعي رحمه الله. وإذا ثبت ذلك : قلنا : إن جملة ما يُعلَّل له أربعة أقسام :
١ - إثباتُ الموجب، أو وصفه.
٢ - وإثبات الشرط، أو وصفه.
٣ - وإثباتُ الحكم، أو وصفه.
٤- والرابع : هو تعدية حكم معلوم بسببه وشرطه بأوصاف معلومة. والتعليل للأقسام الثلاثة الأُول باطل؛ لأن التعليل شرع مَدْرَكاً لأحكام الشرع، على ما بينا، وفي إثبات الموجب وصفته: إثبات الشرع، وفي إثبات الشرط وصفته : إبطال الحكم ،ورفعه وهذا نسخ، ونصب أحكام الشرع بالرأي باطل، فكذلك رفعها.
وما القياس إلا اعتبار بأمرٍ مشروع ، فبطل التعليل لهذه الأقسام جملةً، وبطل التعليل لنفيها أيضاً؛ لأن نفيها ليس بحكم شرعي، فبطلت هذه الوجوه كلها، فلم يبق إلا الرابع.
فأما تفسير القسم الأول : فمثل قولهم في الجنس بانفراده: إنه يُحرِّم النسيئة، فهذا خلافٌ وقع في الموجب للحكم، فلم يصح إثباته بالرأي، ولا نفيه ،به ، إنما يجب الكلامُ فيه بإشارة النص، أو دلالته، أو اقتضائه.
وكذلك اختلافُهم في السفر أنه مُسقِطٌ لشطر الصلاة، أم لا؟ لا يصح التكلم فيه بالقياس، بل بما ذكرنا.
فقلنا في مسألة الجنس إنا وجدنا الفضل الذي لا يقابله عوض عقد المعاوضة محرَّماً بما ذكرنا من العلة، ووجدنا هذا حكماً تستوي شبهته بحقيقته حتى لا يجوز البيع مجازفة؛ لاحتمال الربا.
وقد وجدنا في النسيئة شبهة الفضل، وهو الحلول المضاف إلى صنع العباد، وقد وجدنا شبهة العلة وهو أحد وصفي العلة، فأثبتناه بدلالة النص.
وكذلك فعلنا في السفر ؛ لأن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام قال: «إن الله تعالیٰ تصدق عليكم، فاقبلوا صدقته».
وذلك إسقاط محض، فلا يصح ردُّه ، ولأن القصر تعيّن تخفيفاً، بخلاف الفطر في السفر.
ولأن التخيير على وجه لا يتضمن رفقاً بالعبد ونفعاً : من صفات العبودية، لا من صفات الألوهية، على ما عُرف فهذه دلالات النصوص.
Chapter: The Ruling of the Cause
As for the ruling established by the causation of texts: it is the extension of the ruling of a text to what no text contains, so that it is established therein by preponderant opinion, with the possibility of error.
We have already mentioned that extension is a necessary ruling according to us, permissible according to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him. When that is established, we say: the totality of what is subject to causation is four divisions:
1. Establishing the efficient cause (al-mujib) or its description.
2. Establishing the condition (al-shart) or its description.
3. Establishing the ruling (al-hukm) or its description.
4. The fourth: it is the extension of a known ruling through its cause and condition with known descriptions. Causation for the first three divisions is invalid; because causation was legislated as a means for the rulings of the Shari'ah, as we have explained, and in establishing the efficient cause and its description: is establishing the Shari'ah, and in establishing the condition and its description: is nullifying the ruling and removing it, and this is abrogation. And establishing the rulings of the Shari'ah by opinion is invalid, and likewise removing them.
And qiyas is nothing but considering something legislated. So causation for these divisions is altogether invalid, and causation for negating them is also invalid; because their negation is not a legal ruling. So all these aspects are invalid, and nothing remains except the fourth.
As for the interpretation of the first division: such as their statement regarding the genus by itself: that it prohibits delay (nasī'ah). This is a disagreement that occurred regarding the efficient cause of the ruling, so it is not valid to establish it by opinion, nor to negate it by opinion. Rather, it must be discussed through the indication of the text, or its implication, or its requirement.
And similarly their disagreement regarding travel: whether it drops half of the prayer or not? It is not valid to discuss it by qiyas, but by what we have mentioned.
So we said regarding the issue of genus: we found that surplus (al-fadl) that is not met by a countervalue in a commutative contract is prohibited by what we mentioned of the cause, and we found this ruling such that its similarity and its reality are equal such that selling with uncertainty is not permitted due to the possibility of ribā.
And we found in delay (nasī'ah) a resemblance of surplus, which is the payment term added to the act of servants, and we found a resemblance of the cause, which is one of the two attributes of the cause, so we established it by the indication of the text.
And we did similarly regarding travel; because the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, said: 'Indeed Allah has given you charity, so accept His charity.'
And that is pure dropping, so it is not valid to reject it; and because shortening (qasr) is determined as a concession, unlike fasting during travel.
And because choosing (takhyīr) in a manner that does not contain kindness and benefit for the servant is from the attributes of servitude, not from the attributes of divinity, as is known. So these are the indications of the texts.
Source pages p. 608

Commentary
This passage is a rule in uṣūl al-fiqh from al-Bazdawi’s Kanz al-Wuṣūl, clarifying the legitimate scope of qiyas (analogical deduction). The author first defines the ruling established through ta‘līl (causation of texts) as the extension of a textual ruling to an unmentioned case, based on predominant opinion and admitting the possibility of error. He then asserts that according to the Ḥanafī school, such extension (ta‘diyah) is necessary (lāzim) in the sense that once the cause is identified, the ruling must follow, whereas al-Shāfi‘ī considers it merely permissible (jā’iz) — meaning the mujtahid may choose to extend or not. After settling that point, al-Bazdawi lists four possible objects of ta‘līl: (1) establishing the efficient cause (mūjib) or its description, (2) establishing the condition (sharṭ) or its description, (3) establishing the ruling (ḥukm) or its description, and (4) extending a known ruling through its cause and known descriptions. He argues that ta‘līl is invalid for the first three because ta‘līl was legislated as a source of legal rulings only for deriving new ones, not for creating or negating original causes, conditions, or rulings; doing so would effectively be legislating by opinion (ra’y) — which is invalid whether one is establishing or abrogating a ruling. The fourth division is the only valid one: taking a known ruling (e.g., prohibition of ribā in a sale) along with its known cause (e.g., surplus without countervalue) and condition (e.g., delay) and extending it to a new case that shares that cause and condition. Al-Bazdawi then exemplifies the first division (which is invalid) by the disagreement whether the genus alone (e.g., wheat for wheat) causes the prohibition of delay (nasī’ah). He says such a question cannot be settled by qiyas but only by textual indication. Similarly, the dispute whether travel drops half of prayer cannot be solved by qiyas; he then provides the Ḥanafī proofs for these issues from textual indications and the hadith about travel.

Key Terms
التعدية (al-ta‘diyah): Extension: applying the ruling of a text to an unmentioned case on the basis of a shared cause.
الموجب (al-mūjib): The efficient cause of the ruling; the attribute that necessitates the ruling.
العلة (al-‘illah): The cause (in qiyas); the attribute that serves as the basis for extending the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how did the Hanafis establish that surplus without a countervalue is the cause of riba in the genus example?
A) By direct qiyas from the genus
B) By textual indication (dalālat al-naṣṣ)
C) By consensus of the Companions
D) By istihsan

Key Takeaway
The existence of an efficient cause or condition is determined by textual indication, not by qiyas; qiyas only extends rulings that have already been established.

The Fourth Type: Qiyas and Istihsan
Introduction to the fourth category of reasoning: qiyas and istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify qiyas and istihsan as the two aspects of the fourth type of differences in usul al-fiqh and explain their basic distinction.

Core Idea
Qiyas and istihsan are two distinct methods of deriving rulings, forming the fourth category of ikhtilaf.

Al-Bazdawi introduces the fourth type as comprising two aspects regarding the ruling: qiyas (analogy) and istihsan (juristic preference). This passage forms the foundational classification before detailed discussion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fourth type in the classification of differences
Recognize that this passage is introducing a category of ikhtilaf (scholarly disagreement) in usul al-fiqh, specifically the fourth type which concerns methods of deriving rulings.
2
Recognize its two aspects
The text states that this type has two aspects (وجهين) regarding the ruling: qiyas and istihsan. Note that they are presented as separate, parallel categories.
3
Understand the basic distinction
Qiyas is analogical reasoning, extending a ruling from an original case to a new case based on a common effective cause. Istihsan is juristic preference, allowing departure from a clear analogy in favor of a stronger evidence, custom, or necessity.
Hanafis accept both, while Shafi'is reject istihsan.
4
Connect to the broader chapter
The passage ends by announcing the beginning of the chapter on qiyas and istihsan, indicating that detailed discussions will follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النوع الرابع: فعلى وجهين في حق الحكم، وهما القياس والاستحسان. وهذا باب القياس والاستحسان
As for the fourth type: it is of two aspects regarding the ruling, and they are qiyas (analogy) and istihsan (juristic preference). And this is the chapter of qiyas and istihsan.
Source pages p. 611

Commentary
This passage marks the introduction of the fourth broad category of differences in usul al-fiqh: the methods of deriving rulings through qiyas (analogical reasoning) and istihsan (juristic preference). The author, Imam al-Bazdawi, states that this type concerns two aspects with respect to the ruling itself. He then announces the beginning of a new chapter specifically dedicated to qiyas and istihsan. This sets the stage for the detailed discussion that follows in the subsequent pages. In Hanafi usul, qiyas is a primary source of law when a direct text is not available, and istihsan is a preferred method over qiyas when necessary.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning: extending a ruling from an original case (asl) to a new case based on a shared effective cause (illah).
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference: departing from an apparent analogy in favor of a stronger evidence, custom, or necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Qiyas and istihsan
C) Istihsan and istishab
D) 'Illah and hukm

Key Takeaway
The fourth type of differences in rulings comprises two distinct methods: qiyas (analogy) and istihsan (juristic preference).

Types of Qiyas and Istihsan in Hanafi Usul
Defining two types each of qiyas and istihsan and their divisions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between qiyas and istihsan based on the strength of the effect (athar) and identify the three categories of istihsan according to al-Bazdawi.

Core Idea
Istihsan is the preferred analogy: stronger qiyas, not a separate source.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul provides a foundational classification of qiyas and istihsan. Understanding this distinction is crucial for Hanafi legal theory, as it explains why some analogies are favored over others. The passage also introduces three categories of istihsan that arise from different sources of authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basis for qiyas
The passage states that qiyas is valid only when a textual basis (asl) exists. Note the examples of hand-to-hand exchange and the conditions for slaughter and i'tikaf.
2
Distinguish the two types of qiyas
One type has a weak effect; the second has apparent corruption but hidden soundness and effect.
Weak effect (أثر ضعيف) vs. hidden soundness (استترت صحته)
3
Distinguish the two types of istihsan
One type has a strong effect even if hidden; the second has an apparent effect but hidden corruption.
Strong effect (قوي الأثر) vs. apparent effect with hidden corruption
4
Recognize istihsan as a type of qiyas
The author says istihsan is 'one of the two qiyases' and is called 'preferred qiyas' (قياس مستحسن). It is not an independent source.
5
List the three categories of istihsan
Based on: transmitted tradition (athar) – salam, ijarah; consensus (ijma) – istisna'; necessity (darura) – purification of water containers.

يصح تعليله : وإنما أنكرنا هذه الجملة إذا لم يوجد في الشريعة أصل فأما إذا وجد : فلا بأس به، ألا يُرى أنهم اختلفوا في التقابض في بيع الطعام بالطعام وتكلموا فيه بالرأي ؛ لأنا وجدنا لإثباته أصلاً، وهو الصرف، ووجدنا لجوازه بدونه أصلاً، وهو بيع سائر السلع، فإذا وجد مثله في غيره : صحت له التعدية. و ألا يُرى أن مَن ادعى إيجاب التسمية في الذبيحة شرطاً بالقياس : لم يجد له أصلاً، ومن أراد إيجاب الصوم في الاعتكاف شرطاً بالقياس : لم يجد له أصلاً أيضاً. وهذا باب لا يُحصى عدد ،فروعه فاقتصرنا فيه على الإشارة إلى الجُمَل. وأما النوع الرابع: فعلى وجهين في حق الحكم، وهما القياس والاستحسان. وهذا باب القياس والاستحسان
باب القياس والاستحسان وكل واحد منهما على وجهين : أما أحد نوعي القياس : فما ضعف أثره. والنوع الثاني : ما ظهر فساده واستترت صحته وأثره. وأحد نوعي الاستحسان: ما قوي أثره وإن كان خفياً. والثاني : ما ظَهَرَ أثره، وخفي فساده. وإنما الاستحسان عندنا أحد القياسين، لكنه استحساناً؛ إشارةً سمي إلى أنه الوجه الأولى في العمل به وأن العمل بالآخر جائز، كما جاز العمل بالطرد وإن كان الأثر أولى منه. والاستحسان أقسام : وهو ما ثبت بالأثر، مثل السلم، والإجارة، وبقاء الصوم مع فعل الناسي. ومنه ما ثبت بالإجماع، وهو الاستصناع. ومنه ما ثبت بالضرورة ، وهو تطهير الحياض والآبار والأواني. وإنما غرضنا ها هنا تقسيم وجوه العلل في حق الأحكام، ولما صارت العلة عندنا علةً بأثرها : سمينا الذي ضعف أثرها: قياساً، وسمينا الذي قَوِيَ أَثرها استحساناً، أي قياساً مُستحسناً، وقدمنا الثاني وإن كان خفياً
Its reasoning is valid. And we only reject this entirety when there is no basis in the Shariah. But if there is a basis, then there is no problem. Do you not see that they disagreed about hand-to-hand exchange in the sale of food for food and spoke about it by opinion? Because we found a basis for its establishment, which is currency exchange, and we found a basis for its permissibility without it, which is the sale of other commodities. So if a similar case is found in another, then transference is valid. And do you not see that whoever claimed the obligation of saying the name over the slaughtered animal as a condition by analogy did not find a basis for it. And whoever wanted to make fasting obligatory in i'tikaf as a condition by analogy also did not find a basis for it. This is a topic whose branches are countless, so we limited ourselves to indicating the general principles. As for the fourth type, it is of two aspects regarding the ruling: qiyas and istihsan. This is the chapter on qiyas and istihsan. Chapter on Qiyas and Istihsan. Each one is of two types: one type of qiyas is that whose effect (athar) is weak. The second type is that whose corruption is apparent but its soundness and effect are hidden. One type of istihsan is that whose effect is strong even if it is hidden. The second is that whose effect is apparent but its corruption is hidden. According to us, istihsan is one of the two qiyases, but it is called istihsan as an indication that it is the preferred aspect to act upon and that acting upon the other is permissible, just as acting upon al-tard is permissible even though the effect is more appropriate. Istihsan has divisions: what is established by tradition (athar), such as salam, ijarah, and the continuity of fasting despite the forgetful person's action. Among it is what is established by consensus (ijma), which is istisna'. Among it is what is established by necessity (darura), which is purifying tanks, wells, and vessels. Our purpose here is only to classify the aspects of causes (ilal) with respect to rulings. Since according to us a cause is a cause by its effect, we called that whose effect is weak: qiyas, and we called that whose effect is strong: istihsan, i.e., a preferred qiyas, and we presented the second even if it is hidden.
Source pages p. 611

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul defines the two types of qiyas and two types of istihsan, and explains the Hanafi position that istihsan is a kind of qiyas. It begins by discussing the validity of analogical reasoning (qiyas) when a textual basis (asl) exists in the Shariah. Using examples like the hand-to-hand exchange in food sales (where the basis is currency exchange) and the conditions for slaughter and i'tikaf (where no basis exists), the author shows that qiyas is valid only when a proper asl is found. The passage then introduces the 'fourth type' of reasoning about rulings, which is qiyas and istihsan. Each is divided into two types: qiyas can have a weak effect or an apparently corrupt but hidden sound effect; istihsan can have a strong but hidden effect or an apparent effect with hidden corruption. Critically, the author states that istihsan is actually one of the two qiyases—it is a 'preferred qiyas' (قياس مستحسن). This is the distinct Hanafi view: istihsan is not a separate source but a stronger analogical reasoning. The passage then lists three categories of istihsan based on their source: 1) established by transmitted tradition (athar), examples include salam (advance sale) and ijarah (lease); 2) established by consensus (ijma), example is istisna' (manufacturing contract); 3) established by necessity (darura), example is the purification of water containers. Finally, the author reiterates that they classify causes (ilal) by their effect: weak effect = qiyas, strong effect = istihsan. The Hanafi school prioritizes istihsan even when its reasoning is hidden.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning in usul al-fiqh; here defined as a type of reasoning where the effect (athar) is weak or the soundness is hidden.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference; according to this text, it is a type of qiyas with a stronger effect, considered the preferred analogy.
الأثر (al-athar): The effect or impact of a cause (illa) on the ruling; used to differentiate between qiyas (weak effect) and istihsan (strong effect).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Bazdawi, what determines whether a reasoning is classified as qiyas or istihsan?
A) The presence or absence of a textual basis (asl)
B) The strength or weakness of the effect (athar) of the cause
C) Whether the reasoning is based on consensus or necessity
D) The apparent corruption or soundness of the ruling

Key Takeaway
Istihsan is the Hanafi term for a stronger qiyas, classified by the strength of its effect, and it includes divisions based on tradition, consensus, and necessity.
